Approval
This thesis has been prepared over the period of 01/02/2023 to 03/10/2023 at the Section for Structural
Engineering, Department of Civil Engineering, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial
fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc Eng. It was assembled independently
as an academic work, The author and the industry advisor for this report are both employed by the client
team of the NHN super hospital by Region H. This interest has not affected the process or assembly of this
report. Every attempt has been made to limit the influence of this related interest.
It is assumed that the reader has basic knowledge in the areas of statistics and building science. The Harvard
referencing style is used throughout.

Preface
”An engineer uses the limited resources of the world, efficiently, for the betterment of mankind.”
Tom O’Neil
This report was written in light of the social changes needed to meet the European sustainable development
goals (SDG’s). The risks related to the Anthropocene (a new epoch created by our lifestyles and waste),
require a structured and categorical redirection of the development of society.
”When we build, let us think that we build forever. Let it not be for present delight nor for present
use alone. Let it be such work as our descendants will thank us for; and let us think, as we lay stone
on stone, that a time is to come when those stones will be held sacred because our hands have touched
them, and that men will say, as they look upon the labour and wrought substance of them, “See! This
our father did for us.” John Ruskin
This thesis involved research and experimentation with the concept of flexible design in buildings and,
as all good research reports should, raised more questions than it answered. It follows certain schools
of thought and logical processes which by no means encapsulate the full sphere of understanding in this
field of study, but represent certain possibilities, and is a stepping stone in the continued progression of our
understanding of flexible design. it builds from past knowledge and forges new paths for future researchers
to explore.
”One of the key principles of sustainability is that development today should not compromise the
ability of future generations to meet their own needs” World Commission on Environment and De-
velopment, 1987; Stern, 2006
The material herein is assembled with the hopes that it will contribute to a more sustainable future in that
the continued demolition of existing structures, the design of planned obsolescence in new constructions
combined with the blatant waste of resources within the AECM (Architecture, Engineering, Construction,
Management) industry is not a feasible course for a continued future. Planning for change and defining,
categorically, a metric to measure our success therein is the way to chart this new course, for our benefit
and the benefit of generations to come.
”Let the grandchildren gather the apples” Robinson Jeffers
Information and resources contained in this thesis are supplemented by
a website (http://tabernacledesign.com/adaptable-flexibility-design-transformation/),
a podcast (https://open.spotify.com/show/7AQSmYWAo7MTs9sPTBLfwv)
and a youtube channel (https://www.youtube.com/@tabernacledesing/playlists) that contains additional
ideas, visualisations and information.
Kongens Lyngby, October 3, 2023
Patrick Finn Colclough
Flexible Design Analysis
iv
==PAGE:6==
Abstract
The problem: The research question addressed in this thesis is ”Can flexibility in the context of the design
of the lifespan of a building be rated?”. With flexibility being defined as the ability for a building to adapt
to new uses during its lifetime and to be easily transformed to facilitate a second life. This question is a
critical aspect of sustainable design.
The key performance indicators (KPI) to assess if this question has been answered are:
• Develop a unified approach to evaluate flexibility (a Flexibility Framework).
• The categorisation of a building into design characteristics.
• Analysing how a mix of these characteristics within a design solution affects flexibility.
• Generating a single numerical evaluation for the flexibility of a building.
The method: The action taken in this thesis to achieve these KPIs is, first, the development of a methodol-
ogy for evaluating flexibility (a Flexibility Framework). Then to develop a tool to evaluate the flexibility of
building designs based on key design characteristics and their relative importance. The research methodol-
ogy involves literature reviews, interviews and surveys. The tool is developed using an analytical hierarchy
process, modern portfolio theory and Monte Carlo calculations. The tool is tested and refined with a case
study on the NHN super hospital, comparing the interchangeability of the bed-wards and offices of the
buildings based on the metric of indoor climate.
The results: A Flexibility Framework was developed from past research and life cycle assessment method-
ologies. Nine design characteristics were generated as key assets for a building’s flexibility: Cost, Own-
ership, Lifecycle plan, Maintenance, Society, Technical, Structural, Aesthetic, Space, and Digitisation. A
tool was created using Excel and Python that calculates an augmented mean as a single flexibility score,
based on the weighting of design characteristics in different possible future scenarios and a global mini-
mum variance portfolio (taken to be the optimal design solution, with the most reward to the least risk, of
the given designing characteristics). This flexibility score reflects the adaptability of the finished building
given the set of possible future scenarios compared to an ideal design. Several impact analysis takeoffs
were generated, predominant of which is the ”returns” of the design characteristics (The mean of the ben-
efit of each design factor across the different scenarios) representing the utility of the energy put into each
design characteristic.
The case study: The case study yielded a flexibility score of 0.68 (68%) for the NHN super hospital, with a
standard deviation of 0.0003. The most dominant characteristic of the design, regarding flexibility, was the
lifecycle plan requiring 53% of the design focus, or energy, for success in a negative future scenario, 45%
for a scenario incorporating no future change and 19% for a positive future scenario. This was developed
from a preliminary flexibility score of 22.24 aggregated with a global minimum variance score of 15.21.
The limitations: This report is intended as an academic work relevant for the geographic region of Den-
mark within the temporal scope of weighting being 2023. The intended audience is the reviewing body
for the Technical University of Denmark and future academic researchers. The implementation of the tool
is limited in its current state and a broader data set must be compiled for effective industry use. This the-
sis provides a stepping stone for further development and refinement. Developed as a prototype, not all
aspects discussed are fully implemented.
The Conclusion: Research indicates the development of a numerical assessment of the flexibility of build-
ings is possible and a common framework can be developed to unify the field and increase accountability.
An evaluation should consider scenario probability, the design characteristics, acknowledge the subjec-
tivity of such evaluation through incorporating designer preference, and be, itself, adaptable to changing
needs. A flexibility index score is a useful tool for the early design process to evaluate where a design
team should focus their efforts.

Flexible Design Analysis
vi
==PAGE:8==
Executive summary/Research proposal
Introduction
As an academic report for DTU Civil Department, structural section, this report outlines a tool to analyse
how well buildings incorporate future use into their design, using the case study of the interchangeability
of a bed ward and staff office in the NHN super hospital in regards to indoor climate for the testing and
development of the tool. The report deals with the finished hospital as modelled in 2023 and its possible
alternative life cycles thereafter for the 100-year-plus lifetime of the building. The aim is to develop a
tool that can numerically asses and compare the flexibility of building design, serving as a guide for future
developments.
Conceptual framework
The development of a flexibility index, framework and assessment tool based on analytical hierarchy pro-
cesses, principle performance indicators and modern portfolio theory to evaluate hospital design using the
NHN super hospital as a case study.
Functional unit
This report analyses the change of use of space in regard to the requirements of its inhabitants under specific
the design characteristics. It develops a flexibility index to numerically rate the implementation of these
design characteristics in a number of scenarios. The report uses the metrics of the probability and variance
of a scenario arising and the effect of the change on the system. These incorporate the two main paradigms
of the probability of scenarios and the process involved in adaptation. The assessment looks at the effect of
design characteristics on the final design solution for different future scenarios, measured by the risk and
reward of alternative design solutions contributing to a flexibility score. This score could theoretical then
be used as a factor to develop cost savings calculations for environmental impact and investment returns
though this is not implemented in this report. The scenarios are:
• Current use case/environmental conditions
• Negative/unwanted future use case
• Unchanged future use case
• Positive/beneficial future use case
The tipping point between flexibility (reward) and cost (risk) for the scenarios is then calculated and the
effect of the change in the parameters is noted to suggest what the benefits of the different designs are and
to what degree their changes affect the flexibility of the structure under different probable future situations.
Methodological framework
The report is formed from a literary review of the current practices and knowledge of adaptable and flexible
design. Design categories are evaluated and weighted, the scenarios and systems are developed, elabo-
rated and quantified, then compared through BIM and mathematical modelling using analytical hierarchy.
Principle or ”key” performance indicators (KPIs) are then analysed for the given case (such as ventilation
requirements). The datas reliability is catalogued through a perturbation analysis. An efficiency frontier is
modelled to asses what mix of design characteristics would give the best return for the assumed risks of a
design solution. The Monte Carlo method is used to check the flexibility score produced for consistency.
Experimental process
The following data gathering and modelling processes were used.
1. Survey of designers, end users, and other adaptive structures actors, including questions such as ”Do
current solutions get used?”, ”Will new scenarios get used/ are likely?”, ”How much time is needed to
adapt?”, ”Are there abuses of the design?”.
2. Elaboration on design characteristics (Cost, Ownership, Lifecycle plan, Maintenance, Society, Techni-
cal, Structural, Aesthetic, Space, and Digitisation).
Flexible Design Analysis
vii
==PAGE:9==
3. Modelling of the scenarios in BIM to compile and analyse construction elements and develop relevant
ifc take offs.
4. Run probability and sensitivity models such as modified analytical hierarchy excel, Monte Carlo simu-
lations and an efficiency frontier.
5. Modelling of possible future scenarios.
Findings and recommendations
The findings and reconditions from the report are presented in the form of a comparison of modelled results
and effectiveness of the developed tool. The feasibility of a quantitative assessment for building flexibility
has been established through literature, interviews, and surveys. This evaluation considers multiple future
scenarios and specific design attributes’ impacts on flexibility, acknowledging varying importance. A tool
for flexibility analysis should cater to designer preferences and adapt to the dynamic Architecture, Engi-
neering, and Construction Management (AECM) field. A flexibility index score guides early design, aiding
focus on lifespan extension. Yet, the tool’s limitations include data scarcity and subjectivity, demanding
careful result interpretation. Recommendations encompass broader respondent sampling, IFC library ex-
pansion, cost integration, and probabilistic refinement. The tool’s context-specificity and passive control
proxy are vital considerations. Its potential to incentivize adaptable design through government initiatives
is noteworthy. Diverse respondent involvement, IFC library expansion, layered adaptability analysis, and
broader use of cost assessment are pivotal for tool enhancement. A parametric interface for diverse design
variations and wider engagement could amplify its value. This tool holds potential to guide cost-effective
adaptable design, necessitating further exploration and refinement.
Goal and scope
Goal
The goal is to develop a tool to allow the analysis of the effectiveness of the design of structures for future
use. It is assumed that the audience of this report is an academic board, the Client team of the NHN super
hospital and the general public. The results of this thesis is intended to develop the concept of flexible
design and improve and add to the plethora of concepts regarding best practices in hospital design.
Scope
The functional unit of the report is flexibility utilisation, represented by the augmented Reward (mean ben-
efit of a combination of design parameters) and risk of a design solution when implemented in different
scenarios. This is realised through a flexibility index based on mathematical models of design metrics. The
reliability of the models and performance of the different systems will be analysed under the alternative
scenarios. The system boundary will be that of the scenarios and metrics listed in this thesis. Additional
circumstances and elements affecting the measured parameters that are not explicitly stated are not con-
sidered to be within the scope of this report. This report only considers changes in design or effects on the
likelihood of the scenarios for the case study up to and including data available in 2023. Though state-of-
the-art technologies are assumed and likely future innovations have been considered not all possibilities
can be accommodated and all possible scenarios are set within the context of the Danish healthcare system.
The processes included are based on considerations of current literature and discussion with stakeholders
and experts.

1
Introduction
An answer only makes sense if you know the question it answers. Similarly, a flexible building is only
useful if the users know how to, and wants to, engage with a building. Not everyone is interested in
these structures, and not everyone cares about buildings or their use, it is peripheral to a vast portion of
society, boring and unintelligible to some. The reason for engagement with a structure must be made
clear; comfort, cost, time and health of the inhabitant and of society. This is why a user should engage
with the structures they inhabit and why flexibility should be part of their system of symbiosis. Planed,
staged, linear, contemporaneous and synchronized. If we do not plan and make a habit of engaging with
our buildings, we will stop seeing them. Thus, we will lose sight of the forest in the trees and accustom
ourselves to sub-optimal conditions without being aware of it, like a lobster slowly cooking in a pot.
1.1
Flexibility - what is it?
What is flexibility in the context of the design of the lifespan of a building? This is the first question to
be asked when engaging with this thesis. This study defines flexibility as a building’s ability to support
change, and adaptability as a building’s ability to be changed. For this study, adaptability is the abil-
ity to make modifications or adjustments to suit a new environment that can be forecast, and flexibility
is the ability to adjust to accommodate unknown needs in the future. Though the term ”Flexibility” is
often confusing when talking of the design of a building as it is already a well-established term in struc-
tural disciplines and brings to mind the physical properties of a structure to most people, it is becoming
the most well-established description for the phenomenon of a building’s intrinsic characteristics to sur-
vive through time. This study will follow the following definitions as defined by Kristoffer Negendahl
(Kristoffer Negendahl 2023) and Schmidt et al. (Robert Schmidt 2016) in the hopes of strengthening an
industry-recognised definition of these terms. The flexibility of a building’s design is the ability to incor-
porate future needs. Encapsulating a design response which allows a building to cater to different uses,
rather than being torn down when its initial purpose is no longer profitable and the demand for its innate
ability to support its inhabitants in their goals decreases.
Other terms for this phenomenon include transformation capacity, elasticity and, predominantly, adaptabil-
ity. The distinction between the terms adaptability and flexibility is an important first step in understanding
how to assess buildings with regard to them. Flexibility encapsulates a wide scope of opportunities that
the building may or may not be used for, targeting unspecific scenarios. Flexibility should maximise value
over time. Flexibility is an inbound, built in opportunity, whereas adaptability is targeting specific scenar-
ios. Adaptability is specifically adaptable to something as such it is easier to talk about adaptability than
flexibility. A building is flexible if it can adapt in both the short and long term, though flexibility is not
time dependent. A more complex definition of flexibility can be defined as the use cases in the long term
(use specific), which includes adaptability, and the use cases in the short term (specified), with respect to
the design of buildings for future use. A use case refers to a specific scenario or functional requirement
within the lifecycle of a structure. It encapsulates a particular task or objective that the structure is designed
to fulfil.
Often flexibility and adaptability are used interchangeably within the AEC (Architecture, Engineering and
Constructions) industry and one goal of this study is to better define what is meant by flexibility and how
it can be rated regarding building design.
Table 1.1, adapted from the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) course on flexibility (Kristoffer Ne-
gendahl 2023) comprises the various definitions of adaptability and flexibility.
Flexible Design Analysis
1
==PAGE:15==
Table 1.1: Definitions (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023)
Source
Adaptability
Flexibility
Blyth and Worhtungton (2000)

Schneider and Till (2005)

Technological changes (physical aspects)
For simplicity, the term flexibility is used as a general term to encapsulate the transformation potential of
a building throughout this report.
1.2
The Why of Flexibility: A background
Flexible design in buildings can provide numerous benefits, including adaptability to future changes, cost
savings, and reduced environmental impact. Understanding and analyzing the degree of flexibility in a
building’s design can aid in decision-making processes related to building design and renovation.
Buildings are being demolished at an ever-increasing rate, 40 years ago, the average building was 400
years old, and today it is 40 as can be seen in figure 1.1. This trend, which can be seen to have many
sources, not least planned obsolescence and capitalism’s need for continued growth, is unsustainable. If
we as a society do not stop building new buildings within the next three years, we will not meet the 2050
SDGs (Sustainable development goals) outlined in section1.5 (UN Sustainable Development Goals n.d.)
and by (Kristoffer Negendahl 2022).
Figure 1.1: Increase in demolition rate (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023)
Though the total halt of construction is unlikely, we can move towards more sustainable construction, with
the reuse of materials in a circular economy and the reuse of buildings, and building elements, to reduce
waste to a minimum wherever possible as outlined in figure 1.2.
Flexible Design Analysis
2
==PAGE:16==
Figure 1.2: Environmental value of reuse (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023)
As the construction industry accounts for a third of global environmental emissions, consuming 35% of
global materials, generating 37% of global waste and producing 40% of global Co2 emissions ((Kristoffer
Negendahl 2023)) every step to economic and sustainable practices can have marked results. As outlined
in figure 1.3, which shows not only the environmental costs of a typical construction project but the savings
possible with even the simplest of principles, like material choice.
Figure 1.3: Co2 cost of a ten-storey building (Larsen 2022)
The flexibility of a building’s design aligns with broader EU objectives related to sustainable development,
green building practices, and improving the overall quality of the built environment. Flexibility in buildings
can contribute to sustainability and energy-efficient design. Energy efficiency is easily measured and
can therefore be rated and improved. In recent years much work has been done to categorize, monitor,
measure and rate the environmental impact of our activities as a species, including construction, however,
the design requirement of ”Flexible structures” is something that is still not widely researched or covered
by an overarching, generally accepted, theory and strategy. It is enough to highlight the possibilities for
Flexible Design Analysis
3
==PAGE:17==
change in a design and think superficially about possible future scenarios to claim a ”flexible design”. This
can be seen in the often interchangeable use of the terms, flexible, adaptable, elastic, versatile and future-
proof when discussing the design of structures. This unclear understanding of flexibility undermines the
usefulness of the concept and diminishes its significance. A similar taxonomy for flexibility is needed as
to that which is currently used for energy and sustainability within the construction industry if it is to be
held in the same regard and given the consideration it requires.
1.3
What will be developed
This thesis follows the previous concepts developed at DTU of numerically evaluating the flexibility of
the design of a building, allowing a single number to be assigned after analysis. Thus, making it possi-
ble to rate and compare the future-proofing of newly constructed and historic buildings, in the hopes of
increasing interest in these elements of the design of structures and helping to establish a framework for
more sustainable, long-lasting structures. To analyse flexibility a similar method to that taught at DTU,
is engaged with and built upon in this thesis. Furthermore, this concept will have parallels to the German
building councils (DGNB) system of analysis (described in chapter 4) as well as the incorporation and
adaptation of LCA (Life Cycle Analysis) frameworks (figure 1.4) which gives a basic guide of the steps
needed to complete a life cycle anaysys report.
Figure 1.4: LCA framework (Anders Damgaard 2023)
When building from these established academic and industry theories, it is intended to help the transition
into a holistic and broadly accepted ideology of flexibility in building design and allow for the existing
logic string from sustainability theory to move laterally to the related field of flexibility.
This thesis outlines the development of a design analysis tool (DAT) that can numerically assess the flexi-
bility of the design of a building. The outline of the initial tool concept is shown in figure 1.5. The finished
tool is accessible in the technical appendix, (Appendix B) supplied in a zip folder with this thesis and dis-
cussed in full in chapter 6, as well as visually represented in appendix ??. DAT is developed in line with
the existing tools from DTU, and is tested on a case study of its use in the analysis of the flexibility of
bed-wards in the New Hospital North Zealand (NHN) super hospital in Hillerød, Denmark as it is in 2023.
Flexible Design Analysis
4
==PAGE:18==
Figure 1.5: Flexability Analysis Tool
1.4
How can Flexibility be achieved
To analyse flexibility, first, a framework for this analysis is developed (figure 1.6) in the hopes of creating
a homogeneous process for future analyses, similar to that of the current LCA framework. In this thesis,
the fields of assessment, decision analyses, probability, comparison, mathematical modelling, and building
information modelling (BIM) are explored and expanded to create one take on the possible form of this
framework. From this framework, a tool can then be developed to assess and rate the flexibility of a
building. The analysis tool (DAT) developed in this thesis has been used to assess the flexibility of a test
case, the bed-wards in the NHN super hospital, as outlined in the chapter 7, to highlight how the flexibility
of a building’s design can be assessed.
1.4.1
Aim
The aim of this thesis is to develop a tool that can numerically analyze the degree of flexible design in
buildings. The tool is developed based on the NHN super hospital in Hillerød, Denmark, as a case study.
The tool is based on a framework that incorporates principles of flexible design and aligns with the SDGs
(see section 1.5). A single score for the flexibility of a building is generated from the tool which can then
be used to compare designs and help the designers in the early phase of the design cycle to assess the
potential of their concepts and enhance their contemplation of flexibility. The importance of early design
phase optimisation can be accepted as common logic but is also highlighted in several articles, such as
”Building energy optimisation in the early design stages: a simplified method” by Kristoffer Negendahl
and Toke Rammer Nielsen: ”Many researchers reason that building performance simulation (BPS) tools
in the early design stages is beneficial for building performance such as energy, daylight.” The concern
of using optimisation processes in early design is very real, regardless of how the model is constructed.
However, the benefit of optimisation may in many cases exceed the downsides of artistic control if the
optimisation processes is controlled and supervised by the designers themselves.” (Kristoffer Negendahl
and Nielsen 2015)
Flexible Design Analysis
5
==PAGE:19==
1.4.2
Framework
The following framework for analysing flexibility is based on principles of flexible design and is designed
and incorporated as part of this thesis. It is expanded upon in the appendix A and chapter 9. This framework
also aligns with the SDGs, which provide a framework for sustainable development.
Figure 1.6: Flexibility Framework
This framework is based on the steps of defining the goal of the flexibility analysis, assigning weighting
to the different design factors considered, developing future scenarios, assessing the probability of the
scenarios and effects of the design factors, running the analysis, checking the reliability and sensitivity of
the data, generating results and comparing the results. It is indicated that most of these steps are part of a
cyclical loop and should be seen as an iterative process.
1.5
Sustainable Development Goals
Figure 1.7: un sustainable development goals
The UN sustainable development goals (SDGs) (UN Sustainable Development Goals n.d.) provide a set
of goals and targets to achieve sustainable development and include 17 goals as seen in figure 1.7 and 169
Flexible Design Analysis
6
==PAGE:20==
targets. The SDGs are intended to guide action towards a sustainable future for all. The goals cover a
range of areas, and this thesis deals with the SDGS of:
• 4. Quality education. By providing a publicly accessible source for reference and further research.
• 8. Decent work and economic growth. By promoting the ideology of a circular economy and new
labour markets in the field of flexible analysis.
• 9. Industry information and infrastructure. By studying and developing innovative solutions to the
problem of assessing flexibility in buildings and promoting efficient long-lasting infrastructure.
• 11. Sustainable cities and communities. by developing sustainable construction principles and de-
veloping social interest in the full life cycle of the buildings we inhabit.
• 12. Responsible consumption and production. By developing an ideology to reduce waste within
the construction industry.
• 13. Climate action. By promoting the reduction in the use of resources and environmental hazards
of demolition through developing a best practice guide.
1.6
Historical Overview
The history of flexible design can be traced back to the 1960s and 1970s when architects and designers
began to experiment with open-plan office spaces. The aim was to create more flexible spaces that could
accommodate a range of activities and uses. In the 1980s, the concept of flexibility in building design was
expanded to include the ability to adapt to changing environmental conditions and technological advance-
ments.
The concept can be said to have started as the idea by John Habraken (1960)(openbuilding.co 2020) of the
user of the building having control over their environment, this idea was then adapted by Frank Duffy’s
concepts on open offices (1992). Stephen Groak’s concept of dynamic buildings (1992) added to the
field, and then Stewart Brand, in his work on layers of change (1994), outlined new perspectives on what
we conceive as buildings and how their components interact, such as viewing a building as categories
of functions and defining each category and there respective lifespans as separate entities. Slaughter’s
work in 2001 can furthermore be seen as a step towards the current understanding of flexible buildings
and best practices in the field were since outlined and developed by Robbert Schmitt (2016), Adaptable
Architecture - Theory and Practice (Robert Schmidt 2016) where a complete history of flexible design
concepts is outlined in this seminal work. This is outlined in Chapter 3.
1.6.1
History of the hospital
The NHN super hospital in Hillerod, Denmark, was chosen as a case study as it is a modern hospital that
was designed with flexibility in mind. It was built with the aim of providing a high level of adaptability to
meet the changing needs of the healthcare industry and the local community. See chapter7.
Hospital design is a very specialist field within the AECM industry and includes the absolute necessity
for ”state of the future” technology for the function of the hospital and the constant development of the
processes and treatments needed. The immense scale of the construction of the NHN megaproject as well as
the long lead time from design to commissioning (due to the nature of the healthcare industry’s constantly
changing requirements and ever-evolving technology and best practices.) mean that the healthcare industry
needs to be able to grow and change on an almost daily basis. For this reason, the analysis of the NHN
super hospital, with a 20-year design and construction process (Conceived in 2007 and, in 2023, having
a completion date of 2027) and projected life span of 100 years, was chosen as the case study for the
flexibility analysis tool. To ensure that the tool could be easily streamlined and developmental issues
spotted during the case study, and to allow the results to be complied within the required time, the scope
of the analyses was refined to a more realistic scale that could be represented and understood within the
Flexible Design Analysis
7
==PAGE:21==
bounds of this thesis. The adaption of offices to bed wards was decided as the refined metric as this was one
of the original flexible design criteria defined by the hospital design team(Arkitekten 2014) and highlights
one of the most pressing requirements of the hospital, the flow of the users and the capacity of patients,
which can change hourly as outlined in the works of (Oscar Westergaard 2022) and (Ballinger 2019a).

==PAGE:22==
2
Methodology
2.1
Preamble
The research question investigated in this thesis is whether it is possible to numerically analyse the degree
of flexible design in buildings. The purpose is to develop a framework and tool that can do just that.
The research approach for this thesis was the development of a flexibility framework and an analysis tool
(referred to as DAT) to allow the numerical appraisal of the flexibility of a building’s design, using the
NHN super hospital as a case study.
Firstly, a review of the current literature was carried out, from which a comprehensive survey (See Ap-
pendix: A.6.5) of key concepts was compiled and detailed interviews (published as a limited podcast series
(Colclough 2023)) were held. From the results of this research, the categories and weighting of the tool
were established and a method for compiling the analytical data gathered and the personal preferences of
the intended user was developed.
This method involved building an Excel user interface, referred to as DATEx (design Analyses Tool Ex-
cel)(See Appendix: A.1) to allow for basic calculations and user inputs, which was linked to a Python
script, referred to as FDAT (Flexibility design analyse tool) (See Appendix: A.2) to allow the data to be
further processed and an efficiency frontier to be calculated for any given input. This tool was then used
to assess the indoor climate adaptability of the bed wards and the office spaces in the NHN super hospital
in Hillerod under three assumed possible future scenarios as outlined later in Chapter 7.
2.2
Research design
The data collection method was a literature review, outlined in Chapter 3. A survey was then compiled
from the key points of this literature and handed out in relevant areas, namely the NHN client team, the
Christiania intentional community (Dale, Supervisor, and Langlais 2011), and the Makvärket AEC society
(Makvärket 2013) (See appendix A.6.5). Interviews on the topic of flexibility in the design of buildings
were also held concurrently with various Designers, users, architects and engineers to get a fuller picture
of the general concepts and principles of flexible design in more open-ended detail. This data was then
analysed by the ”survey monkey” (survey monkey n.d.) back-end analysis and further processed. Recurring
themes were noted and integrated into the categorisation and weightings of the analysis tool as principal
performance indicators (PPI).
This mixed method of quantitative and qualitative information gathering was chosen as the field of flexible
design in buildings is fragmented and largely, at this stage, subjective. As such both categorised hierarchy
and general discussion were needed to fully investigate what is important to users and designers in regards
to flexibility within the given geographic and temporal restraints of this thesis.
2.3
Sampling strategy
Three groups were canvased and the uptake and interaction with the surveys and interviews were random
and voluntary within each group.
The client team was chosen for the survey and interview process, along with site architects and engineers as
their insight and direct experience were seen as valuable to the case study. It was also noted in the research
report for Adapt-star (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013) that the main responses gathered was
from architects and that the opinion and views of more diverse fields, including from the role of client and
user, was missing from the field.
Flexible Design Analysis
9
==PAGE:23==
Similarly, Christiania (see Dale, Supervisor, and Langlais 2011) was chosen as an area of interest for infor-
mation gathering as the inhabitants there represent end users living in adapted structures, their experience
and thoughts on how and why their flexible community has survived and thrived for 50 years gives valu-
able insight to the second+ life of structures and how inhabitants interact with different elements and adapt
them to suit a new life.
Makvärket (see Makvärket 2013) was the third canvas area as the society there was set up as a builders’
community to promote, research and teach sustainable building techniques and philosophies. They repre-
sent both an end-user, developer, designer, and craftsmen of an intentionally adapted structure.
2.4
Data analysis
The data analysis techniques used to analyse the survey data were provided by Survey Monkey. The
survey data was compiled and categorised by Survey Monkeys’ back-end program. This data was then
subjectively analysed to highlight patterns and correlations. These findings are outlined in the Appendix
A.6.5. This data was extrapolated to develop and weight PPI’s for the analysis tool (See Appendix: A.1).
Once the data had been compiled in the Tool Excel sheet it was then extracted by a Python script and
mathematically analysed using an efficiency frontier (See Appendix:A.2).
2.5
Development of assessment tool
After the categories, weightings and relationships of the tool were developed from the research and aug-
mented by information from the surveys and interviews, a concept process flow diagram was then devel-
oped along with an outline of a flexible design assessment method, see figure 1.5 and 1.6. This framework
was the basis of the Excel file, DATEx, where a user interface and the back-end mathematics were de-
veloped based on the DTU course on Flexablity (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023) and an augment analytical
hierarchy process.
Possible future scenarios were then modelled in a scenario matrix and a Python script was developed using
a variation of modern portfolio theory to assess an augmented efficiency frontier of possible flexible design
solutions. The results of this script were compared with the current design solution from DATEx, and used
to grade the initial flexibility score in regard to the optimal, global minimum variance, design. This led to
a single flexibility index score as the final output of the tool and the gauge by which the user can carry out
further analyses and decisions.
2.6
Case study
The NHN super hospital case study was chosen as it represents a structure (currently in the construction
phase) which has been designed to be highly flexible. The healthcare industry represents a fast-changing
industry with ”state of the future” technology, meaning that some services and uses which do not exist yet
need to be planned into the structure. (Arkitekten 2014). There was total access to the data and process
of the hospital’s design, construction and management for this report. This level of access was another
reason this case study was chosen.
The specific study of the interchangeability of the bed-wards and office spaces was then chosen as a nar-
rower area within the super-system of the hospital as this was a key point in the design concepts in regards
to flexibility and allows for both the analysis of the first life and possible second life of the structure.
The focus point of indoor climate was chosen for this case study as the indoor climate is an essential metric
for the success of a structure as the end user experience is ultimately what a building is designed for. The
ability to maintain a comfortable atmosphere within a building is paramount to this experience. This is
explained in detail in chapter 7. Throughout the design phase of the hospital, careful attention was paid
to the dimensions of both the bed ward and office spaces, allowing for the interchangeability of their use.
Reduced inpatient time was also conceived as a factor of improved user experience in the design of the
Flexible Design Analysis
10
==PAGE:24==
NH project. Moreover, the potential future transformation of the building into a hotel was factored into its
design considerations regarding these spaces (Arkitekten 2014).
It is interesting to see how the indoor climate of these areas would fare with changed usage and environ-
mental conditions as interchangeable physical spaces are often assumed to be linked to interchangeable
indoor environments. This aspect is essential not only for the structure’s primary function but also for its
ability to adapt to changing uses over time, making it a critical factor in the overall success of the building
project.
Figure 2.1: Dalux site plan (Dalux 2023)
Data for the case study was gained from the NHN project database, which is compiled in the Dalux project
management application (Dalux 2023). As such certain documents can not to be disclosed, but they are
referenced and explained where relevant for context only.
The MEP services, technical installations, relevant literature, as well as relevant measurements for assess-
ment, such as room volume and window size, were gathered from this platform Dalux. Figure 2.1 outlines
the structure of this software. 3D point cloud imagery (Imerso 2023) was also analysed to assess the inter-
nal structures of the walls and relevant technical installations, and the ’as built’ condition of the building
(see figure 2.2). From this data, and the surveys and interviews from the client team, data points for each
PPI were established and the analysis tool was then used to provide an outline of the flexibility index of
the hospital bed wards as outlined in chapter 8.
(a) Imerso example 1
(b) Imerso example 2
Figure 2.2: Imeros analyses Examples
2.7
Disclaimers
It is important to clarify that the tool’s implementation is partial and is a work in progress. The development
of a full ifc library for analyses and the backed weighting of the tool require further implementation to be
Flexible Design Analysis
11
==PAGE:25==
useful within the AECM industry. The development of the utilisation of the score generated by the tool
also needs further attention. This highlights the possibility of errors when utilising the data generated and
the importance of using the tool correctly.
The tool’s purpose is to translate the design team narratives into quantifiable results as an internal tool
for debate and discussion in the early design phase. The tool guides users in providing documentation to
support their claims for building flexibility utilising a flexibility framework. The tool should only be used
to analyse areas where flexibility makes sense, with the relevance of probability estimates within a specific
discipline and building class assumed to be researched and expanded upon by the end user of the tool.
2.8
Summation
The analytical input of the interview process added to the philosophy developed through the literary review.
An assessment method was developed and a process flow diagram was then created. Using Mathematical
modelling and probability analysis a tool was built in Excel and Python to numerically assess flexible
design in buildings. The empirical data gathered through surveys were analysed by SurveyMonkey which
added weighting to the Principle performance indicators used in the tool.
The NHN super hospital was used as a case study, taking data from Dalux project management software,
Imerso 3D point scans and assembling a 3D model of the hospital to develop a context for the study, along
with the survey and interview results of the client team.

3
Literary Review
3.1
Introduction, background and objectives
This literary review was conducted to develop a comprehensive overview of the concept of flexible design
in buildings, covering the history, philosophy, theories, key design elements and practical application to
better understand the existing research in the field as it stands today and to asses any knowledge gaps
in current studies on the subject. It provides context for this paper and gives insights into the different
approaches to flexible design and its benefits and challenges. It was carried out over a 6-week period as
the initial step of this thesis. From this literature, it was possible to develop parameters (Such as categories
and weighting) for the Flexible design analysis tool developed in this thesis.
The following literature has been assessed for this review. The literature has been ordered chronologically
and organised into the groups of seminal works with the most influence on this thesis, secondary papers of
note, literature published by the publishing university, representing the vernacular theories of this report
and Literature specific to the case study of this report, the NHN super hospital. It does not comprise a full
list of works reviewed for this report, however, a comprehensive list of relevant material considered for
this paper can be found in the further reading section of the appendix (A.8).
Seminal books on Flexibility in the design of buildings:
• John Habraken (1960’s): Open buildings manifesto (openbuilding.co 2020)
• Groak (1992), The idea of building: thought and action in the design and production of buildings
(Book Review) (Barry Russell 1992)
• Frank Duffy (1992), The Changing Workplace (Book Review) - ProQuest (Gorb 1992)
• How Buildings Learn (Brand 1994)
• Adaptable buildings theory and practice (Robert Schmidt 2016)
Secondary papers on Flexibility in the design of buildings:
• Ballinger ”The Big 5” (Ballinger 2019a) (Ballinger 2019b)
• AdaptSTAR model: A climate-friendly strategy to promote built environment sustainability (Cone-
jos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013)
• Stakeholder perspectives on developing more adaptable buildings (Pinder, Schmidt, and Saker 2013)
Technical University of Denmark literature on flexibility:
• Transformation capacity as a design parameter (Langballe 2021)
• Flexhospital (Oscar Westergaard 2022)
• Energy flexibility of building systems in future scenarios: optimization of the control strategy of a
dynamic shading system and definition of a new energy flexibility metric (Carlucci, K. Negendahl,
and Fiorito 2023)
Literature on the NHN super hospital:
• saertryk arkitekten 2014 (Arkitekten 2014)
• 2016 NSH konferanse - Trends and pressures in Dutch hospital design (Hinkema 2023)
• The Danish Super Hospital Programme 2021 (Health 2021)
• 20220519 NHN projektintroduktion for Bispebjerg Vonsild (2022)
• konkurrenceporgram- this is it - VISION AND FUNDAMENTAL PRINCIPLES (T. C. R. o. Den-
mark 2013)

13
==PAGE:27==
3.2
Search strategy
Figure 3.1: Search strategy (T. U. o. Denmark 2022)
To identify relevant literature, a range of different databases was used, including Google search engine(Google
n.d.), DTU Find it (T. I. C. o. Denmark n.d.), Scopus (Scopus n.d.), Google Scholar (LLC n.d.), Re-
search Gate (GmbH n.d.), Library Genesis (RuNet n.d.), OpenAI Chat GP (O. AI n.d.), connected papers
(connected_papers_2023), semantic scholar (A. A. i. f. AI n.d.), Knovel (Corporation n.d.), web of sci-
ence (Clarivate 2022), Mendeley (Paul Foeckler n.d.) and Lecture material from DTU’s Flexible index
Course(Kristoffer Negendahl 2023).
The objectives and questions for the research that the literature review will address were defined as ”What
is flexibility” ”What is adaptability” ”What is elasticity” ”What is versatility” ”How can a buildings trans-
formation potential be weighted” ”How can a building be designed for change” ” what makes a building
last longer” ” what Characteristics increase the lifespan of a building” ”what makes building fit for multi-
ple uses” ” what stops buildings from being fit for multiple uses” ” how can you upgrade a building for a
new purpose”.
Search terms used included Adaptability in building, Flexible building design, Futurism and Elasticity in
buildings.
The methods used to search for relevant literature, as outlined in figure 3.1, were developed from the DTU
course 11222,(T. U. o. Denmark 2022) the presentation on literature searches, and the guidance and tools
outlined in (Keshav and R 2016) (Ellway n.d.) (Institute n.d.).
10 noteworthy authors and specific literature were identified from the DTU course on Flexibility, ”Målbar
Fleksibilitet i Byggeriet” (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023). This list was then shortened to ﬁve highly-cited
papers and a ”one pass” reading approach was made to this literature and their related works section.
Recent survey papers of the literature were also searched for to develop an overview of the literature, but
they were not found.
This collection of known authors was the prime literature used to inform this thesis however a secondary
search was also carried out.
The secondary search was a ”citation pearl search” as outlined in the DTU course 11222 (T. U. o. Denmark
2022), which involved finding shared citations and repeated author names in the bibliography of the prime
literature, these were noted as key literature and researchers and formed the basis for the secondary search.
Flexible Design Analysis
14
==PAGE:28==
This key literature was then compiled in a single folder. The key researchers were then searched using
connected papers (connected_papers_2023) and web of science (Clarivate 2022) and recent literature by
them was assessed, top conferences were also identified by where this recent literature had been published.
From the websites of these top conferences, their recent proceedings were analysed and recent high-quality
related literature was found. These were added to the original collection. The bibliography and further
reading lists from this literature were then investigated to find any additional literature of note missing
from this collection as outlined in the paper by Keshav, 2016 (Keshav and R 2016).
The literary assessment strategy adapted from Keshav, 2016 (ibid.) used to assess the primary and sec-
ondary literature was a three-pass approach with the aim to save relevant images, right down key points,
copy good quotes and write down what was missing from the literature.
The ﬁrst pass was a birds-eye analysis (given 10 minutes). It focused on the title, abstract, introduction
and mathematical content (theoretical foundations), conclusion and references (noting popular papers and
new sources from the references).
From this the 5 C’s (ibid.) of the literature were compiled:
• 1. Category: What type of paper is this? A measurement paper? An analysis of an existing system?
A description of a research prototype?
• 2. Context: Which other papers is it related to? Which theoretical bases were used to analyze the
problem?
• 3. Correctness: Do the assumptions appear to be valid?
• 4. Contributions: What are the paper’s main contributions?
• 5. Clarity: Is the paper well written?
In the second pass, key points were assessed (over one hour), noting if the conclusions were considered
signiﬁcant, terms that should be investigated and questions to ask the author (conceptually). Illustrations
were carefully examined and relevant unread references were marked for further reading. The goal of this
pass was to grasp the content of the paper and be able to summarize the main concept of the paper, with
supporting evidence.
The third pass should involve a thorough and full assessment of the paper to the point that it could be
reproduced by the reader (this step is outlined as only necessary if literature of special worth to the thesis
is found, and was not taken to be required for this thesis).
The inclusion criteria for literature to be assessed using the three-pass approach started with the base of
literature mentioned during the DTU flexibility course (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023) and was then built
from the relevance of the literature in the categories of Author, the validity of publisher/paper, content,
recommendations and concepts. A check was made for coherent section and sub-section titles, a con-
cise and comprehensive abstract, good graphical information and graphics presented in the abstract and
conclusions that were statistically signiﬁcant.
The temporal and geographical elements of the literature were also taken into account, with older literature
given precedent as the foundation of knowledge in the area of flexibility and newer works assessed as
more relevant in regard to their application to current needs. Literature from the Technical University
of Denmark was also specifically sought out and examined to apply a thread of lineage to this thesis and
ensure a coherent narrative for studies in this field from the philosophy of the institution it has been created
in.
The exclusion criteria, considered in tandem with the inclusion criteria, can be seen as the requirements
for a paper to be analysed in the first pass. It involved reading the title of the paper found from the search
technique and assessing its specificity and relevance. If it was too specific in relation to a sub-discipline,
Flexible Design Analysis
15
==PAGE:29==
or not relevant for this thesis it was excluded, if it passed the first criteria the abstract was read and checked
again for specificity and relevance.
Finally, the inclusion/exclusion criteria used to ensure the sources were relevant and up to date as mentioned
above, included a three-pass approach, centred around cross citations between articles and publications in
recognised papers/sources. Were a source did not fit this criterion but was relevant for another reason,
(case specific etc) it was included and analysed, such as the Technical University of Denmark papers and
NHN super hospital reports.
3.3
Classification of sources
The literature is classified into the categories of theoretical analysis, conceptual modelling, case study, and
philosophical development.
This categorisation allowed the literature to be assessed based on their relevance to the research objective
and question. Each category can be seen to add a different dynamic to the field of flexible design in
buildings.
Theoretical analysis built the basis for the categories defined in the matrix used by the ”DATEx” design
tool, as described in chapter 6(Also see Appendix A.1), developed in this report. Literature in this category
added to the understanding of what makes a building flexible.
Conceptual modelling literature gave examples of existing flexible analysis tools and different concepts of
layout, weighting and function for the DATEx tool.
Case study-specific literature was used to aid in the implementation of the DATEx tool and inform the
parameters used to evaluate the NHN super hospital.
Philosophical development literature was used to gain an understanding of the root of the field of flexible
design in buildings and allow for a holistic understanding to be developed, as well as to analyse supporting
and contradictory theories prevalent in the field.
The literature was also separated into: books on Flexibility, papers on Flexibility, DTU literature on flex-
ibility and case study-specific literature on the NHN super hospital.
Flexible Design Analysis
16
==PAGE:30==
3.4
Synthesis of findings
The key findings synthesised from this review are analysed using the 5 c’s (Keshav and R 2016) of category
(type of paper), context (other related papers, theoretical base), correctness (validation), contribution (main
contribution), and clarity (well written).
3.4.1
John Habraken (1960’s) open buildings
"Open Building seeks to enhance longevity and resilience through a set of basic principles
that affect design, as well as how buildings are constructed and managed over time"
- N. John Habraken, 1960
The theory presented by John Habraken in ’Supports: an Alternative to Mass Housing ‘De Dragers en de
Mensen’ (1961) was assessed through two secondary online sources (MANIFESTO OPENBUILDING.CO
(openbuilding.co 2020) and Open system (open building) (Raghad Almarhoon 2021)) which are reviewed
through the 5c, as discussed above. (The theory was also developed in the film ”DE DRAGER / A film
about Architect John Habraken” (schwarzpictures.com 2013) which is not reviewed here).
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: Philosophical development.
• Context: ”Open Building supports the transition to a society based on co-creation, participation,
involvement and inclusion”.(openbuilding.co 2020)
• Correctness: An accurate portrayal of current work and history of the field, professional layout.
• Contribution: Constant transformation of the built environment at different levels, such as City
structure, Tissue, Support, House, Infill and design, factors heavily in the literature of philosophy
3.2. Overview of ideology, case studies, further reading.
• Clarity: non-academic, strongly opinionated.
Figure 3.2: Open Building - N. John Habraken (1961) (openbuilding.co 2020)
Findings
Open building is an adaptive ideology developed by several prominent figures and organisations, it re-
volves around the key concept of the differentiation between support and infill of a structure, and the
levels of involvement and intervention theory. A user-centred ideology, it is noted as one of the origin
points of the Adaptable/flexible architecture movement and plug-and-play systems.
Flexible Design Analysis
17
==PAGE:31==
Though the theory of open buildings is the forefather of further development in the field, it in itself lacks
certain clarity and depth, with little attempt to quantify the principles it dictates. A solid theoretical foun-
dation to be developed on with detailed, visceral categories. The timeline developed in this theory could
be seen to not hold true for all sectors and use types.
"We must engage the environment’s terms, not just our own intentions" - N. John Habraken
1960
Further reading

https://www.habraken.com/index.html https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=85vhtwRwk9k&ab_channel=
schwarzpictures.com https://councilonopenbuilding.org/
3.4.2
Steward Brand (1994), How Buildings Learn
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: philosophical development/theoretical analysis.
• Context: Based on open buildings with the incorporation of a ”whole earth” (Brand 1986) mentality.
• Correctness: Detailed concepts outlined with examples.
• Contributions: The theoretical lineage for many future works on adaptability, concepts of shearing
layers of change: Site, Structure, Skin, Services, Space plan, Stuff. 3.3
• Clarity: Researched and presented in both text and video formats.
Figure 3.3: Shearing Layers of Change (1994) Stewart Brand
Findings
This review references both the book How Buildings Learn: What Happens After They’re Built (Brand
1994) and the television series ”How Buildings Learn by Stewart Brand” (Brand 1997) based on the book.
Stewart Brand outlines the benefits of ”low road, accessible construction” and gives a good view of the
unconsidered side of the housing stock, analysing such things as Edge cities and promoting the concept
that there are ”no finished spaces”. His work shows high respect for heritage and builds insightful recom-
mendations such as setting aside resources for future repair at the initial building stage. He coins the term
"Adaptive state" in this literature.
Flexible Design Analysis
18
==PAGE:32==
No quantifiable metrics were developed and this work has the temporal disadvantage of being an older re-
port. As such, there is little consideration of technologies’ role and analysis of heating and energy costs are
not developed in the case studies, only elements of structure and finance. The theory is mainly concerned
with ”Mature building” and grassroots projects and solutions.
Concepts developed
The concepts developed from this literature were the inclusion of the maintenance aspect of adaptability
in any flexibility/adaptability tool, such as an open-source building management/tracking software.
3.4.3
Steven Groak (1992), The idea of building: thought and action in the design and
production of buildings (Book Review (Barry Russell 1992))
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: Philosophical development
• Context: The text highlights the author’s perspective on the need for holistic thinking in science
and the building industry, drawing from systems theory principles from over fifty years ago.
• Correctness: Insightful overview of the author’s viewpoint, correctly references relevant works.
• Contributions: The text emphasizes the importance of holistic properties in understanding com-
plex systems like buildings and touches upon the role of research and development in the building
industry.
• Clarity: The text covers a broad range of topics related to architecture and systems theory, but its
brevity and the fast-paced movement between topics sometimes make it challenging to delve deeply
into each subject.
Findings
Groak’s work was broadly reviewed through a pre-existing book review, the main takeaways from this
work was the concept of holistic thinking and its application to the building industry, particularly in un-
derstanding buildings as dynamic and adaptable entities. While the text provides a valuable perspective
on the evolving nature of the building industry and the role of research and development, its brevity may
leave some topics less explored than desired. A more comprehensive referencing method would enhance
the text’s support. Overall, the text offers a unique perspective on the intersection of art, science, and
technology in architecture, drawing attention to the importance of holistic thinking in building design and
construction.
3.4.4
Frank Duffy (1992), The Changing Workplace (Book Review (Gorb 1992))
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: philosophical development
• Context: A unique perspective from both an architect and a social anthropologist.
• Correctness: Author’s viewpoint, no citation, not academic.
• Contributions: Focus on office buildings and their interaction with users, evolving perspective on
office spaces and their design.
• Clarity: Accessible to a broader audience.
Findings
This work was broadly reviewed through a pre-existing book review. The main takeaways from this work
were Duffy’s unique background as an architect and social anthropologist developing his view of office
Flexible Design Analysis
19
==PAGE:33==
buildings and their relationship with users and the reference to Duffys changing views over time, empha-
sizing the absence of self-justification. Additionally, the review discusses the book’s organization into
chapters with distinct themes, allowing it to be used as a reference document, architectural history, or so-
cial commentary. The text also commends Duffy’s perspective on wider relationships involved in building
design and management, including architects, clients, developers, and managers. Overall, it underscores
the value of Duffy’s insights in understanding the evolving nature of office spaces and their impact on the
business world.

practice
"Adaptability is the combination of elements that provide a stable infrastructure
that supports the change of infill elements." (Robert Schmidt 2016) pg296

This book serves as an extensive compendium for designers and academics. It outlines the history, theories,

"As others before us have found, setting out a clear, unambiguous definition and position

Value judgment as a key part of adaptable flexibility is paramount to the tool developed in this thesis,

Mixed-use buildings, maximizing the reward for the investment in a structure and its design,
The question, what is a building? Vans, mobile homes and boats are movable structures, are they buildings?

Flexible adaptation requires ”quality that increases with age”. This raises the questions of why the in-
dustry aims towards unskilled labour (factory-built components), and does the push towards adaptability
incorporate an insidious effort to reduce the quality of structures?
An concept that the need for flexibility can be met with an open-sourced app for building management,
building controls (heat, light, ventilation, structure) by the user through a smartphone, planned adaptability
(daily/yearly) review by Facility managers (FM) or clients/users (similar to an M.O.T for buildings)
3.4.6
Ballinger (2019), The big 5
This review is based on both a website (Ballinger 2019a) and video presentation (Ballinger 2019b) which
outline the assessment method used by the contractor and design firm Ballinger to rate the adaptability of
buildings to be converted to healthcare institutions, for current healthcare facilities to be renovated or new
builds constructed 3.7.
Figure 3.7: Ballinger- the big five (Ballinger 2019b)
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: conceptual modelling/case study
• Context: Hospital design and ”State of the Future technology”.
• Correctness: Professionally biased - based on undocumented forecasts and speculation.
• Contribution: Interesting concepts and distribution of factors influencing the adaptability of space.
Development of ”The big five”: Floor to floor heights, column spacing, fixed vertical elements,
strategic zones of flexibility and resiliency. A clear and simple analysis method.
• Clarity: Clear infographic - no details about the research of data is provided.
Findings
It is a clear 5-scale rating system, based on 5 predefined categories with laid out recommendations. The
tool itself is simple and relies primarily on empirical systems for its evaluation, though some analysis is
required. Overall it does not take the whole form of adaptability into consideration but mainly the space
and structure of a building.

5 Cs Evaluation

Green Star or LEED methodology.” (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013)
• Correctness: Valid assumptions backed up by several detailed case studies.
• Contributions: The development and testing of a new design rating tool called adaptSTAR, which
is ”a weighted checklist of design strategies that lead to future successful adaptive reuse of buildings

and not all case studies have relevant descriptions.
Findings
Flexible Design Analysis

==PAGE:37==
This article has a good discussion of design criteria, ”weighted according to physical, economic, functional,
technological, social, legal and political categories” 3.8 and includes detailed case studies for further study.
Its focus is on green implications and its acknowledgement of the lack of consensus within the field high-
lights a fuller understanding of the topic. Its discussion of the importance of socio-cultural design is also
of note.
Gaps in the report revolve around its focus on existing structures (The Case studies are all heritage build-
ings), geographical constraints (it is focused on the Pacific region) and temporal relevance as it was com-
piled in 2012 (since then there has been technological change and climate issues have increased).

From this paper the concept development of including the different weighting of indicators of different
categories, (e.g. the social metric being more important for government projects, structural metrics having
a heavier weight for commercial buildings etc.) was gained, as was the concept of including the option
of evaluating by only a specific selected criterion (eg Structure, energy etc.) as part of the tool. The
additional model criteria of Fire, disability, and Acoustics were gained. Finally, the reduction of the case
study analysts to ”Loose Fit (Functional)” (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013) aspects only is
considered after this review.

3.4.8
James Pinder, Robert Schmidt and Jim Saker (2013), Stakeholder perspectives on
developing more adaptable buildings.
Figure 3.9: Examples of the interplay between cost and uncertainty when developing more adaptable
buildings (Pinder, Schmidt, and Saker 2013)
5C s Evaluation
• Category: Theoretical analysis
• Context: ”Determine why, despite the compelling arguments in favour of adaptability, more build-
ings are not constructed to be more adaptable”(Pinder, Schmidt, and Saker 2013)
• Correctness: High-quality academic research with extensive references, methodical interviews and
data reading/categorizing
• Contribution: Literary review, additional research interviews for the industry.
• Clarity: Very succinct analyses with well-presented limitations and recommended further research.
It is a Well-developed insight into the topic of adaptable structures framed by commercial imple-
mentation and realism.
Findings
This article has a good overview of the conceptual change needed to initiate flexible design, as it outlines
the necessity to sell the present (first use) for the concept of a future (second use) by implementing often
more costly designs, and the lack of empirical data from the field and evidence of the benefit of design
makes it financially unfeasible for clients to fund ”guesswork” in regards to adaptable design.
Shortfalls: self-proclamation of stakeholder bias (mainly architects interviewed), no solution suggested,
just a detailed outline of the issue.
Concepts Developed
The concepts developed were the inclusion of a cost-benefit analysis element of the tool. The discount
factor for weighting: What is useful now and in the future, and what is only useful for the future? 3.9

3.4.9
Karen Vase Langballe (2021), Transformation capacity as a design parameter
5c s Evaluations
• Category: Conceptual modelling/case study
• Context: Academic thesis for DTU based on DGNB (German Sustainable Building Council) stan-
dards
• Correctness: Well-presented academic report. It outlines the steps taken in the research process
and the application of indicators and measurement methods for assessing transformation capacity.
• Contributions: The text provides information about certification systems like BREEAM and DGNB
and their adaptation for assessing building flexibility. It also introduces the Analytic Hierarchy
Process (AHP) and the Grading Method as tools for evaluating criteria in building design. Well-
presented mathematical evaluations are also presented.
• Clarity: It explains the concept of transformation potential in building design and describes the
development of a transformation capacity tool, presenting a case study to illustrate its application.
It also mentions the discussion of limitations, advantages, and areas for further research, indicating
a comprehensive approach to the topic.
Findings
This text provides valuable information about certification systems and methods for assessing flexibility in
buildings. It effectively introduces the concept of building transformation potential as a design parameter
for renovation, outlines the research methodology used, and presents a case study to demonstrate the tool’s
application. The discussion of limitations and future research areas adds depth to the study.
3.4.10
Oscar Westergaard and Kristoffer Negendahl, (2022), Flexhospital
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: Conceptual modelling
• Context: Academic thesis for DTU based on scenario simulation.
• Correctness: Fits the academic schema, but is based on modelling with questionable validity.
• Contributions: Development of virtual user flows in hospitals and modular design concepts.
• Clarity: Well-modeled theory but hard-to-follow report.
Findings
This report outlines a theoretical ”Flex hospital” made of movable modular units attached to a central
highrise core. It details a digitalised user flow for hospitals to assess the utility of such a design.(Oscar
Westergaard 2022)
Good overview of flow, and a decent literary review to expand the search. Little evidence of the actual
performance of the concept - focuses on only one metric of flexibility and doesn’t consider other influenc-
ing factors. A very detailed analysis, it is held back due to the complicated mechanical system proposed
and only basic layout available for this solution with no style considered.
It stands to the thesis that a test and evaluation of the tool were conducted before assessing the final results.
However, no margins of error for the user flow (such as for human error or rest break times) are included
in the model.

3.4.11
Francesco Carlucci, Kristoffer Negendahl and F. Fiorito (2023), Energy flexibility
of building systems in future scenarios: optimization of the control strategy of a
dynamic shading system and definition of a new energy flexibility metric
5 Cs Evaluation
• Category: Conceptual Modeling
• Context: Academic thesis for DTU
• Correctness: Well-presented academic article, discussing the increasing awareness of climate change,
the importance of energy efficiency in buildings, and the need for flexibility in building design. It
also outlines the methodology used in the study.
• Contributions: Addressing the challenges of future scenarios in building design, particularly re-
garding climate change. It introduces the concept of an Energy Flexibility Index (EFI) and assessing
various scenarios.
• Clarity: The text is well-structured and logically presented, providing a clear overview of the re-
search objectives and methodology. However, some sentences are lengthy and complex, which
might require careful reading for full comprehension.
Findings
This text effectively introduces the context of climate change and energy efficiency in building design. It
highlights the complexities of future scenarios and the need to account for various factors. The concept of
flexibility in buildings and the introduction of the Energy Flexibility Index (EFI) (Carlucci, K. Negendahl,
and Fiorito 2023) are valuable contributions.

==PAGE:43==
3.5
Conclusion
Key findings from the literature review indicate that it is possible to develop a tool to analyse the flexibility
of the design of a building and that much work has been done to assess the different elements that affect
this concept into:
• Levels (openbuilding.co 2020)
• Layers (Brand 1994)
• Typology (Robert Schmidt 2016)
• Strategies (ibid.)
• Characteristics (ibid.)
• Tactics (ibid.)
• Solutions (ibid.)
• Variables (Ballinger 2019a).
There is a cohesive concept of what adaptability is but no unified standard system for description or assess-
ment. Many tools have been developed such as Adaptstar (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013),
and most are based on pre-existing sustainability tool frameworks.
The theory is well established but numerical evaluation and categorical assessments are still speculative
and not widely implemented in the AEC industry.
There is room for the development of a unified theory and numerical evaluation to be developed in fol-
lowing the logic of environmental analysis models that have already been well established.
3.6
Recommendations for future research
From this research further development of a unifying tool is realised.
The knowledge gap appears to be within the concept of linear evaluation. A spectrum-based approach
using a design circularity to develop a phase view of the concept of adaptability is seen as a novel solution
to be engaged with.
The other missing feature from similar tools and concepts developed in this field is the ability for end-user
weighting of the different criteria. Further development is possible by giving agency back to the designer
and allowing for the aspect of opinion inherent in any evaluation to be catered for.
Complex ideology is condensed to simple figures in much of the work reviewed, this trend of simplicity is
followed in the further development of this report and tool, with the adaptability of assessment complexity
allowed for, to meet the specific use case.
The importance of maintenance is mentioned in some texts but never defined as its own category, the
addition of which is supported by this thesis.
