4
Theoretical Concepts
4.1
Introduction to the Theories
This section outlines the theoretical background for the development of a flexible index design assessment
tool and presents the logic structure followed in this report.
The basis of this report stems from two key systems of analysis, the analytical hierarchy process and
modern portfolio theory, both of which are explained here. These theories form the basis for the numerical
evaluation of the flexibility of structures presented in this report. The audience is assumed to have some
prior knowledge of statistics, economics and mathematical modelling.
The analytical evaluation carried out in this thesis, including the basis of the analytical hierarchy process, is
far less developed than the numerical principles and has been built upon previous studies and extrapolation
of current best practices, but is enshrined in strong assumptions of logical sequences.
This analytical basis is outlined through the development of a framework for flexible evaluation, presented
below in figure 1.6.
The theories of analytical hierarchy process (AHP), modern portfolio theory (MPT), classical probability
theory, the Monte Carlo Method, a flexibility framework, LCA, DGNB, desing characteristics, sensitivity
analysis, reliability analyses, and environmental psychology are outlined below.
4.2
Analytical hierarchy process (AHP)
The Analytical Hierarchy Process (AHP) (Saaty 2008) is a decision-making methodology that provides
a structured approach for systematically evaluating and prioritising multiple criteria or alternatives in a
decision-making context. AHP involves breaking down complex decisions into a hierarchy of criteria and
sub-criteria and then comparing them by assigning weighting to them based on their relative importance
(Vaidya and Kumar 2006). The AHP methodology consists of the following steps:
• Problem Structuring: To identify the decision problem and establish a hierarchical structure of crite-
ria and sub-criteria. In the context of assessing the flexibility of structures, the criteria chosen were:
Cost, Ownership, Lifecycleplan, Maintenance, Society, Technical, Structural, Aesthetic, Space, and
Digitization.
• Pairwise Comparisons: To compare the criteria and sub-criteria pairwise to determine their relative
importance using a numerical scale. The scale ranges from 1 to 10 for this report, with the ability of
the user to augment this weighting with their preference using a factor from 1-3 as a multiplier. On
these scales, 1 represents equal importance and 10 (or 3 for the user) represents extreme importance.
For example, if the design characteristic of cost is compared to the design characteristic of Aesthetics,
with a value of 5 assigned to cost and a value of 4 assigned to aesthetics, this indicates that cost is
moderately more important than Aesthetics.
• Weighting Calculation: To calculate the weights or priorities of criteria and sub-criteria based on
the pairwise comparison results. This is carried out through the Excel tool developed for this thesis,
DATEX (see appendix A.1).
• Consistency Check: To assess the consistency of the pairwise comparisons to ensure logical and
reliable results. Inconsistencies may arise if the user’s judgements are contradictory. A pedigree
matrix to assess the information used by the end user/analyst and Monte Carlo simulations to evaluate
the accuracy of the weighting are used to evaluate the reliability and sensitivity of the data.
Flexible Design Analysis
31
==PAGE:45==
• Aggregation and Ranking: To aggregate the weights of criteria and sub-criteria to determine their
overall priorities. This is done by multiplying the weights along the paths of the hierarchy. The final
result is a ranking of the alternatives based on their overall desirability. This is the last step in the
standard AHP process. From here the adjusted average of these results are found and the mean is
taken to allow for further analysis of the flexibility of the structure as a whole.
4.3
Modern portfolio theory (MPT)
Modern Portfolio Theory (MPT) is a widely recognised framework for optimising investment portfolios
based on the principles of risk and return. Originally, introduced by Harry Markowitz in 1952. MPT em-
phasises the importance of diversification to reduce risk while maximising potential returns. It is outlined
in the (Nina Lange 2022) course at the Technical University of Denmark and in (Fabozzi, Markowitz, and
Gupta 2008).
The core idea behind MPT is that an investor can construct an efficient portfolio by combining assets with
different risk and return characteristics. The theory assumes that investors are risk-averse and seek to
maximize their expected returns, given a certain level of risk, or minimize their risk, given a desired level
of return.
It is posed in this report that in the context of analyzing a building’s design factors, MPT can be applied to
assess the performance and risk associated with different combinations of these factors. Each design factor
can be treated as an asset, and the design variations present in various design solutions represent different
portfolios. The goal is to identify the optimal portfolio that offers the best trade-off between performance
and risk.
The mathematics of MPT involves several key calculations, including expected returns, variances, and
covariances of the design factors. These statistical measures quantify the average return, volatility, and
interrelationships between the design factors. By considering these measures, an efficient frontier can be
constructed, representing the set of portfolios that offer the highest expected returns for each level of risk
or the lowest risk for each level of expected return.
Within the context of building design, for this thesis, the design factors of Cost, Ownership, Lifecycle-
plan, Maintenance, Society, Technical, Structural, Aesthetic, Space and Digitisation have been assigned
Weights, based on the AHP, reflecting their importance in the overall design. These weights are taken
as the ”return” of the different ”assets”, which represent the benefit of the design characteristics to the
flexibility of the structure.
Based on historical data (see Chapter3) and expert opinions (See appendix A.5.4), the expected returns
can be estimated as well as the variances, and covariances of these design factors. Using these inputs,
we can construct an efficient frontier by generating various combinations of the design factors (referred
to as portfolios/design solutions) and calculating the expected returns and volatility’s associated with each
combination. The efficiency frontier represents the optimal trade-off between the design factors, allowing
us to identify the most desirable design solutions based on our risk-return preferences.
It is important to note that while MPT provides a valuable framework for portfolio optimisation, its ap-
plication to building design requires adapting the mathematical calculations and assumptions to suit the
specific context.
The mathematical principles of the efficiency frontier are outlined in the slides from the 42104 Introduction
to Financial Engineering Fall 22, lecture notes 7 and 8 and are presented in the appendix (A.3.3) (Nina
Lange 2022).
4.3.1
Assumptions
MPT is based on several key assumptions that form the foundation of its framework for portfolio analysis
and optimisation.
Flexible Design Analysis
32
==PAGE:46==
• It is assumed the actors involved are rational decision-makers who aim to maximize their expected
returns while minimizing risk.
• MPT assumes that the actors involved evaluate risk solely based on the statistical measure of variance
or standard deviation. It assumes that higher volatility or dispersion of returns implies higher risk.
• MPT assumes that actors are risk-averse, meaning they prefer portfolios that offer higher returns for
a given level of risk or lower risk for a given level of returns.
• MPT assumes that actors can reduce the overall risk of their portfolio by diversifying their invest-
ments across different asset classes. Diversification helps to mitigate the unsystematic or idiosyn-
cratic risk associated with individual assets.
• MPT assumes that asset returns follow a normal distribution, meaning they have a symmetrical bell-
shaped pattern. This assumption allows for the use of statistical measures like mean and variance in
portfolio analysis (Normalisation is carried out for the design characteristics by using the adjusted
average of the design characteristic final weighting).
• MPT assumes that there is no friction associated with developing assets. This simplifying assump-
tion helps focus solely on the risk-return trade-off.
These assumptions can limit the applicability and accuracy of MPT in real-world scenarios.
4.3.2
Similar work
The concept of an efficiency frontier or Pareto front is not new in this field. Though not assessed in the
literature review, the outline in Langston, 2008, of a ”useful to physical” obsolescence efficiency frontier,
as presented by (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013)is of note. 4.1. This, along with other papers,
such as (Kristoffer Negendahl and Nielsen 2015), outline the precedent for this line of logical application
of MPT and similar theories.
Figure 4.1: Adaptive reuse potential model (Langston, 2008) (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013)
4.4
Classical probability theory
The probability theory that provides the chance of an event as a percentage is known as ”frequentist prob-
ability” or ”classical probability.” In this theory, the probability of an event is calculated by dividing the
number of favourable outcomes by the total number of equally likely outcomes. The result is usually ex-
pressed as a percentage, ranging from 0% (impossible event) to 100 % (certain event). This approach is
commonly used in various fields, including statistics, mathematics, and decision-making as outlined in the
Technical University of Denmark course (Katsanos et al. 2022).
Flexible Design Analysis
33
==PAGE:47==
4.5
Monte Carlo method
The Monte Carlo method is a computational technique used to simulate and analyse complex systems or
processes that involve uncertainty. It relies on generating random samples from probability distributions
to approximate and analyse the behaviour of a system. By repeatedly sampling from these distributions, it
allows for the estimation of statistical outcomes and the evaluation of various scenarios, as outlined in the
Technical University of Denmark course (Katsanos et al. 2022).
The Monte Carlo method is utilised in this thesis to assess the performance data sets under different un-
certain conditions and parameters. It provides a robust framework for analysing the complex systems,
analysed here.
4.6
Flexibility framework
A primary goal for this report was to identify the best existing practices for the analysis of flexibility in
structures. For this purpose, a framework is developed as a step-by-step guide to the process involved in
such an analysis outlined in figure 1.6.
The field of flexibility studies lacks a common path to structural assessment, such as is present in the ISO
standards for LCA (standards 2006). Therefore this report develops a best practice framework for carrying
out flexibility analysis, not specific to any tool or principles, but based on sound scientific actions and
process checks as seen in current literature and study programs. For this report chapter 3 and the Technical
University of Denmark course (Kristoffer Negendahl 2023) were used to gain this insight.
A framework plays a crucial role in any process by providing a structured and systematic approach to guide
its execution. It serves as a road map, offering a clear and organized pathway to achieve desired outcomes
efficiently and effectively. The importance of the framework lies in its ability to bring clarity, consis-
tency, and coherence to a process. Four frameworks were developed from relevant literature and current
practices. These frameworks are outlined in figure 1.6, figure 9.1, figure 9.2 and figure 9.3. The process
for their development is outlined in chapter 9. The frameworks developed establish a set of principles,
guidelines, and steps to help streamline activities, align efforts, and facilitate decision-making. It provides
a common language and understanding among stakeholders, allowing for collaboration and coordination.
Furthermore, the framework ensures that critical aspects and considerations are not overlooked, promoting
comprehensive and thorough analysis.
By establishing a framework, the process of evaluating the flexibility of buildings becomes increasingly
more manageable, predictable, and scalable, thus enabling practitioners to navigate challenges, optimize
resources, and achieve flexibility with greater confidence and success.
4.7
Life cycle Assessment (LCA)
LCA involves a comprehensive evaluation of environmental impact throughout an entire life cycle. An
assessment considers various stages, from raw material extraction and manufacturing to construction, op-
eration, maintenance, and eventual demolition or recycling. (Anders Damgaard 2023)
The primary objective of LCA in building science is to quantify and analyze the environmental effects
associated with a building or construction project. This includes examining factors like energy consump-
tion, resource depletion, greenhouse gas emissions, water usage, and waste generation. LCA provides a
holistic view of sustainability by accounting for all these aspects. An LCA can be used to produce an
environmental product declaration (EPD), which is a detailed evaluation of the level of environmental
impact.
Key steps in conducting an LCA for building science typically include:
• Goal Definition and Scope: Clearly defining the goals and boundaries of the assessment. What
Flexible Design Analysis
34
==PAGE:48==
aspects of the building’s life cycle will be included, and what environmental indicators will be mea-
sured?
• Inventory Analysis: Collecting data on all relevant inputs and outputs throughout the building’s life
cycle, such as energy use, materials, and emissions.
• Impact Assessment: Evaluating the environmental impacts of the identified inputs and outputs. This
involves using models and databases to calculate indicators like carbon footprint or water usage.
• Interpretation: Analyzing and interpreting the LCA results to draw conclusions about the building’s
environmental performance. Comparing alternative designs or materials to identify opportunities
for improvement.
• Recommendations: Providing recommendations for making the building or construction project
more environmentally sustainable based on the LCA findings.
This process is augmented in the standard Danish LCA process for buildings which incorporates stricter
guidelines, scope and goals. For clarity, in this thesis, the LCA approach outlined here is the method
considered.
4.8
DGNB
DGNB (Deutsche Gesellschaft für Nachhaltiges Bauen or German Sustainable Building Council) refers
to a comprehensive sustainability certification system for buildings and urban developments. DGNB fo-
cuses on evaluating and promoting environmentally friendly, socially responsible, and economically viable
construction and planning practices. (Nachhaltiges Bauen 2007)
Key aspects of DGNB include:
• Sustainability Assessment: DGNB provides a framework for assessing the sustainability of buildings
and urban areas. It covers various criteria, such as energy efficiency, resource conservation, health
and well-being, and socio-cultural aspects.
• Life Cycle Perspective: DGNB takes a life cycle approach, considering the environmental impact
of a building or development from construction through its entire operational life and eventual de-
molition or repurposing.
• Certification Levels: DGNB offers different certification levels (Platinum, Gold, Silver, and Bronze)
based on how well a project meets sustainability criteria. This allows for benchmarking and recog-
nition of sustainable achievements.
• Customisation: DGNB is flexible and allows for tailoring sustainability criteria to the specific con-
text and goals of a project. This adaptability is valuable for addressing unique challenges in engi-
neering and construction.
• Holistic Approach: DGNB considers various aspects of sustainability, including ecological, eco-
nomic, functional, technical, and social dimensions. It encourages interdisciplinary collaboration
among architects, engineers, and planners.
• Innovation: DGNB encourages the integration of innovative technologies and sustainable practices
to advance the field of building science.
• Market Recognition: DGNB certification is recognised internationally and can enhance a building
project’s marketability.
• DGNB is a leading example of the success of sustainability certification and environmental con-
struction practices.
Flexible Design Analysis
35
==PAGE:49==
4.9
Design characteristics
4.9.1
Introduction
In this section the basis for the categorisation of design characteristics and the design factors chosen for
use in the DAT flexible design analysis tool, developed in this report, are outlined. A brief summary of the
current theory that influenced the tool developed in this report is given and the categorisation used for the
tool is explained. Both the connection with previous theories and how each category should be assessed
by the end user of the flexibility design analyses tool is discussed.
4.9.2
Background
Various design characteristics used to develop categorisation systems for analysing flexibility in buildings
are outlined in works such as (Robert Schmidt 2016), (Ballinger 2019a), (Brand 1994), (Langballe 2021),
and (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013). These theories are outlined in 4.2. Below is an account
of the categorisation theories that have influenced the design characteristics used in the DAT tool developed
in this report.
Figure 4.2: Current Theory
Elaborating on figure 4.2 initially with Robert Schmidt and Simon Austin work(Robert Schmidt 2016).
The frame cycle meta-model, figure3.4, lists 6 design strategies, 12 characteristics and 136 design tactics.
The design strategies comprise of
Adjustable, versatile, refitable, convertible, scalable and movable
The 12 characteristics, as outlined in the design for adaptability (DfA) model (Figure 4.4) encompass:
Modularity, Design ‘in’ time, Long life, Simplicity and legibility, Loose fit, Spatial, planning, Passive
techniques, Unfinished design, Maximise building use, Increased interactivity, Aesthetics, Multiple scales.
Robert Schmidt and Simon Austin also outline 5 ”black box” solutions (figure 3.6), and 12 generally
unconsidered physical and social variables in their work (ibid.):
Black Box (common solutions): Movable walls, Taller floor height, Access points, Service Strategies,
High-tech Components.
Physical Variables: Storage space, Materials, Window size, Fire, strategy, Construction Method, Building
form.
Flexible Design Analysis
36
==PAGE:50==
Social Variables: Procurement process, Regulations, Market Conditions, Local appreciation, Ownership,
model, Designer skills.
Further (Ballinger 2019a) identifies five essential design characteristics and provides a numerical range for
these characteristics to ensure compliance with structural flexibility 3.7 (Specific to the healthcare industry,
these parameters can also be utilised on a wider stage). These are floor-to-floor heights, column spacing,
fixed vertical elements, strategic zones and resiliency.
Stewart brand developed a different technique, rather than segregating the structure by aspects of design,
he concludes that subdividing the structure into ”layers” is the best model for assessing and planning for
flexibility. He developed six layers of change from the open building concepts outlined by John Habraken
(openbuilding.co 2020) in his works on ”how buildings change” (Brand 1994). The layers include:
The site, structure, skin, services, system, stuff.
Langballe, 2021((Langballe 2021)), analyses multiple works from the Dutch school of flexibility and in-
corporates them as part of the analytical rating system, giving a good overview of the prevalence of the
different concepts in the field as it stands (Also refer to the accompanying podcast (Colclough 2023)).
These include the rating metrics of:
Dimensions and functionality: Dimensions of facade openings Modular coordination of dimensions Clear
structural spans Ceiling height Building depth Space efficiency factor Space functionality factor Vertical
access Corridor width
Layout and positioning: Facade division Positioning of loadbearing elements Distribution and positioning
of service Positioning of vertical access
Access and disassembly: Adaptable facade elements Access to service
Capacity: Vertical extension Extra capacity for service Horizontal extension Capacity of vertical access
Capacity of corridors
Langballe, 2021((Langballe 2021)), scores these parameters under the combination categories of Simplic-
ity, Standardization and Detachability.
The final pre-existing categorisation used to develop the logical segregation of building design into concept
blocks for analysis used for the tool developed in this thesis was the AdaptSTAR analysis tool.
AdaptSTAR utilises 6 categories for its assessment of adaptable buildings: Physical, Economic, Func-
tional, Technical, Legal and Political (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013)
4.9.3
Design Characteristic Development
From reviewing the above classification, it is posed here that the nine design characteristics of:
• Cost
• Ownership
• Lifecycleplan
• Maintenance
• Society
• Technical
• Structural
• Aesthetic
• Space
Flexible Design Analysis
37
==PAGE:51==
• Digitization
Are primary for the evaluation of flexible structures. These characteristics have been used in the DATEx
tool developed in this report and are elaborated below.
Cost
Cost comprises the financial implications associated with the structure and its lifecycle. This cost can
be related to many sub-categories, such as environmental costs, cost of demolition, the future cost of
adjustments to the structure, operating costs, and opportunity costs. Therefore, it is essential to clearly
state the specific case under consideration and the required depth of analysis when assessing the flexibility
of a building in relation to cost.
An expensive building is more adaptable due to the perceived worth of the design and services and the
unwillingness of stakeholders to lose the initial investment in the structure by demolishing the structure.
A cheap structure is adaptable as it can be transformed with little investment. It is easier to add a space to
a shed than it is to a skyscraper, however, a skyscraper is far more difficult to demolish. Small and large
scale adaptability both lead to a flexible design.
Ownership
The ownership model (private, public, single owner, shared ownership, owner occupier, developer, retire-
ment fund etc.) is vital in understanding the ultimate goal of the project and the priorities of the client
and designer. How a structure adapts depends on this ownership model and how it changes with the life
of the structure. A declaration of ownership as well as assumed responsibility and priorities for the struc-
ture should be included in the report generated from the use of the design analysis tool. A basis for this
characteristic comes from the ”black box” model where Ownership is referred to under ”social variables”.
A building owned by many owners can have an extended life as decisions on change come with additional
bureaucratic effort. A multiple owners model can both slow flexibility and increase the knowledge and
skill involved in implementing a good, flexible, design solution. A single owner has far more agency over
the needs and decisions around change but can also lack the finance and knowledge needed to develop
lasting, flexible concepts. Consideration of how future ownership can affect the structure and its use is
the metric assessed in the tool developed in this report (Dale, Supervisor, and Langlais 2011) (Makvärket
2013).
Lifecycle plan
Planning for flexibility is considered a primary factor in a building’s ability to adapt, the concept of the
increase in the likelihood of an event if that event is planned for, in itself highlights this parameter’s core
connection with the process of adaptability. Without a clear framework for concepts and design elements
which allow for the future, the ability for change is diminished.
The idea of a lifecycle plan is covered in the ”black box” model under service strategies, the big five model
under resiliency, and in part, strategic zones, the idea of unfinished designs concept from (Robert Schmidt
2016), figure 4.4 design characteristics, versatility in the frame cycle meta-model, figure 3.4, and discussed
by Stewart brand in his work (Brand 1994), though never specifically categorized. This highlights that
a lifecycle plan is an intrinsic part of the consideration of super categories of other tools, showing that
buildings with the capacity for unplanned change and buildings with a well-developed road-map to future
states have the most flexibility.
Maintenance
As discussed by Stewart Brand (Brand 1997) maintenance is vital to the lifespan of a structure, and a
building’s ability to change both structurally and in its use. If a building is neglected and falls into disrepair,
its adaptability is not necessarily linearly related to its state of dereliction. As shown in ”How Buildings
Flexible Design Analysis
38
==PAGE:52==
Learn” (Brand 1997), a derelict building, in direct relation to price, can be more adaptable. This is because
it represents an opportunity for talented and passionate people to adapt the space without the need for large
systems of investment and structures of control. However, it is also evident that as a building falls into
disrepair, the chance of its demolition increases. A building’s potential falls as its systems degrade, till a
certain point, when it becomes attractive to alternative, un-gentrified actors. At this time its adaptability
increases. Meaning both the most and least maintained structures are the most adaptable.
Society
Social variables are considered as one of the three primary categories of the ”black box” model and this
design characteristic can also be linked to the ”increased interactivity” characteristic of the DfA model, as
well as the ”political” category of the AdaptSTAR model.
Society can encompass many of the other elements of a design and is interwoven into the essence of
the analysis itself. It affects the perception of the design characteristics of aesthetics, legal, political,
functionality, economics, ownership, and how we define a structure in itself. Buildings are designed in
many different ways across the globe, and variations in building technologies, processes and building codes
can be seen as much a part of the sociocultural differences as that of geographical and environmental
constraints. The analysis of social influence on the designs should include concepts adapted from the
design brief of the specific project. An introspective evaluation of what the geopolitical and sociocultural
expectations for the structure are, with considerations of the growth/future paradigm of the macro and
micro-social setting of the structure must also be considered in the evaluation of this design characteristic.
Technical
The technical aspects of a building bring us to more physical influences and away from the conceptual
aspects of a building’s design. This category is included in the AdaptSTAR model, the black box model,
and under ”passive techniques” in the DfA model. It is also represented as its own layer (services) by
Stewart Brand.
A high-tech building with state-of-the-future technologies can be seen as adaptable as it is taken that these
technologies increase the potential use of the building and will lengthen the period between renewal and
renovation. It is also posed that low-tech ”passive” solutions provide increased adaptability as they re-
duce the complexity of the design, do not become ”outdated” and have no planned obsolescence. Also,
due to lower running costs and fewer maintenance requirements, passive solutions can have a longer life
expectancy than high-tech solutions.
This Technical category is used to rate the implementation, prior consideration and layout of MEP (Me-
chanical, Electrical, Plumbing) services, both passive and Mechanical. There are dogmatic arguments for
both passive and high-tech, mechanical, solutions. However, the category of ”Technical” is an assessment
of the considerations of the prevailing design method (passive or mechanical) used, not a means to measure
the two against each other.
Structural
The structural aspects of a building are the primary focus of the Transform potential tool outlined by (Lang-
balle 2021), a layer of change from Stewart Brand’s exploration of the subject of flex ability and a primary
aspect of the AdaptSTAR models ”physical” category. Structural considerations are also intrinsic to many
lower-level design characteristics. As a primary disciple in the AEC industry and based on fundamental
laws of physics, its place as a characteristic of consideration could be seen as obvious.
The structure of a building can often be over-designed as a tactic for flexible buildings, allowing more
loads to be taken by the structure and so allowing for more intense uses than that originally designed to be
facilitated for by the building. Alternatively, a sleek and economical design allows for minimal waste and
Flexible Design Analysis
39
==PAGE:53==
for the resources (money, energy, time) saved from the construction process to be channelled into other,
more useful areas.
The analysis of the ”structure” design characteristic should follow the designer’s concept of best use and
best future use of the building. Consistency and clarity of forethought are the metrics for gauging its
implementation.
Aesthetics
A design characteristic category from the DfA model and inherent in the black box characteristics of
designer skill, building form and aesthetics, the ”aesthetics” design characteristic is a prime influential
factor in flexible design. Buildings that become UNESCO heritage sights and historic structures, have
all withstood the test of time and share a tangible element of superior design. The facades, detailing and
features of a building are aspects that make it unique and beautiful and can help develop a social tie with its
time and place and, therefore, its survival. Though not necessarily a factor that allows for changing future
use, the more aesthetically pleasing a building is, the less likely it will be torn down. Superior aesthetic
design leads to the adaptability of users, who are far more likely to change their habits rather than disrupt
a visually appealing structure.
Aesthetics is, as with other design characteristics, subjective and so hard to factor into a widely applicable
numerical analysis. Whether the aesthetics is part of the brief or not should be considered when utilising
the DATEx tool, other factors affecting this category’s weighting are dependent on the analyst using the
tool.
Space
Space in this context refers to both the physical spaces of buildings and the strategic zoning of that building.
It is considered a layer of change (Brand 1994), included in the ”spatial planning” characteristic of the DfA
model, and the ”strategic zones” category of the ”Big 5” model.
Having the space to adapt, supplied by floor-to-ceiling heights, column spacing, large sit plan, and other
design tactics, is a key to adaptability. The availability of space to change makes change more easily
accomplished, which thereby makes the decision to change a space instead of demolishing it far easier.
To consider space, one must consider the physical elements that allow for user activities and the layout of
those spaces. Do the physical elements allow for multiple user activities, and is the layout of those spaces
augmentable?
Digitization
Digitization is not specifically considered in the reviewed literature, though it could be seen as an element
of ”technical” categorization of the AdaptSTAR model and ”high-tech components” characteristic of the
”Black box” model.
It is, however, theorised in this report that the digitization of building information into building information
models (BIM) is a paramount factor in the ability of that building to be changed. If it is known how the
building was built, what components were used and how they were placed, it is far easier to remove, change
and repair necessary elements. This is referenced in episodes one, three and five of the interview series
carried out in this thesis (Colclough 2023), see appendix A.4.9.
In episode three (time stamp 26:38) of Stewart Brand’s TV show ”How Buildings Change” (Brand 1997),
he outlines how a photo album of the construction process is invaluable to the residents when repairs are
needed. Time has moved on since then, but the core concept remains the same. Detailed information about
the constriction of a structure allows for easier and quicker augmentation. Digital twins (virtual replicas
of physical buildings that integrate real-time data to simulate, analyze, and optimize their performance
throughout the entire lifecycle), BIM software and 3D laser scanning are all advancements towards the
Flexible Design Analysis
40
==PAGE:54==
digital integration of buildings and the benefits inherent in the increased analysis, planning and monitoring
ability therein.
Digitization is a characteristic that requires the assessor to rate the integration of modelling, information
processing and continued facility management of a structure and its planned use with digital solutions.
4.9.4
Categorisation Conspectus
This assembly of categories is objective. It is made based on various works of literature and key concepts,
though the exact terms and definitions have varied. The core area of development of this report is not in
the speciality of categorization theory within the field of flexibility. Categorization has been considered
here, and developed, as a small contribution to the theory of the discipline of flexibility and in an attempt
to improve clarity within the area. It is important to fully understand and clearly present the elements being
assessed by the tool developed in this report.
Other assessments of the complexity, description and definition of the design characteristics that make a
building flexible are not excluded due to the categorisation here. Moreover, they are embraced as important
variations that should be considered and researched by any designer or analyst who has developed an
understanding of the importance of flexibility in structures. As such the tool developed in this report
allows for the categories outlined above to be easily changed in the Excel file provided.
Robert Schmidt 2016 have developed many interesting visuals to represent the depth and interlarded prop-
erties of these principles (figure 4.3 and 4.4) highlighting the depth and complexity of assigning definite
characteristics. To move away from subjectivity separation must be made between attempting to precisely
define shifting concepts and developing repeatable processes that can be defined within a certain scope, in
which these concepts can be assessed.
Flexible Design Analysis

==PAGE:55==

4.10
Sensitivity Analyses
Sensitivity analysis is a technique used to determine how variations in input parameters affect the output
of the design analysis tool. It helps identify which input factors have the most significant impact on the
results and provides insights into the reliability and robustness of the data. As outlined in the Technical
University of Denmark’s course (Anders Damgaard 2023). The methods and techniques used to conduct
a sensitivity analysis in the tool developed in this Thesis include:
• Scatter plots: Scatter plots can be used to visualize the relationship between input variables and
outputs. By plotting different combinations of inputs and observing corresponding output variations,
one can identify influential factors.
• Sensitivity indices: Analytical hierarchy is used to quantify the contribution of each input parameter
to the overall output variance. These indices provide a ranking of parameters based on their relative
importance.
• Monte Carlo simulation: Monte Carlo simulation involves generating random samples of input pa-
rameters within specified ranges and analyzing the resulting distribution of outputs. It helps assess
the variability and uncertainty associated with different input values.
4.11
Reliability analyses
The reliability of the data is checked using a pedigree matrix which examines the pedigree and lineage
of the data. It involves verification of data sources, data quality checks, and data validation, as well as
data origin, collection methods, transformations, and manipulations performed. By establishing a robust
pedigree for the data, it becomes possible to evaluate its trustworthiness, consistency, and relevance for
the specific analysis. This is outlined in the Technical University of Denmark’s course (ibid.).
Flexible Design Analysis
43
==PAGE:57==
Implementing a systematic approach and a well-defined data pedigree matrix provides a structured frame-
work to document and evaluate the reliability of data sources, and it becomes easier to identify potential
limitations, uncertainties, or biases in the data, allowing for a more transparent, and defensible analysis.
In the tool developed in this report, a 5 by 5 matrix is used with the categories of data source, completeness,
temporal implications, geographic implications and technological correlation. These categories are then
given a scoring form 1-5 depending on what degree of reliability the gathered data has in the given field,
with 1 being the highest level of reliability and 5 being the lowest level of reliability.
For the specific data points relevant to the analyses being conducted using the DATEx tool, the tools user
highlights the relevant ”accuracy field” and assesses the overall pedigree matrix score. The score of the 5
categories is then averaged, and a reliability score is computed. The accuracy of this score is dependent on
the skill and effort of the analyser using the tool. As such this matrix is not included in the compilation of
the flexibility index, though the results of the pedigree matrix should be presented with the results of the
tool in order to provide a more clear and reliable result.
4.12
Environmental psychology
Environmental psychology is a theoretical concept that revolves around the study of how the physical
environment, including buildings and spaces, influences human behaviour, emotions, and overall well-
being. This concept provides valuable insights into designing structures that not only meet functional
requirements but also create environments that enhance the quality of life for their occupants.
At its core, environmental psychology emphasizes the interconnectedness between people and their sur-
roundings. This theory recognizes that the built environment has a profound impact on human experiences,
productivity, and even health.
The psychological and emotional effects on the people who inhabit or interact with a building plays a
pivotal role in the development concepts of the case study (the NHN super hospital project) used to test
and improve the DAT tool. It is also interlinked with the concept of flexibility itself.
Environmental psychology principles encompass various aspects, including how the layout and spatial
organization of a structure can influence people’s navigation and interactions within it. Additionally, these
principles explore how factors like natural lighting, ventilation, and material choices impact the comfort
and mood of occupants. Furthermore, they emphasize the role of noise levels, aesthetics, and overall
ambience in shaping the perceived quality of a space.
Furthermore, sustainability and environmental considerations are integral components of environmental
psychology. Designing environmentally responsible structures not only reduces the ecological footprint
but also aligns with the psychological well-being of individuals. People tend to have a more positive
perception of and connection to spaces that promote sustainability and harmony with the natural world.
In summary, environmental psychology offers a comprehensive framework for creating designs that go
beyond structural functionality. It encourages the creation of spaces that nurture the human spirit, promote
well-being, and contribute to a sustainable and harmonious coexistence with the environment. In the pur-
suit of flexible design strategies, understanding and applying the principles of environmental psychology
are essential.
4.13
Theoretical summation
In conclusion, this chapter has laid the foundation for the development of a flexible index design assessment
tool. It introduced key theoretical concepts and frameworks that serve as the basis for the subsequent
numerical evaluation of a building’s flexibility.
The main theories used in the tools numerical evaluation, Analytical Hierarchy Process (AHP) and Modern
Portfolio Theory (MPT), were presented.
Flexible Design Analysis
44
==PAGE:58==
While the analytical evaluation of flexibility is more subjective, it has been built upon prior research and
logical assumptions. From this evaluation, various design characteristics were developed, cost, ownership,
lifecycle plan, maintenance, societal, technical, structural, aesthetic, spatial, and digitisation.
A flexible framework was introduced to guide the analysis process, providing a structured approach to
assessing flexibility and serves as a practical tool for evaluating flexibility.
This chapter also briefly touched upon sensitivity analyses and reliability analyses, which play a vital role
in evaluating the robustness of the tools results.
Lastly, the inclusion of explanations of LCA, DGNB and environmental psychology present some of the
fundamental principles behind this report.
With this theoretical foundation in place, the subsequent chapters will delve into the practical implemen-
tation of these concepts in the development of the DATEx flexible design analysis tool.

==PAGE:59==
5
Experimental Technique
5.1
Preamble
This section outlines the experimental techniques, mathematical models and programs used to build the
DATEx flexibility analysis tool developed in this thesis. The techniques employed to test the hypothesis
of this report, which aims to determine if a singular flexibility score can effectively evaluate a structure’s
transformation capacity, encompassed several key steps. A comprehensive survey of relevant stakehold-
ers was conducted to gather initial data. Concurrently, in-depth interviews were carried out with both
stakeholders and relevant actors. These interviews aimed to provide contextual insights and a deeper
understanding of how flexibility is perceived within the realm of structures and their design. Mathemat-
ical modelling, involving the development of a numerical flexibility assessment using Excel and Python
programs, formed the basis for creating a practical flexibility index tool. Furthermore, Building Informa-
tion Modelling (BIM) and physical modelling were employed in the case study of this thesis. These two
modelling approaches were instrumental in gaining a more profound insight into both the advantages and
limitations of the developed flexibility assessment tool.
5.2
Interviews
The interview process was carried out from the initial stage through to the finalisation of this thesis, and
the information gathered was utilised, as it was gained, to augment and improve the accuracy and concepts
outlined in this thesis. The concept of flexibility and current theories, as well as personal, professional and
academic views on flexible design in buildings, design rating tools and other influencing factors, and the
relevance of these, were primarily discussed. The interviews followed a semi-structured interview process.
The interviews were carried out in an open-ended style with certain key principles developed in different
interviews depending on the discipline of the interview. The ”framework” questions used to generally
guide the interviews are available in the appendix A.5.4. The interviews were then published in podcast
format for ease of access and to allow future research and analysis to be freely carried out (Colclough
2023).
The primary purpose of the interviews was to develop and explore the design factors for the tool and to de-
velop the questions used in the survey to weigh the design categories. The secondary use of the interviews
was for further analysis of the NHN super hospital project (the current design, the project management,
and the design teams’ concepts on flexibility). This was done in order to mimic the design team’s input
for the tool and give an accurate view of the concepts and motivations behind the design of the NHN su-
per hospital in relation to its flexibility. Thirdly, the interviews aimed to enhance the comprehension of
flexibility concepts while expanding the repository of experiences and information used in this report’s
analysis.
The interviews were held online, via Microsoft Teams (microsoft-teams n.d.), or in person. Recordings
of the interviews were then uploaded to Spotify (spotify n.d.) for publication on their podcast service.
The Audacity (audacity n.d.) audio editing software was used to edit the interview and to add episode
introductions in line with a standard podcasting form.
5.3
Survey
As discussed above, data for this report was collected using a survey. The survey, an advertisement poster
used to acquire the responses of the survey, and the survey’s results can be seen in Appendix A (A.6.5). This
present section will discuss the process involved in carrying out the survey. After the survey was generated
the engagement with respondents was achieved by the use of a poster. To ensure these respondents repre-
sented a range of actors within building usage, the location of these posters was carefully considered. The
Flexible Design Analysis
46
==PAGE:60==
survey was promoted in multiple locations, each deliberately chosen to represent a diverse pool of stake-
holders within the Architecture, Engineering, and Construction (AEC) industry. These locations included
the site offices of the NHN super hospital construction site, the Technical University of Denmark, the
adapted community of Christiana (Dale, Supervisor, and Langlais 2011), and the social construction com-
munity Markverket (Makvärket 2013). To mitigate Social Desirability Bias, the responses to the survey
were collected online and anonymously (Social Desirability Bias refers to respondents providing socially
acceptable responses rather than truthful answers).
The survey was hosted on the SurveyMonkey platform (survey monkey n.d.), and the results were col-
lected and analyzed using the platform’s system software. These results were instrumental in determining
the weightings of different design factors within the DATEx tool and in gaining insight into a broader
audience’s perception of the importance of various design features.
The survey questions contained a range of response types, as explained below, including close numeric
responses, a Likert Scale approach, and open-ended questions to gather quantitative data and a gauge of
the respondents’ attitudes and opinions as outlined in Colosi, 2006( (Colosi 2006)). The close numeric
response question type involves providing respondents with predefined numeric options to select as their
answer, such as rating on a scale from 1 to 5, thus, providing exact numerical values. This approach
allows for quantifiable and precise data collection. The Likert Scale approach involves presenting a series
of statements or items and asking respondents to indicate their level of agreement or disagreement on a
predefined scale, typically ranging from strongly agree to strongly disagree. The Likert Scale provides a
way to measure the intensity or strength of respondents’ attitudes or opinions. The open-ended questions
were used to broaden the understanding of the area of flexibility in buildings, as it is conceived by certain
actors involved in the interaction with structures, and to assess the respondent’s mentality in regard to this
field.
As flexibility is a concept specific to geographical, temporal, and social contexts, it is important to gauge
the perception of flexibility at the time of, and in the location of, the development of the tool in this thesis
with references from a range of actors from the society the tool, was developed and used in. It is important
to note that these results should not be utilized outside of Denmark or after a significant amount of time
since the publication of this thesis.
5.4
Mathematical Models
As discussed, the theoretical concepts of AHP and MPT were implemented in the analysis of the flexibility
of a given building and to give a numeric evaluation of the current (ethereal) concept of flexibility.
The AHP model was used to calculate the weighing, mean, standard deviation, and ratios of the different
design factors in different future scenarios in Excel using the DATEx Excel file. The implementation of
the AHP model is discussed in the next section. MPT, used primarily as an economic model, had to be
altered for the purpose of this report. It is hypothesized in this thesis that a design characteristic/factor
represents an asset and a specific design solution (made up of design factors varying in importance and
energy) represents a portfolio of these assets or design characteristics.
In standard MPT, it is hypothesized that the returns (which in this case represent the flexibility score),
either of a specific design factor or a whole design solution, are the mean result of the ”historic” returns
(in classical MPT theory). In the case of the analysis of the flexibility of structures, there is, at present,
no historical data. Therefore, for the current use-case of MPT, the mean returns were calculated from the
flexibility scores of the design solution, or design characteristics, across three different possible future
scenarios. The Monti-Carlo method was therefore used to generate multiple outcomes of this data set
and to establish a probable mean of the returns if more data had been collected and assessed. Through
further case studies, and over time, it may be possible to augment this alteration in classical MPT theory
by supplying historical data on the flexibility score of different design factors across different solutions.
Flexible Design Analysis
47
==PAGE:61==
Using this augmentation the global minimum variance portfolio was found, representing the design solution
with the most return (best flexibility score to the least risk). This ”best” design solution was compared to
the flexibility score of the current design to generate a final, aggregated, flexibility index for the structure.
5.5
BIM models
Building information modelling (BIM) was used extensively to analyse and test the flexibility of a building
within the tool and in the case study developed in this thesis.
BIM is a digital approach that streamlines the management of building design, construction, and operation.
It integrates various project elements like geometry, spatial data, quantities, and component details. BIM
supports data exchange between different software tools, such as REVIT, using standardized formats like
Industry Foundation Classes (IFC). It enables stakeholders to visualize, simulate, and analyze building
performance, enhancing project efficiency, quality, and risk management. This is outlined in the Technical
University of Denmark’s course (McGinley, Vejlgaard, and Danet 2021).
The BIM software of REVIT and Dalux were used to gain information for the specific case study outlined
in this report. Furthermore, the DAT tool itself incorporates the addition of an ifc-integrated Python script.
For the DAT tool to function, it is essential that an ifc file of the building being analysed be placed inside
the ”models” folder of the tool. Although the DATEx Excel file used to calculate the AHP gives the option
to insert building data straight into it, it is more convenient, streamlined and adaptable to incorporate a
Python code that can automatically assess the model of the building. As such generating ifc files from
BIM models is required to use the flexibility analysis tool developed in this thesis. This is delved into in
detail in chapter 6.
5.6
Developing a tool
The theories and techniques discussed above lead to the development of a tool to aid in the early design
process of building by allowing the flexibility of its design to be assessed. It aims to promote a deeper con-
sideration of the factors of flexibility and create accountability for the perception of their implementation.
This tool is discussed in detail in chapter 6.
5.7
Case study
After the assessment of design requirements and analyses of the current concept of flexibility and the
amalgamation of mathematical models to numerically assess the value of certain design factors in regard
to flexibility, a case study was carried out on the NHN Supper Hospital to test and refine the tool. This
case study is presented in chapter 7.
Flexible Design Analysis
48
==PAGE:62==
6
DAT - Design Analyse Tool
6.1
Tool outline
This section describes the user interface (UI), organisation and operation (using Excel and Python) of the
design Analyst’s tool developed in this report. The DAT files and folders are supplied in the technical
appendix, Appendix B. A visual representation of the tool is also supplied in Appendix A.1.
Two specific programs are used to run the tool, Excel and Python. The Excel file is the primary tool and
interface and is referred to as ”Design Analysis Tool Excel” (DATEX). The Python part of the tool is
referred to as the ”Flexibility Design Analysis Tool” (FDAT). When referring to the tool as a whole the
DAT, definition is used. Though DATEx can also be seen as the full tool as it is designed to produce results
independently of the Python program, though the Python program adds significant relevance to the results.
The tool produces different assessment outputs, the seminal output being the ”flexibility index score”.
A single score ranking the flexibility of the building being assessed. This score, along with a flexibility
report, generated by following the best practice framework, are the outcome of the tool.
The tool is developed to be used in the early design phase by a specialised flexibility analyst as part of
a larger design team, though it was developed to be open-sourced and flexible in its function and use, as
outlined below.
6.2
Organisation
Figure 6.1: File Tree
The DAT tool outlined in this report was structured using a standard computer file directory structure, see
figure 6.1. It comprises a parent folder (DA_Tool) as the ”root directory” or ”main directory”. Within this
file is a read-me document (Readme.txt), containing instructions for the functionality, use and editing of
the tool. The Python script (FDAT.ipyd) designed to read an Excel file, generate Excel sheets, and run
MPT models is also inside this parent folder. An input folder ( DA\Tool\input), containing the DATEx
Excel file, acts as a user interface and calculation program. An IFC file of a model of the building being
assessed is located in a model sub-folder of the parent folder (DA_Tool\model\modelA.ifc). There is also
a sub-folder containing test and reference IFC files within the models’ sub-folder to allow for incremental
improvements of the tool and any bugs to be assessed using successive successful models (See Figure 6.2).
Flexible Design Analysis
49
==PAGE:63==
Figure 6.2: Model folder
The third and final sub-folder is an output folder (NDA_Tool\output) in which an edited version of the
DATEx Excel tool will be produced from the Python file.
The system of use for the tool is, therefore, first to engage with the Readme.txt document, input the relevant
IFC model, then input data in the ”DATEx” Excel file and then run the ”FDAT” Python script before
returning to the ”DATEx” Excel file for the final results.
6.3
Development of Characteristics
The development of a hybrid of numerical and analytical approaches, MPT and AHP, was used for the
creation of a flexibility index tool. The first stage was to use the literary review and interviews presented
earlier in this thesis to compile a list of ten design factors related to flexible design in the building. The
nine factors were:
• Cost
• Ownership
• Lifecycleplan
• Maintenance
• Society
• Technical
• Structural
• Aesthetic
• Space
• Digitization.
The survey was then used in conjunction with the interviews to assign backend weightings of importance
to these ten design characteristics to allow for the use of AHP to develop a rank of these characteristics.
This ranking was implemented in both the backend and input sheets of the Excel file as discussed in the
next sections of this chapter.
6.4
Weighting
The weighting was developed analytically from the research carried out as part of this study as outlined
in chapter 8 and chapter 9. Three weightings were estimated for this thesis, discipline, building class and
scenario factor weightings. The development of detailed weighting systems ultimately lay outside the
scope of this thesis, however, the capacity to argue and improve on these weights is inherent in the tool.
The tool outlines a possible weight distribution for one discipline and one building class, with a secondary
building class being developed out of the author’s best knowledge to allow for further testing of the tool
on a randomised sample for comparison with the case study results. The assignment of weights to these
categories is discussed below.

6.4.3
Scenario factor weighting
Table 6.3 and table 6.4 outline the weighting assigned as a discount factor for the different possible future
scenarios. The scoring system rates from 01. to 2, with 0.1 indicating the design characteristics are less
important in the future scenario, 1 indicating the design characteristics is as important in the future scenario
as in the current scenario and 2 indicating the design characteristic is more important in the future scenario
than in the current scenario. The factor weighting of the two future scenarios of a negative change to the
buildings’ use/environment and a positive change to the buildings’ use/environment are outlined here. The
no-change scenario is not discussed here as it has a scenario factor of 1 for all characteristics, as it assumes
the future scenario is the same as the current design scenario.

Table 6.4: positive scenario factor weighting
The assignment of these weightings is considered to be subject to adjustment of future user input or further
developed using more advanced probability theory. The logic behind the current weighting is as follows.
For the negative change
The cost was assigned a 0.1 as it is taken that if the future scenario puts more load on the characteristics
of the building, then it will be more expensive to fix in the future, so the initial cost or budget constraints
have less relevance as it will be mitigated by future renovation requirements.
Ownership receives 2 as if the use case puts additional loads on the building design characteristics then
the ability for action with clear control and implementation is important to remedy the negative aspects of
the scenario.
Lifecycleplan receives a 2 as being prepared for multiple eventualities and considering future needs may
mitigate the effect of a negative scenario.
Flexible Design Analysis
52
==PAGE:66==
Maintenance receives a 1 as it is expected that if the different layers of the buildings are fully functional
they can better adapt to changing needs, however, if an element of the buildings is deemed obsolete or needs
to be adapted due to the negative scenario, then maintenance of it may have been a waste of resources and
energy. This indicates maintenance is as important as in the original design scenario.
Society receives a 2 as in a negative future scenario a high social integration is likely to extend the lifespan
of the building in the case of reduced functionality (An extension of lifespan is seen as a metric of flexibility,
as the longer the building services the more opportunity for changing use it encounters).
Technical receives a 2 as an adaptable MEP design able to change to meet the requirements of different
situations is intrinsic to the continued use, and new uses, of a building in a negative future scenario (as
outlined in Chapter 7.
Structural receives a 1 as the majority of the black box model See figure 3.6) and Ballingers (Ballinger
2019a) assessment of flexibility incorporates structural spacing. Structure is seen by many as the key to
flexibility, as such it is equally weighted for all scenarios.
need to adapt space as a layer of change is highly likely to be incorporated in all future scenarios and as
such this characteristic is as important for a negative future scenario as it is for the current scenario.
Aesthetic receives a 0.1 as in a negative future scenario good aesthetics are likely not enough to maintain
the relevance of a poorly performing structure over the long run. It is taken that aesthetic features elevate
the life span of a well-functioning building over less well-received buildings of similar construction and
function.
Space receives a 1 as it is as important in all future scenarios given the high probability of the change of
the space layer within 15 years Brand 1994 regardless of the future scenario.
Digitisation receives a 2 as in a negative future scenario the need to analyse the effect of the excessive
loads on the design of the building and the ability to assess the original aspects and later changes to the
building is crucial to allowing for and aiding in implementing the required changes.
For the positive change scenario weighing
Cost receives a two as in a scenario where there is less load on the design than expected or originally
designed for the likelihood of additional funds spent on adaptable solutions may lack additional benefits
as the planned flexibility solutions may not be realised. This is exemplified in the movable walls of the
new Dutch hospital designs as discussed by Ole Kongsbakand and Mette Stockholm of the NHN client
team in the podcast series accompanying this thesis Colclough 2023.
The cost was assigned a 0.1 as it is taken that if the future scenario puts more load on the characteristics
of the building, then it will be more expensive to fix in the future, so the initial cost or budget constraints
have less relevance as it will be mitigated by future renovation requirements.
Ownership receives 0.1 as if the requirements for the function of the building operate under less load in a
positive future scenario less action will need to be taken and the ownership model could engage less with
the building. It would also be easier to transfer ownership or alter ownership models for a building that is
performing better than expected.
Lifecycle plan receives a 1 as in a beneficial scenario the building’s lifecycle is taken to be as important
as its original design, given that the end of life and second life/reuse of the building is still of importance
in a positive scenario.
Maintenance receives a 1 as though with less respective loading on the different elements of the building
design the need for maintenance could be less, a thorough and regular check of the building function will
still add to the overall lifespan of the building.
Society receives a 0.1 as though social integration can be seen to increase the lifespan of a building in
Flexible Design Analysis
53
==PAGE:67==
all cases it is seen to have proportional less of an effect for a building functioning above its designed
requirements.
Technical receives a 1 as an adaptable MEP design able to change to meet the requirements of different
situations is as important for a change in use or occupancy of the building as it is for the initial design.
Structural receives a 1 as the majority of the black box model See figure 3.6) and Ballingers (Ballinger
2019a) assessment of flexibility incorporates structural spacing. Structure is seen by many as the key to
flexibility, as such it is equally weighted for all scenarios.
Aesthetic receives a 2 as in a scenario in which the design life of the structure can be seen to be extended
by positive environmental and use factors, the aesthetics of the building become more predominant as a
factor of flexibility as these features will be dated to and by a verity of different concepts over this extended
lifespan. Good design principles are a core aspect of good aesthetics and good design should, ultimately,
allow for change.
Space receives a 1 as it is as important in all future scenarios given the high probability of the change of
the space layer within 15 years Brand 1994 regardless of the future scenario.
Digitisation receives a 0,1 as in a positive future scenario the need to analyse the structure is less prevalent
and the likelihood of major changes is less. Though the end of life or second life of the structure still
requires good documentation to allow for effective implementation of the building flexibility.
6.4.4
Disclaimer
It is important to note that this weighting is indicative and a fuller investigation of the weighting of these
categories should be implemented for industry use of this tool. As much work on weighting has been done
by Langballe 2021 and Robert Schmidt 2016 this element of the tool was covered for completeness only
and is not a key aspect of the sim of this report, the investigation and ratification of a weighting method,
not the exact weighting of design characteristics is the final takeaway as a main element of the tool is the
ability for it to be adapted to different concepts and allow for the subjective nature of design, making the
tool itself adaptable. The framework as it stands is developed as a guide and to allow the concept to be
tested and refined.
The implementation of these weightings into the Excel file is explained in the following ”Excel file -
DATEx” section of this chapter.

sheets, one backed calculation sheet and eight sheets outlining the conclusions drawn from the backend
process. These sheets are:
• Instructions

• Input
• Analytical hierarchy

• Backend
• Monti Carol - sensitivity
• Results
• Results - current state

• Benefit
• SB
• Returns

The following section will outline their logic and sequence. (N.B. The figures in this and the following
sections represent the tool in different states of development and different versions of the tools use, they
are to exemplify the layout and use of the tool only.)

figure6.6. It is intended to incite the development of the concepts of the analyst in a unified manner and
generate evidence to support the design team’s claims of flexibility.
