6.7
Scenario matrix
The 2nd sheet of the Excel file is the Scenario matrix sheet. This sheet prompts the user to find the most
probable future scenario with the highest impact. This matrix goes on to influence the next sheet, ”Input” as
it is used to develop the probability of future scenarios and is intrinsic to further user inputs. The example
below, in figure 6.3, comes from the NHN case study in the next chapter of this report.
Figure 6.3: Scenario Matrix
The user must input nine possible future scenarios under the scenario headings and rate them from 1-10
based on the probability the event will take place and the impact of the event should it take place. A
probability score of 1 represents a scenario which is not likely and a probability score of 10 represents
a future scenario which is extremely likely. An impact score of 1 represents a scenario with negligible
impact on the future use of the building and an impact score of 1 represents a scenario with high impact
on the future use of the building. The scenarios are then ranked by probability and impact in a matrix and
the scenario with the highest ratio is then chosen for further analysis.
6.8
Input
The 3rd sheet was designed for the input of the user and comprises of five input classes, see figure 6.4.
Figure 6.4: Sheet two: Input
The relevant sections or input classes of the input sheet were chosen based on similar tools, developed in
the area of flexible analysis, and current literature in the field (See Chapter 3). These input classes include:
Step 1: Discipline, Step 2: building class, Step 3:Factors importance, Step 4: Solution ranking, and future
scenario probability.
6.8.1
Step 1: Discipline
The first class, defined as ”discipline” (see figure 6.5, was chosen to represent the interests of the different
actors likely to interact with the tool and the design of a structure. It includes the selectable options:
Flexible Design Analysis
57
==PAGE:71==
Structural, Architectural, Energy, Heating, Lighting, Sustainability, Indoor climate, and Client.
Though the tool is intended for use by a specialised flexibility analyst, it is also possible that it might be
used by a specific actor, or to analyse a specific area of a building, rather than the whole building, in regards
to its flexibility, such as in the case study in this thesis outline in chapter 7.
The discipline class frames highlights the areas it makes sense to analyse, it is the setting you are rating for
flexibility. It is linked to the scenario matrix. The area of analysis and considered scenario matrix should
be reflected in the discipline chosen, and vice versa.
Figure 6.5: Step 1: Discipline
The discipline class automatically assigns different weighting factors to the ten design characteristics in
the back-end calculations. As such selecting two disciplines augments the factor by which the design
characteristics are assessed.
6.8.2
Step 2: Building class
The second input class of ”Building class”, represents the different major building topography (see figure
6.6). They are Residential, Commercial, Office, and Industrial.
Figure 6.6: Step 2: Building Class
The building class represents the magnitude of the setting you expect the flexibility to be in. It affects the
expectations of the building during use and the criteria the building is designed to, for instance in regards
to the weight the floors are expected to hold or the energy rating that is needed.
Flexible Design Analysis
58
==PAGE:72==
The current tool and case study are set up for a single Building disciple analysis, however with further aug-
mentation two or more classes could be selected, representing a second-life scenario, or dual-use buildings.
Selecting two disciplines would require nine or more analyses as the building would need to be evaluated
for the three possible future scenarios presented in three combinations (each individual building class and
a combination of the two). This also highlights that whether the design is expected to go from one state to
the other, or if the design is expected to be utilised under both building classes, must be considered if the
tool is adapted to analyse multiple building classes.
6.8.3
Step 3: Factors Importance
The third input of ”Factors Importance” is for the user of the tool to rank the importance of the ten design
factors, discussed in Chapter 4, on a scale of low, medium or high. This represents the importance of
the design characteristics to the design team in regard to their general concept of flexibility. This was
developed to assess the design team or flexibility analysts’ personal concept of building design and give a
degree of influence to the weighting of the ten design categories.
This input class was seen as pivotal as the relative importance of these factors is subjective, as outlined in
much of the reviewed material, and ultimately the designer’s own concept of flexibility is what will shape
the future of the building through the realisation of their efforts.
Figure 6.7: Step 3: Factors Importance
The weighting of this process could involve a more in-depth AHP approach than the one utilised in the
DATEx tool, comparing each of the ten design characteristics to each other, for instance, cost to ownership,
cost to life-cycle etc. The drop-down option of low, medium or high stops any specific design characteristic
from being rated as completely unimportant. It is the relative impact that is of concern. In the case that
every design characteristic is rated with the same score then the effect of this class is mute.
6.8.4
Step 4: Solution ranking
The user also has the facility to input their perceived ranking for the specific design solution of the building
being analysed for the ten design characteristics. This is done on a scale of 1-5, with 1 indicating the
design characteristic is poorly represented in the implementation of the design, and 5 indicating the design
characteristic is highly influential in the design.
Flexible Design Analysis
59
==PAGE:73==
Figure 6.8: Step 4: Solution Ranking
To assign this weighting the analyst needs to know the design brief and context of the structure. It is a
brief overview of how the design solution of the building is rated based on building industry standards.
It is this element of the analysis which requires the most effort for the analyst as it requires evidence of
meeting or exceeding relative building standards. The initial Python evaluation comprises of an IFC model
check, as discussed further on, which automatically analyses elements of the building to assess whether
the building is compliant with, or exceeds certain standards, for instance, if the opening-to-floor area ratio
of the spaces of the building would allow for ventilation by passive means. If the current IFC assessment
library does not contain the relevant factors for analysis then a detailed, discipline-specific report must
be attached to the Flexibility Index analysis report to highlight the justification for the solution ranking
scoring in relation to the flexibility of the building.
The concept of this input is to highlight any discrepancy between the design team’s view of flexibility and
the design solutions’ compliance with the required factors to allow for such flexibility, and then further to
prompt a deeper investigation of whether the claims of flexibility can be substantiated within the design.
A full report or automated analysis should be carried out for each of the ten design characteristics in line
with the best practice framework developed in this report (see figure 1.6). The exact industry standards
that the building is assessed against should be edited depending on the standards of the political region in
which the tool is being used.
6.8.5
Future scenario probability
The final input in the UI is the ”future scenario probability” where the user is prompted to choose three
possible futures, one with negative connotations for the design, one where the situation and environment
of the design remain unchanged and a future scenario in which the environment and situation the building
is utilised in are more favourable for the design solution. These future scenarios are broad and unrefined
and can thereby be easily tailored to a variety of different situations. The user is prompted to input the
probability of each scenario, as a percentage, following classical probability theory ( See figure 6.9).
Figure 6.9: Future scenario probablity
Flexible Design Analysis
60
==PAGE:74==
This section is directly related to the scenario matrix sheet. It is assumed the scenario with the highest
impact and highest probability ratio has been selected. The purpose of this input is to then numerically
assess the probability of the conceived future scenario and to then calculate the effect that a different or
inverse future scenario would have on the design solution, thus analysing the true flexibility of the structure
across possible futures. It also serves as a rough sensitivity analysis as it outlines how the building would
perform given different weightings of the ten design characteristics.
The level of accuracy of this input depends on the probabilistic modelling undertaken by the analyst or
design team to calculate the probability of future scenarios.
This is the final input for the user/analyst however it is linked to backed scenario weightings which should
be checked and verified for the given scenarios, as discussed further on in the back-end calculations section.
6.9
AHP
The 4th sheet gives an overview of the analytical hierarchy process used to weight the ten design char-
acteristics, thus allowing the designer/analyst to edit the weighting while following the initial logic and
implementation practice if required.
Disclaimer This sheet is outlined as part of the tool as an idealised conceptual component but not imple-
mented in the current version of the tools analysis system.
6.10
Pedigree matrix
The 5th sheet in the Excel file is a pedigree matrix tool that allows users to rank the data used for their
assessment and analyze the reliability of both the data, their rationale, and the final results of the tool (See
figure 6.10).
Figure 6.10: Pedegre matrix
This matrix is not included in the weighting of the flexibility index but should be presented with the results
of the tool in a flexibility index report, following the process outlined in the flexibility framework (figure
1.6) in order to provide a valid and reliable result.
The specific data points relevant to the analyses in the relevant accuracy field should be highlighted by
the analyst with grey and the overall pedigree matrix score should be assessed. A score of 5 represents
accurate and trustworthy data, and a score of 1 represents vague and generalised data.
The specific categories of the pedigree matrix are:
• Reliability: The source of the data and its robustness
• Completeness: How full or detailed the data sets are
Flexible Design Analysis
61
==PAGE:75==
• Temporal: The age of the data and time of collection
• Geographic: How specific the data is to the area the analysis is taking place in
• Technological correlation: How common the elements being assessed are, if the data relates to more
theoretical or more practical services or elements.
6.11
IFC elements
The 6th to 8th sheets represent specific inputs from the design of the building. The default elements are
set as ”ifcSlab” ”ifcOpenings” and ”ifcspace”. The data input for these sheets is the columns of ”Name”
and ”Area”. They assess the number of different types of IFC elements and their size for the given cate-
gories in the sheet names. These pages allow the Excel file to be used in a reduced capacity, without the
accompanying Python script and are directly related to the analysis of the solution ranking input class of
the input sheet.
6.12
Backend calculations
The 9th sheet comprises the backend calculations for the tool and is editable by the user, though it is not
intended, by design, to be augmented. The relevant weightings of the different classes, the calculation
procedure, and the process of this page are outlined below (see Figure 6.11).
N.B This, and following images, shows an alternative weighting scheme as that used in the case study.
This different augmentation is explained in chapter 9 and the images here are to outline the process of the
tool only.
Figure 6.11: Back end weighting overview
The backed weighting is developed based on the basic concept of equation equation 6.12
Total Score = (input 1×input 2×input 3×input 4)×((Importance Factor 1×Data 1)+(Importance Factor 2×Data 2)+. . .
+ (Importance Factor n ×Data n))
(6.1)
This is elaborated on in Appendix A.3.3.
Flexible Design Analysis
62
==PAGE:76==
6.12.1
Discipline class weighting
The first weighting is for the discipline class. It is set as 1 or 0 depending on which discipline is chosen
by the analyst. This class is important as further weighting is based on which discipline the design is
focused on. This is because it is taken that the different disciples have different priorities and concepts of
importance when it comes to flexibility in design.
Figure 6.12: discipline class weighting
6.12.2
Building class weighting
The user input of ”building class” also gives a backed weighting score of 0 or 1 in regards to which classi-
fication is chosen by the user. This factor is of a higher influence as more than one building classification
could be chosen for a single structure.
Figure 6.13: Building class weighting
these factors’ scores are then combined to assess their impact on the design characteristic weighting (See
Figure 6.14).
6.12.3
Discipline and building class weighting
The next factor weighting comprises multiplication applied to each design characteristic for each discipline
and building class. This backed weighting form, 1-10, has been compiled from the literature, interviews,
and questionnaires. It is a subjective look at the importance of the different characteristics for the different
aspects of the project but is a key part of the assessment of the structure and of the flexibility of the building.
The alternative weighting system presented in figure 6.14, figure 6.13 and figure 6.12 than that discussed
in the weihgtin section are included to highlight the subjectivity of these values.
From these weights, a new combined factor weighting is calculated by multiplying the values of the disciple
with the disciple factors, and by multiplying the building class with the building class factors. If multi-
ple disciplines or classes are selected, their multiple scores are added together to form the final numeric
importance factor.
Flexible Design Analysis
63
==PAGE:77==
Figure 6.14: Combined weighting
6.12.4
Factor of importance weighting
Next, the factor of importance is weighted as 1 for low, 2 for medium, and 3 for high. This weighting
is indicative and currently does not have a scientific method to discern the score’s relevance, except the
logical hierarchy of the terms used to assess the building (see Figure 6.15).
Figure 6.15: Factor of importance weighting
Flexible Design Analysis
64
==PAGE:78==
6.12.5
Solution ranking weighting
Similarly, the specific design solution being assessed is weighted by the user from 1-5. If the design solu-
tion exceeds the requirements of the best practices of the aspect being assessed, the design characteristic
receives a score of 5. If the relevant standards and best practices of the aspect being assessed are not met,
the design characteristic receives a score of 1. This is based on a logical progression (See figure 6.16).
The solution ranking is generated from the design team’s assessment of the design and the score given
to that design characteristic should be justified by appending a relevant report for each characteristic to
the flexibility report. It is also possible to generate these design Solution ranking weights by analysing
the building in question using an IFC analysts script in python, as discussed further on and outlined in
the appendix A.2. This weighting is generated from a standards check of the IFC model for the specific
case being assessed (For the case study Example outlined in this thesis the floor area to opening area ratio
for a given layout is assessed, as outlined in Chapter 7). The automatically assessed design characteristic
solution ranking is outlined in grey in figure 6.16 below.
Figure 6.16: Solution Ranking
6.12.6
User weighting
The user input classes ”factors importance” and ”solution ranking” are then multiplied by the disciple
and building class aggregated score, giving a matrix of values for each design characteristic based on the
discipline being assessed. The values for each design characteristic across each discipline are then added
together to give a total weight for each design characteristic.
Flexible Design Analysis
65
==PAGE:79==
Figure 6.17: User weighting
6.12.7
Current scenario weighting
The augmented weighting is then multiplied by the probability of each future scenario to give a weighted
probability score. An adjusted average of these scores is then compiled to remove outliers and normalize
the valuation. This also increases compliance with the assumptions of MPT.
The relative impact of each design factor is then found by dividing the weighted probability by the adjusted
average. The relative impact presents a view of how much each design factor contributes to the overall
design solution after removing outliers and normalizing the data. This aids in understanding which design
factors are most critical for the flexibility of the design solution (see figure 6.18.
Figure 6.18: Current Scenario calculations
Flexible Design Analysis
66
==PAGE:80==
Data normalization can be achieved using the formula:
Normalized Value =
X −Xmin
Xmax −Xmin
where:
Normalized Value is the value after normalization,
X is the original data value,
Xmin is the minimum value in the dataset,
Xmax is the maximum value in the dataset.
This formula scales the data to a range between 0 and 1 based on the minimum and maximum values in
the dataset. The normalised score is then multiplied by 100 to allow for better analysis of smaller numeric
differences.
6.12.8
Future scenario weighting
This score is then multiplied by a scenario factor, developed from the expected utility of each design factor
in the future scenario considered. This scenario factor ranges from 0.1 to 2. A score of 1 represents the
design characteristic in the considered future scenario having the same importance as in the original design
scenario. A score of 0.1 represents that the design characteristic is totally undesirable in the future scenario
compared with the original design scenario. A score of 2 represents that the specific design characteristic
has far more utility in the considered future scenario than in the present design scenario.
Figure 6.19: Negative Scenario calculations
Figure 6.20: No change Scenario calculations
Flexible Design Analysis
67
==PAGE:81==
Figure 6.21: positive Scenario calculations
The resulting score from the multiplication of the scenario factor with the adjusted average is defined
as the ”benefit” of the design characteristic and used as a representation of the reward that the design
characteristic generates in the specific future scenarios considered.
The average (mew\µ) of the weights of the design characteristics for each scenario is generated and used
as a return/reward metric for the design in that future scenario. The standard deviation from this mean is
also generated representing the risk of the design characteristics of the specific structure analysed in that
future scenario (see figure 6.22).
The µ generated from this process is taken as the preliminary ”Flexibility Index” or ”Flexibility Score”.
This is a weighted numeric assessment of the combination of design characteristics for the specific structure
being assessed. It is discounted by the probability of the future scenarios the design may function in and
the utility of each characteristic in that scenario.
6.12.9
Preliminary flexibility score
Lastly, a ratio is generated to indicate the amount of ”reward” each unit of risk generates by dividing the
standard deviation by the mean. This shows the desirability of the given design solution for a given future
scenario and is taken to serve the same purpose as the sharps ratio in standard MPT. This ratio is defined
as the versatility ratio. For example, an average ratio could be considered a ratio of 0.5, which indicates
that the scenario’s reward is generating 0.5 units of excess reward for each unit of risk taken. A ratio of 1
or higher can be considered good, and a ratio of 2-3 could be considered excellent (as outlined in MPT).
Figure 6.22: Preliminary Flexibility Index
6.13
Python analysis in brief
This is an explanation of the backend logic and calculations of the tool, which will not be necessary for all
users to follow but is explained here for clarity and to allow argumentation of the tool by future developers
with new takes on the concept, and practice, of analysing flexibility.
After the user inputs their case-specific data in the input sheet and the IFC model sheets of the Excel file
Flexible Design Analysis
68
==PAGE:82==
(as well as placing the IFC model itself in the appropriate folder), they are directed to save the Excel sheet
and run the FDAT Python script.
The Python tool is explained in detail below 6.22 and in the appendix (see A.2) but for consistency, the
basics are outlined here.
The first section takes IFC data from the supplied model for analysis similar to the basic IFC input tab in
the DATEx file. The Python MPT model produces a global mean-variance (GMV) portfolio representing
the design solution with the best mixture of design features to maximise the return of the design while
minimising its risk. An efficient frontier, of possible design solution returns compared to the risks of those
possible design solutions, is also generated.
After the Python program has been run the user should return to the DATEx file to analyse the results of
the tool fully as the additional Excel sheets in the DATEx tool (9 - 16) present the results of the tool. These
results are categorised in the following section.
6.14
Monti Carlo
The 10th sheet of the DATEx file runs a Monte Carol simulation (see figure 6.23), comparing the GMV
design, generated by the Python script and discussed in the Python subsection below, and the initial flex-
ibility score from the DATEx program. From this analysis, an estimated probability of flexibility for the
design is generated and can be used as a numeric comparison between designs or structures and as an
indication of the accuracy of the predicted flexibility index score.
Figure 6.23: Monti Carlo
Future improvements of the tool could include this process being run for each design characteristic after
Flexible Design Analysis
69
==PAGE:83==
the weighting process rather than on the final mean score.
6.15
Results sheet
The 11th sheet is the ”results tab”. This displays the benefit of each design characteristic to the flexibility
of the structure (given the possible future scenarios outlined by the user). This sheet outlines the effects
of the design solution in relation to the probability of the future situation the building will face and can be
used to gauge the variance of the characteristics. It is a reprint of the ”Benefit %” column in the Back end
sheet of DATEx, which is calculated by percentiling the ’Benefits’ column.
6.16
Results including - current state
The 12th sheet is the ”results including - current state” sheet, which repeats the process of the results sheet
but with the inclusion of the current scenario results. The ”No change” and the ”current state” scenarios
give identical importance to each factor and so the current state is emitted in further analysis. It is included
here simply as a progressive check and to allow future analysts to augment the tool with more ease.
6.17
Scenarios MSV
The 13th sheet presents the ”Scenarios MSV” (Means, standard deviation and versatility ratio), as calcu-
lated in the backend sheet.
The mean or ”design reward” can be seen as a rating of the future reward of the specific design choices
in a specific scenario. The mean shows the flexibility index score of the design for the possible scenarios
considered. The unit is the ”flexibility score” measured in ”Effective Flexibility” (EF). It is theorised that
this score could be further used as a factor to rate cost savings in terms of energy, environmental impact,
time, or another relevant metric if a logical relationship was developed.
The standard deviation from this mean or ”design risk” shows the possible variation from this reward and
can be used to measure the uncertainty of the design’s rewards.
The versatility ratio is the relationship between the risk and reward for the given scenarios as previously
discussed.
6.18
Benefit
The 14th sheet (”Benefit”) outlines the benefit of the design characteristics in the given scenarios and
is a direct presentation of the ”Benefits” columns from the backend sheet. The benefit of the design is
displayed as the factors effective flexibility scores before conversion to percentiles. This can also be seen
as how efficient a design solution is in a given scenario. The more efficient the design solution in the
new scenario, the more flexible its original design. A higher efficiency score indicates a more robust and
future-proof design in regard to the specific factor of flexibility.
6.19
SB
The 15th sheet (”SB”) is a combined table of the ”Scenario MSV” and the ”Benefit” sheets. It is displayed
to allow analyses of the predominant factors of the design solutions risk and reward in a single takeoff.
6.20
Returns
The 16th sheet is the ”Returns” sheet. The data in this sheet represents the mean of the benefit ( effective
flexibility scores displayed in ’EF’ units) of each design factor across the different scenarios, giving an
overall view of the return of each design characteristic when taking the probability of the scenarios into
account. It is an imaginary or idealised representation of the utility of the energy put into each factor.
The scores here can be seen as representations of the distribution of effort that should be put into each
factor for the design of a building which will continue to meet demands and give the benefits of its service
accounting for the vicissitudes of time, that is to say, ”flexible”.
Flexible Design Analysis
70
==PAGE:84==
6.21
Flexibility score
The 17th, and final, sheet shows the flexibility score (see figure 6.24). This is the final output of the
tool and is taken as the penultimate flexibility index for the structure. The final aggregated flexibility
score is calculated from the relationship between the GMV design solution, calculated in Python, and
the mean flexibility score of the design solution (calculated as the mean of the combined scenario design
characteristics scores, as presented in the ”Returns” tab). The aggregated flexibility score is calculated
by dividing the GMV score by the mean flexibility score. This aggregated flexibility score (AFS) shows
proportionally how far from the ”best” design, in regards to flexibility and design energy breakdown, the
current design is. It reflects the flexibility of the finished structure given a set of possible future scenarios.
The closer the score is to 1 the more flexible a structure is.
Figure 6.24: Flexibility Score
6.22
Python
6.22.1
What is Python
Python is a programming language that offers a wide range of libraries and frameworks which facilitate sci-
entific computing, data analysis, machine learning, and other research applications. Its extensive standard
Flexible Design Analysis
71
==PAGE:85==
library, along with the vast ecosystem of third-party packages, provides an abundance of tools for vari-
ous tasks with efficient numerical computing libraries like NumPy and SciPy, powerful data manipulation
tools such as pandas, and advanced machine learning frameworks.
6.22.2
What Python was used for
For this study, Python was used as a primary program to code and run the mathematical tools and IFC
analyser that comprises the core of the flexible index design analysis tool that is the primary focus of this
report. A basic level of knowledge in the use of Python is required to use the tool developed in this thesis.
The ability to run MPT calculations at high speeds and to incorporate many different functions is the main
reason for Python utilisation. It also supplies a user interface and the ability to process and ”print” results
throughout the analysis, which can be used for specific take-offs and as functionality checks.
The Python script is integrated with Excel as Excel provides a more visual user interface and a more widely
accessible format for the tool’s utilization. It is entirely possible to build and run the tool’s functions using
just Excel or Python, but for the purpose of this thesis, an integrated solution was developed to maximize
the tool’s potential by utilizing the beneficial aspects of both programs.
Excel is used for user inputs and visualisation of the categories and their back end relationships, whereas
Python is used for more advanced mathematical processes and graphic generation.
6.22.3
Breakdown of Python script used
The exact script, used as the basis of the analysis tool, can be found in the appendix, A.2 but an overview
is supplied here for clarity and further explanation. The Python script in relation to this thesis starts by
importing the relevant libraries. It utilises a number of libraries, but primarily, it works from IFCopenshell
(to analyse the IFC model), pandas, and numpy (for the mathematical modelling).
IFC analyses
The first section takes IFC data from the supplied model ( DA_Tool\model\modelA.ifc ) for analysis.
This analysis contains specific scripts to import data about specific IFC elements from the building model.
As such this section of code must be augmented for the analysis of different building and design categories.
Currently, it is set to analyse a structure under the ”technologies” category discussed in Chapter 4. This
analysis is carried out by checking the ratios of the area of a zone, or slab, to the openings of that zone,
indicating the capacity for passive ventilation. This is further discussed in the case study (chapter 7) of
this report.
It is important, as stated in the ”Readme document” supplied with the tool, to ensure that all files and
folders are named correctly and in the right file path. Further, any alterations to sheet names or data in the
DATEx file should be cross-checked with the Python script to maintain continuity.
Once the slabs, spaces, and openings have been imported and assessed, they are then printed to the Excel
file to be incorporated into the weighting and used as a check for the competency of the tool user and the
homogeneity of the tool user’s goals and actions. This printing is done to allow the Excel file to be utilized
without the MPT script if needed. However, the same operations are carried out within the Python script.
The ”results” (The benefit of each design factor), ”Scenario MSV” (Mean, Standard deviation and ver-
satility ratio, which shows the flexibility index score of the design for possible scenarios), and ”SB” (A
combined table of the ”Scenario MSV” and ”Benefits” sheets) sheets are then imported from the DATEx
file. These data sets are then displayed in a table for clarity and to cross-check the data (The ”Returns”
sheet is also analysed later in the script).
Data frames and variables are then defined from this data and assessed using MPT. The data is then printed
after categorisation into risk, reward, and ratio sets. This serves as a first appraisal of the flexibility of the
current design using the Python platform.
Flexible Design Analysis
72
==PAGE:86==
The next section of the script generates an efficiency frontier. This is a concept used in portfolio optimi-
sation to determine the optimal allocation of assets that maximises the expected return for a given level
of risk or minimises the risk for a given level of return. A point on the curve of the efficiency frontier
represents a portfolio that offers the highest possible return for a given level of risk, or the lowest possible
risk for a given level of return. The GMV portfolio is computed and taken as the ideal mix of assets (design
factors) in a specific portfolio (Design solution).
Correlation matrix
The first step in developing this efficiency frontier is computing a correlation matrix (see figure 6.25) to
assess the diversification benefits of combining assets with different correlations. By including assets that
have low or negative correlations, a portfolio can potentially reduce overall risk without sacrificing returns.
The correlation matrix helps in constructing efficient portfolios by considering the covariance structure of
asset returns and selecting assets that have desirable correlations with each other. This allows an optimal
balance between risk and returns to be achieved and the construction of portfolios that are efficient in terms
of achieving the desired objectives.
Figure 6.25: Correlation Matrix
The next section of the code computes the covariance matrix.
Covariance matrix
The covariance matrix (see figure 6.26) is a tool for analysing the relationships between multiple variables.
It provides information about the co-movements and dependencies among different variables by quanti-
fying their joint variability. The covariance matrix allows us to understand how changes in one variable
correspond to changes in another variable, indicating whether they move together or in opposite directions.
By examining the elements of the covariance matrix, the diversification benefits of combining different
assets can be assessed.
The covariance matrix provides information about the direction and magnitude of linear relationships be-
tween variables, while the correlation matrix additionally standardizes the values to provide a measure of
the strength and direction of relationships that can be directly compared across different datasets.
Flexible Design Analysis
73
==PAGE:87==
Figure 6.26: Covariance Matrix
The covariance matrix is used in collaboration with the correlation matrix as a correlation matrix is needed,
in the context of an efficiency frontier, for developing the ”best” design mix. The covariance matrix pro-
vides information about the relationships between different design characteristics given different possible
future scenarios. The correlation matrix measures the strength and direction of the linear relationship be-
tween the variables, in this case, the returns of different design characteristics in different scenarios. By
analysing the correlation matrix, designers can understand how different design factors move in relation
to each other and how their returns are likely to be correlated for possible future scenarios.
This data is useful as we assume that diversification reduces the risk of any distribution of design charac-
teristics, as discussed earlier in chapter 4.
Once we have developed a relationship between the data, then portfolios with different distributions of
design characteristics can be developed following MPT.
Risk-free rate
A risk-free rate must be input as the first part of the MPT process. This risk-free rate refers to the the-
oretical return of a design that carries no risk. It is considered the baseline or benchmark for assessing
the attractiveness of other designs. The risk-free rate is assumed to be zero to simplify calculations and
isolate the risk premium associated with other designs. By assuming a risk-free rate of zero, the focus
shifts to evaluating the excess returns generated by riskier assets above and beyond the risk-free return.
This simplification allows for a clearer analysis of risk and return relationships. However, in practice, the
risk-free rate is not always zero. It can vary. In situations where the risk-free rate is not zero, it reflects
the compensation a designer receives for taking on no risk, it implies that the designer can earn a return
without incurring any significant risk.
Therefore, the decision to change the assumed risk-free rate depends on the specific context and purpose
of the analysis. In real-world applications, it is essential to use an appropriate risk-free rate that aligns
with the prevailing conditions and reflects the opportunity cost of investing in a risk-free asset. This may
involve considering short-term solutions, government legislation, or other suitable proxies for the risk-free
rate based on the first used horizon and the specific structure being analyzed.
An example of a risk-free design factor would be the inclusion of solar panels that are entirely covered
by a government grant and which do not impose on aesthetic, structural, or space considerations, and are
easily removable.
Design solutions/portfolios
The data from the ”Returns” sheet is used for the MPT calculations from here on out in the Python code
as it represents the benefit of each design factor to the flexibility of the structure, given the possible future
scenarios as outlined by the user. This data outlines the effect of the design in relation to the probability of
the future situation the structure will face and can be used to gauge the contribution of each factor across
all possibilities. The mathematical principles of this process are outlined in the appendix A.3.
After the MPT calculations are carried out using the Python script, the GMV portfolios’ design factors
Flexible Design Analysis
74
==PAGE:88==
weighting, risk and return are printed through other design solutions besides the GMV can be taken as the
metric from the MPT process. This is because the ”success” of a design solution depends on the level of
risk the designer is willing to take and the reward they are aiming to achieve. A range of ”optimal” solutions
can be found along the efficiency frontier. The efficiency frontier is a graph that shows all possible design
solutions that maximize expected return for a given level of risk (standard deviation of return) or minimize
risk for a given level of expected return. Efficient design solutions are those that are plotted along the
curve of the efficient frontier. They represent portfolios that achieve the best trade-off between risk and
return, given the available set of assets.
Figure 6.27: Efficiency Frontier
One noteworthy portfolio is the tangent portfolio, which is also calculated in the MPT process. This
represents an optimal portfolio with the highest risk-adjusted return (the portfolio that offers the highest
return for a specific level of risk). It is constructed by combining a risk-free design characteristic with a
portfolio of risky design characteristics in such a way that it lies on the efficient frontier at the point where
the capital market line (CML) intersects the efficiency frontier. The CML is a graphical representation that
depicts the relationship between risk and expected return for efficient portfolios. It shows a straight line
connecting the risk-free rate of return with a portfolio of risky assets on the efficiency frontier. The CML
provides a framework to evaluate the risk-return trade-off and determine an optimal portfolio allocation by
considering both a risk-free design characteristic and the risky design characteristics available. Portfolios
with more risk for potentially higher returns are associated with design solutions that lie to the right of the
Tangent Portfolio on the efficient frontier. The tangent portfolio is included in the tool to allow designers
to gauge different design solutions (portfolios) with different flexibility scores based on their needs but is
not presented as a result for the sake of clarity. Though the tangent portfolio has the best ”risk to return”
trade-off, the minimum possible risk is seen as more beneficial for the purpose of this thesis. It should be
noted that any design solution on the efficiency frontier could potentially be used in lieu of the GMV as
an ”optimal” portfolio, depending on the analyser’s risk tolerance, however, the GMV is recommended
and outlined here as it represents the lowest risk. This is seen as optimal due to the massive influence of
unknown elements to the analyses.
Flexible Design Analysis
75
==PAGE:89==
6.23
Efficiency frontier
The next step of the FDAT program is to print a graph of the efficiency frontier which is plotted as a visual
representation of the results along with a table of the weighting of each design characteristic for the GMV
and tangent portfolios. The portfolio’s risk, return and ratio are then also presented.
Figure 6.28: Design solution weights
The efficiency frontier graph represents the curve of portfolios with the optimal risk for a given return and
vice versa, highlighted on the graph are both the GMV, the Tangent portfolios and portfolios comprising
of single design characteristics.
Figure 6.29: Portfolio returns
Figure 6.30: Portfolio risk
The return (mean), risk (standard deviation) and ratio (The benefit attributed to the unit of risk) of the
GMV are the primary results of the Python code and are then taken and input into Excel to generate a final
Flexibility index from a comparison between the current designs risk and return with the GMV portfolios
risk and return. (see figure 6.27)
6.24
Integration
The amalgamation of the results of the Excel and Python scripts produces a single Flexibility index which
can then be compared with different design solutions at the early design phase to improve the possibility
of flexibility for the structure or to compare different structures to rate their worth in regard to flexibility

7
NHN Case Study
Figure 7.1: NHN (regionh 2020)
7.1
Context
The New Hospital North Zealand (NHN) super hospital located in Hillerød, Denmark, was chosen as
the case study for this thesis as a test case for the development of a flexible design index rating tool as
it represents a structure in the construction phase which has been designed to be highly flexible. The
healthcare industry as a whole represents a fast-changing industry requiring ”state of the future” technol-
ogy to cater for services and uses, which do not exist yet. These unknowns need to be planned into the
structure.(Arkitekten 2014). Designing a structure which utilizes ever-advancing technology and needs to
adapt to circumstances such as pandemics, ageing populations, and advances in our understanding of bi-
ology and health means that the structure must be flexible. This is heightened by the 16-year construction
process expected, from concept to completion, and the resulting expected 100-year lifespan of the finished
structure.
The specific study of the interchangeability of the bed-wards and office spaces, with the focus point of
indoor climate, was then chosen as a narrower area within the super-system of the hospital. The inter-
changeability of these spaces was chosen as it was a key point in the design concepts of the hospital, in
regards to flexibility. User experience satisfaction is also cited as a primary goal of the NHN project.
The hospitals’ operating capacity, budget and expected returns are based on the improved patient turnover
resulting from ”healing environments”. This is discussed in detail in this chapter.
The data for this case study is based on interviews, available for review on the Transforming Flexible
Adaptability Podcast (Colclough 2023), case-specific literature supplied by the client team responsible for
managing the construction of NHN project, as well as publicly published sources. Where possible the
relevant sources have been supplied in the appendix A Such as an extract form (regionh 2018).
Flexible Design Analysis
77
==PAGE:91==
7.1.1
Background
The current NHN super hospital will take over from three other hospitals but will have an inpatient capacity
of half of its predecessors due to its revolutionary design. This design strategy reduces patient recovery
time through holistic solutions, emphasizing the impact of the hospital’s environment on recovery. The
design improves ownership models and cooperation, with new system operations that allow for shared
facilities and reduced, interchangeable resources. It also enhances user flow with innovative zone strategies
and layouts. As a result, NHN will treat more patients with higher outpatient turnover, and better inpatient
flow.
”When completed, the hospital is expected to produce savings of DKK160 million a year compared with
the present budget.” (Arkitekten 2014) (pg19).
The project was started in 2007 and is now expected to run till 2026, having an estimated cost of 5.8
billion DKK. (regionh 2020). It is part of a wider government initiative ”the Super Hospital Program” to
regenerate the Danish healthcare system. The Center for Ejendomme, Region Hovedstaden (CEJ) manages
the construction of these projects and have supplied most of the data used in this case study.
The structure will be a 120,000 m2 building on a 300,000 m2 site. The hospital will accommodate ap-
proximately 700 beds and serve more than 310,000 people in the region. The fluid site layout 7.8, seen in
its unique clover shape, gives the possibility for direct extension of the structure at ground level. The op-
portunity for extra floors (through an increase in structural loading capacity) is designed in, and modular,
symmetric, spaces utilizing the philosophy of system adaption are used throughout NHN. The second life
of the structure as a hotel has also been considered through these design strategies (Colclough 2023). The
next section on the hospital’s design investigates these strategies in more detail.
Figure 7.2: NHN during phase 1 of Construction (regionh 2020)
7.2
Hospital design
The innovative design of the NHN super hospital consists of many interrelated elements including system
adaptation, structural overcapacity, satellite installations, structural expansion into site, modularization,
technological innovations, aesthetic exceptionalism and second life considerations. These characteristics
are detailed below.
Flexible Design Analysis
78
==PAGE:92==
7.2.1
Flexible by design
”You can never predict what will happen. What we can do as architects is to make a building that’s
ﬂexible, that allows the hospital to adapt as technology and care change. We can try to provide architectural
qualities that are lasting. Not rooted in today’s world, but in certain fundamental experiences that hopefully
transcend time. So even 20 years from now, people will appreciate the relationship of the spaces to the
landscape, the quality of light and the quality of materials.” (Arkitekten 2014) (pg 29).
Many characteristics enable potential flexibility in the NHN hospital design. At a system level, the own-
ership model of the areas and utilities of the hospital have been done away with and a new communal
ownership model has reduced the need for overcapacity designs and surplus supply. This means that as
needs change and develop, the divisions of resources of the hospital can adapt to meet them. The con-
cepts for expansion consist of the possibility to bridge the leaves of the clover with additional structures,
supported by increased structural capacity at these points. Further expansion is idealized in the car park
areas, with smaller, lightweight, satellites supplying increasing needs or specialized services, such as in
the Dutch hospital model outlined in (Hinkema 2023) and the primary structure also has some capacity
to be built out from. A primary concept of flexibility comes from the modularization and homogeneity
of areas of the Hospital, which allow them to be used interchangeably. In the context of Stewart brands
model (Brand 1994) the structure and site layers can flex, adding capacity, and the Space plan and the stuff
layers can flex, with modularity and use, to alow for the future overcapacity.
Figure 7.3: Internal Expansion (regionh 2020)
Flexible Design Analysis
79
==PAGE:93==
(a) Extension into site
(b) Satellite expansions
Figure 7.4: Capacity Expansion (regionh 2020)
(a) plan
(b) Render
Figure 7.5: Structural Capacity extension (regionh 2020)
”What would be the maximum scale for a hospital? SD: Two things: with this brief, the requirements
for proximity are hard to imagine at twice the size, and everything here is geared towards one lobby, one
emergency department … If you doubled the distances, it wouldn’t work. Already in the brief there was
mentioned the possibility that they might not need all the wards, they could become a hotel, or adminis-
trative offices. That kind of ﬂexibility is also within the structure.” (Arkitekten 2014).
Technology and digitization tools also play a part in the future-proofing of NHN’s design. Information
accuracy and handovers are a large part of this. The project management software Dalux generates a
package of data gathered during construction that can then be handed over to the facility management
team to allow for total documentation and maintenance of the building to be carried out seamlessly from
one stage of the structure’s life to the next. Imerso 3D point cloud technology has also been used to capture
accurate ”as built” models of the building as it is completed, allowing for total transparency of composition
and improved maintenance potential. These technologies are discussed in detail further on in this report.
Many other automated solutions designed into the NHN super hospital adding to the robust nature of
the structure, such as initial plans for meals to be delivered to the ”service village” (a separate building
connected by a tunnel to the main hospital designed to supply all manganese requirements, food, cleaning
Flexible Design Analysis
80
==PAGE:94==
etc,) by robots. This has since been cut from the budget but elevators and corridors are dimensioned to
facilitate automated service robots. Automated solutions such as patient check-in and descriptive nurse
call buttons are set to be placed throughout the hospital.
The second life considerations of the structure also add to the flexibility of the design with the concept of
re-use of the building as a hotel after its functional use as a hospital comes to an end. This is accommodated
by the modular units and the adaptable, lightweight timber framed bed-wards, as well as the concept of
lasting materials and aesthetic beauty which would service many alternative facilities needs. An example
of such a building is Hostal dos Reis Católicos, completed in 1511 as a hospital and hostel for pilgrims of
El camino, it is held by many as the oldest hotel in the world 7.6. This example highlights the effect of
aesthetics and the very real possibilities for change of use of monumental structures.
Figure 7.6: Hostal dos Reis Católicos, 1511
7.2.2
Construction method
The materials and techniques used to build the NHN also have inherent flexibility by design. The base of
the structure, comprising of the first two floors, is made of robust poured concrete, supported by pre-cast
reinforced columns at a spacing of 5-6 meters. The crown of the buildings comprises a lightweight. modu-
lar, timber structure, with prefabricated, phased, elements and steel support columns holding prefabricated
concrete slabs.
This disconnection of the structural system from the aesthetics and layout of the building allows for a
separation of layers of change (Brand 1997) and facilitates the difference in the lifespan of these layers
again mirroring the Dutch hospital concepts (Hinkema 2023) of separating hospital buildings into robust
and heavy cores supporting key facilities with lightweight and easily adaptable peripheral structures than
can be added and changed at a far different pace.
Flexible Design Analysis
81
==PAGE:95==
Figure 7.7: Section of hospital structure
Process flow
The flow of occupants has been paramount to the design of the buildings, with the clover shape allowing
easier navigation of the structure and reducing transit time between areas, maintaining flow paths and
essential service physical relationships.
Figure 7.8: NHN flow diagram
Flexible Design Analysis
82
==PAGE:96==
7.3
Purpose
The purpose of this study is to test the Flexibility index and tool outlined in this thesis, to numerically
assess the flexibility score of the design concepts of the New Hospital North Zealand and compare the
outcome with the flexibility scores of other tools.
7.4
Research questions
The research question answered herein is ”What empirical evaluation can be made of the flexibility in the
design of the bed wards and offices at the NHN super hospital, in regards to indoor climate, using the
design evaluation tool developed here”.
7.5
Scope and limitations
The temporal limits of this study pertain to the evaluation of the hospital given its projected operations
at the time of writing, 2023, when the hospital is still unfinished. The geographic limitations concern the
concepts of adaptability, and building standards as understood in Denmark.
This study is primarily for the development of a design analysis tool and all results should be considered
in that regard.
7.6
Case study process
The following steps outline the development of the case study:
• literary research
• Site inspections
• Surveys
• Interviews
• 3d model/ifc analysis and take-offs
• Indoor climate modelling (partial implementation)
• Sustainable ventilation analysis (partial implementation)
• DATe analysis
• Alternative Tool Analyses: Ballinger analysis
7.7
Research
This case study was compiled by an active member of the project management team of the NHN project.
The research comprises qualitative and quantitative data gathered from multiple sources across the project
group, augmented and additional data and information were also generated for this report from external
sources such as (Thuesen 2022).
7.7.1
Data collection and analysis methods
The data collection methods involved included physical site inspections, aided by Imerso 3D point scan-
ning, 3D modelling using REVIT, document analysis using Dalux project management software, Informa-
tion from the client team was gathered by qualitative questionnaires (Appendix A.6.5) and a quantitative
interview process (Colclough 2023) as well as through first-hand site observations.
Data from the NHN super hospital is compiled in the Dalux project management (Dalux 2023) application,
which was utilised to document the building and generate detailed reports, photographs and plans that are
readily available to all stakeholders. From this data, the hospital’s layout could be assessed and a laser
cut system model at 1000th scale was made to allow for perspective assessments of the hospital’s layout
Flexible Design Analysis
83
==PAGE:97==
and the location of areas and services. Relevant measurements for assessment, such as room volume and
window size were also gathered from Dalux.
3D point cloud imagery (figure 2.2a) was also analysed to assess the internal structures of the hospital and
relevant technical installations. From this data, and the surveys from the client team, data points for each
principal performance indicator (PPI) were established and the flexibility analysis tool developed in this
thesis (Chapter 6) was then used to provide an outline of the flexibility index of the hospital bed wards.

7.7.4
Modeling
The primary modelling method for the data gathering was REVIT takeoffs to analyse the quantity and
dimensions of the proposed spaces, as well as integrate with the DATex tool. This was supplemented by a
Dalux model and both laser cut and three-D printed layout prototyping.
7.8
Area of analyses
The area of analysis was specifically the bed wards and the office spaces and how the indoor climate
fluctuated given three different possible future scenarios.
The indoor climate and ventilation considerations are highlighted here as a background and justification for
the weighting metric and scoring used in the DATEx tool. The physical data of the spaces are directly input
into the tool but a description of both the bedwards and offices are supplied to outline the flexibility and as
a base for their evaluation. The bed-wards and offices comprise of similar geometry. The special aspect
of their construction is the scope of their flexibility. The similarities and their potential interchangeability
can be seen through the modelling methods used for the dimensions of these spaces, as outlined below.
Flexible Design Analysis
84
==PAGE:98==
7.8.1
Bed wards
The bed wards are 17.54 m2 and the internal area of 16.89 m2. The ensuites have an area of 5.8 m2 and
an internal area of 5.07 m2. The bed ward is 3.141 meters high and 3.617 meters long with a width of
4.578 meters, internally. The ensuites have a height of 2.99 meters internally, with a length of 3.189 meters
and a width of 2.336 meters There are 700 bed-wards in the hospital, each designed as single occupancy.
Additional bed-wards for outpatients are located on levels 0 and 1. These are not considered in this report
as their use is multinational.
There are two external openings, one window and one door, and one internal opening in the space (not
including the opening to the ensuite which is a closed space). The window dimensions of the space are
a height of 2 meters and a width of 1.7 meters, giving an area of 3.4 m2. The external door opening for
this space is a height of 2.45 meters and a width of 1 meter, giving an area of 2.45 m2. The door opening
leading to the internal space has a height of 2.1 meters and a width of 1.61 meters giving an area of 3.38
m2. This gives a total external opening area of 5.85 m2 and a total internal opening area of 3.38 m2.
The occupancy was assumed, in the current design scenario, to be three, a mix of a patient, a nurse, and a
doctor or visitor.

7.8.2
Offices
The offices are dimensioned with an area of 79.34 m2, a height of 3.141 meters, a width of 15.35 meters, a
length of 14.219 meters and a volume of 249.18594 m3. They are designed to cater for 26 office workers.
There is a total of eight office spaces. Four office spaces on each of the bed-ward levels, level 2 and level
3.
The offices situated on the bed ward floor are dimensioned to exactly that of four-bed wards (not including
bathrooms and operational spaces, but with the additional flow area of the corridor now incorporated into
the treatment area). This means that positioning extra needed beds in these areas would be relatively easy
to enable. As the strategic placement of ”field” offices in the bed wards is fundamental to the flow of the
building and the function of the staff, the possibility of retrofitting temporary walls is not considered here
and the offices are modelled ”as is”.
In the case of adaption to bed wards, the assumed scenario would change the occupancy of these spaces
to 12 patients, 6 doctors and 6 nurses/visitors, with a total occupancy of 24 people, less than the current
design capacity. These assumptions are based on the number of beds that could reasonably be assumed to
fit into the office space.
There are 6 windows, 3 external doors and three internal doors (not including the closed storage space).
The window dimensions of the space are a height of 2 meters and a width of 1.7 meters giving an area of
3.4 m2 each. The external door opening for this space has a height of 2.45 meters and a width of 1 meter
giving an area of 2.45 m2 each. The two larger door openings leading to the internal space have a height
of 2.1 meters and a width of 1.8 meters giving an area of 3.78 m2 each. The smaller internal door opening
in the space has a height of 2.1 meters and a width of 1 meter giving an area of 2.1 m2. The total external
opening area of 27.75 m2 and the total internal opening area of 9.66 m2.

7.8.3
Indoor climate considerations
Research on the effects of changing indoor climate is presented here to highlight the importance of this
design metric and to facilitate the assumptions made later in this report.
Indoor climate’s impact on the flexibility of a building can be linked to many factors, not least of which
is the capacity of the inhabitants. 90% of a commercial building’s costs can be attributed to staff 7.16,
with indoor climate affecting the productivity of those staff up to 8%. Indoor climate can also produce 8%
better recovery time for patients. (see figure7.17) (Korsholm et al. 2022).
Figure 7.16: Effect of indoor air quality (Korsholm et al. 2022)
Flexible Design Analysis
88
==PAGE:102==
Figure 7.17: Indoor Air Quality’s Effect on Cognitive Performance (Korsholm et al. 2022)
A key factor in indoor environmental conditions is ventilation. This can be a means of both controlling
the temperature and air quality simultaneously, two of the four fields to consider when apprising an envi-
ronment. The fields are; thermal, Acoustic, Air and light quality, Collectively known as TAIL 7.18.
Figure 7.18: Environmental Components: TAIL (Korsholm et al. 2022)
Flexible Design Analysis
89
==PAGE:103==
Thus for the considerations of this assessment, the ventilation rate available to the selected spaces will be
assessed, focusing on Air quality as a result of ventilation.
7.8.4
Ventilation considerations
Ventilation rates required for adequate indoor climates are listed in the Dansk Standards (standard 2019)
as 7l/s pr person 7.19, or 0.42 L/s/m2, With the general good design principle to design to the higher
ventilation rate of the two.
Figure 7.19: Ventilation and performance
During the modelling process the ventilation rates for the different areas were calculated and augmented
for the alternative scenarios, this is outlined later in the text.
Once the ventilation rates for the area are calculated there are many options to supply the needed rate. The
main categories being mechanical and passive ventilation. It is generally accepted that passive ventilation
is adequate for ventilation requirements, and recommended as the primary design method (Hinkema 2023).
however, due to overheating and a lack of consistency of use (effort to open and close windows), as well as
unwillingness to utilise natural ventilation during colder seasons (due to the energy/heat loss this entails),
mechanical ventilation is often preferred and though it has an associated energy cost, the energy savings
from heat exchangers and efficiency of use mean that this method is often preferred, as outlined in the
technical university of Denmark course (Hviid, K. M. Smith, and Li 2022) and on the podcast series
accompanying this Thesis (Colclough 2023).
Nevertheless, as MEP services can have a lifespan of anywhere from 5 -25 years (Brand 1997) this case
study will focus on natural ventilation as it is a more lasting design feature and so more flexible, as part of
the structure, rather than the services building layer (Brand 1994). Additionally, passive design solutions
are one of the ”black box” model solutions (figure 3.6) and a core characteristics of flexibility according
to (Robert Schmidt 2016) (Figure 4.4). Further consideration of the scenarios of ”pandemic” style shifts
in use, and the reinforced concrete structure of the slabs of the building mean that it would be difficult
Flexible Design Analysis
90
==PAGE:104==
to implement additional mechanical ventilation, at least on a holistic level, though single-space solutions
would be possible, a more likely scenario is the use of passive ventilation.
The exact ventilation achieved by passive means requires in-depth analysis and depends on several factors,
including topography, building height, thermal environment and driving force (Heiselberg n.d.). For this
assessment, the below assumptions are taken to be sufficient for the initial design evaluation of the passive
or ”natural” ventilation of the structure. If considering the structure under the requirements of air change
rates per hour we see that the typical air change rate per hour of 0.67 for single-sided passive ventilation
(windows or exterior doors on one side) or 1 for passive cross-ventilated spaces (windows or exterior doors
on two sides) (Toolbox 2023). The required air change rate by the building standard is 0.5 air changes an
hour (Social and Agency 2023) (standard 2019). Highlighting, that, subjectively, the required ventilation
rates can be achieved with passive means. A passive ventilation rate of 0.5l/s/m2 window area can be
assumed via building leakage (Hviid, K. M. Smith, and Li 2022) and an acceptable ventilation rate for an
opening-to-area ratio, as taken from chapter 4 of the International Building Code (IBC). (Council 2023) is
considered appropriate.
”Habitable rooms must provide openings that total no less than 4% of the floor area of the room being
ventilated.”
Estimating window airflow capacity (ventilation rate) in L/s/m2 for a specific window depends on various
factors, including the type of window, its design, and local conditions. Estimates for operable windows
can vary from between 5 L/s/m2 to 20 L/s/m2 (Hviid, K. M. Smith, and Li 2022). For this study the lower
end of the scale, 5 L/s/m2 is assumed to allow for a margin of safety.
Using these assumptions the natural ventilation capacity of the bedwards and offices can be assessed in
their current design scenario.
For the Bedwards, with a combined area of room and en-suit of 21.96 m2, 4% of the floor area of the room
is 0.8784m2, far exceeded by the external opening area of 5.85 m2.
When assuming a ventilation rate of 5 L/s/m2window/doorarea and taking an external window/door area
of 5.85 m2 m2, we can see there is a potential ventilation rate of 29.25 l/s from the available openings in
the Bedwards. With building leakage accounting for an excess 0.5l/s/m2x5.85m2 = 2.925l/ this brings
the total passive ventilation to 32.18 l/s.
With a ventilation rate p/p required of 3x7l/s = 21l/s and an area-based ventilation requirement of
0.42L/s/m2x21.96 = 9.22L/s, we can see that both the area-based requirements and the occupancy
ventilation rate for the current scenario are sufficient.
For the office space, 4% of the floor area of the room is 3.17 m2, far exceeded by the external opening
area of 27.75 m2.
When assuming a ventilation rate of 5 L/s/m2window/doorarea and taking an external window/door area
of 27.75 m2, we can see there is a potential ventilation rate of 138.75 l/s from the available openings in the
office spaces. With building leakage accounting for an excess 0.5l/s/m2x27.75m2 = 13.88l/ this brings
the total passive ventilation to 152.63 l/s.
With a ventilation rate p/p required of 26x7l/s = 182l/s and an area-based ventilation requirement of
0.42L/s/m2x79.34m2 = 33.32L/s, we can see that though the area based requirement is far exceeded
but the occupancy ventilation rate for the current scenario is not sufficient.
To utilise these considerations in the analysis of the building 3D modeling of the spaces was essential.
Revit was a key tool in the modelling of the spaces and quantity take-offs. The Revit model generated for
this case study, as described in the next section, was also utilised to generated automated ifc takeoffs for
the DAT analyst process. This ifc takeoff gauged the weighting of the building under the indoor climate
Flexible Design Analysis
91
==PAGE:105==
considerations listed above and scored the structure appropriately for the Solution Ranking weighting input,
as discussed in the Analyses section and the Weightings section of this case study.

7.9
The Analyses tool
This section outlines the analyses of the Bed-wards with respect to indoor climate (and more broadly on
the principles of the design of the entire hospital) under the nine categories of flexibility as outlined in this
thesis. The results of the analysis are briefly presented here, with further exploration of these results, their
meaning and the process of evaluation are outlined in chapters 5, 8 and 9 of this thesis.
Flexible Design Analysis
92
==PAGE:106==
Once the metrics, assumptions and data mentioned above were assembled it could be utilized to analyse
the potential flexibility of the structure using the DAT tool developed in this thesis.
Besides project-specific data the scenarios possible future scenarios had to be developed and assessed in
relation to their probability.
7.9.1
Scenarios
From the scenario matrix sheet the most probable future scenario with the highest impact was found to
be the increase of occupancy scenario with the utilisation of modular spaces as the building flexibility
response (Figure 6.3).
The nine scenarios that where considered where:
• Flooding due to climate change.
• Increase temperature due to climate change.
• Civil unrest or war scenario
• Pandemic or famine scenario
• Change of use Second life - use as a hotel”
• Increased occupancy (Satalites)
• Increased occupancy (modular spaces)
• Increased occupansy (extention into courtyards)
• Increased occupancy (extention of base strucutre)
Once the choice for Increased occupancy (modular spaces) was chosen, as outlined later on in this chapter,
this future scenario, and its alternatives where further developed. As discussed earlier, they this impact
category was further defined as Bed-ward to office adaptability and the case of indoor climate was chosen
as a further refinement of the metric. The three variations of the future scenario applied to the offices and
Bed-wards are defined as Scenario A, Scenario B and Scenario C as described below.
Scenario A
The indoor climate was assessed for the hospital bed-wards given an occupancy of three people. This was
assumed to be the current design scenario, with the inhabitants consisting of a mix of a patient, a nurse, a
doctor and a visitor. The offices are taken to have a full capacity of 26 occupants in this scenario.
Scenario B
The indoor climate was assessed given a decrease in the inhabitants of the bed-ward, as this is the model
predicted as most probable by the NHN client team (Arkitekten 2014) 7.22. The evaluation has been made
for one person, assuming automation of patient care via remote monitoring and robotic treatment, or, due
to a reduction in beds needed, re-assignment as office or storage space, giving an occupancy of 1 person -
a single office worker.
The offices are taken to have a full capacity of 26 occupants in this scenario, though remote working may
reduce this figure it is assumed the hospital will maintain its staffing numbers.
Flexible Design Analysis
93
==PAGE:107==
Figure 7.22: NHN predicted bed occupancy (Arkitekten 2014)
Scenario C The indoor climate is assessed given an increase in occupancy, in the event of a pandemic,
war, environmental natural disaster (flooding/fires) or other unforeseen eventuality 7.23. In this case, the
occupancy is seen to increase to 6, that of two patients, two nurses, a doctor and a visitor for the bed wards.
A capacity of 24 occupants for the adapted office spaces is assumed as discussed above.
Figure 7.23: COVID bed occupancy (METRICS and EVALUATION 2023)
From the interviews at NHN (Colclough 2023) it seems that the use of bed-wards as rest areas for the
workers in the hospital is a reasonable assumption as adequate recreational spaces have not been provided,
and from the occupancy flow models, it is clear that removing ”field offices” from the bed-wards would
hinder patient recovery by slow the ability to treat patients more than the addition of beds would benefit
the volume of patients being processed at a given time. This consideration is taken into account in the
weighting valuation of the user input in DATEx.
7.9.2
DAT in action
The theory behind the flexible design analysis tool, DAT, is discussed in depth in the theoretical concepts
section of this report (Chapter 5) and will only be covered here where it aids in the explanation of the
results that were generated.
The designer inputs for the Excel tool were generated from the case-specific literature and interviews with
the client team as collaboration with the actual design team was not possible for this report. The list of
procedures taken to assess the NHN bedwards flexibility in regard to indoor climate is listed below.

Cost was assigned a 5 as the project is currently three hundred million Danish krone over budget leading
to downscaling of the project team. This factor had already defected the initial design of the hospital with
its overall size being reduced on two separate occasions during the design process.
Ownership received a weighting of 4 as the funding, function, facilities and forward planning involved in
the project would not have been possible if it was not a government initiative.
Lifecycleplan received a 1, as, although there were 4 well-developed plans for overcapacity and the pos-
sibility of a variety of second life options, no other scenario probability was planned for, for instance,
increased temperature or severe flooding. This aspect was also considered trivial to the client team, whose
input these scores are attempting to emulate.
Maintenance revered a 3 as the Dalux handover package will allow the facility managers (FM) access to
the full construction and planning data packages and the FM has also had input into the design choices of
the hospital. However, due to the nature of the facility, its size, material choice, access plans and space
allocation, it does not receive a full score.
Society received a 4 as the building serves the public, and will also have open access areas to the public,
such as the inner courtyard gardens, making it highly integrated with the local community. The hospital
also engages in several community outreach programs, such as open site days, tours and regional surveying.
Technical characteristics of the building received a 5, automatically generated from the IFC file, as part of
the indoor climate evaluation, showing that the bedwards could be ventilated by means of passive ventila-
tion for varying occupancy levels.
Structural received a 4 as the dual use of heavy and lightweight structures, over capacity design of selective
areas and use of reduced bulky structural elements, instead utilising well-spaces precast columns allows
for a high level of flexibility.
Aesthetic received a 5 as it is a landmark building with a long design lead time, constructed and developed
by leading architects. Its use of form and to develop function makes it an Exemplary building in terms of
aesthetics.
Flexible Design Analysis
95
==PAGE:109==
Space received a 1 due to the set layout of the facility and inability to adapt spaces to different specialities
in the hospital, as described by Jan Eric Thompson in episode three of the interview podcast associated
with this thesis Colclough 2023
Digitisation received a 4 due to the widespread utilisation of Dalux project management software and
Imerso 3D point cloud analysis.
Preliminary NHN Flexibility score
From this data, a preliminary ”flexibility” score for the structure is generated of 22.24. Further outputs
that were generated are discussed in the following sections.
7.10
Tool Analyses Results
A Monte Carlo sensitivity check and seven additional ”outcome” data sets were generated from this tool
and ultimately an aggregated flexibility score was calculated. These data sets and their analysis are pre-
sented below.
7.10.1
Monti Carlo
A Monti Carlo simulation was run to assess the generated flexibility score. 10000 scenarios with random
data variations were run, resulting in a flexibility score of 22.68, indicating that the preliminary score
(22.24) is reliable.

in relation to the probability of the future situation the structure will face and can be used to gauge the

7.10.3
Scenario MSV
The Scenario MSV (Mean, Standard deviation and versatility) shows the flexibility index score of the
design for possible scenarios.
The mean or ”design reward” can be seen as a rating of the future reward of the design choices, and it is
theorized that it can be used as a factor to rate cost savings in terms of, energy, environmental impact, time,
or another relevant metric.
The standard deviation from this mean or ”design risk” shows the possible variation from this reward, and
can be used to measure the uncertainty of the design rewards.
The versatility ratio is the relationship between the risk and reward for the given scenarios. A ratio of 0.5
indicates that the scenario’s reward is generating 0.5 units of excess reward for each unit of risk taken.
This could be considered an average ratio. a ratio of 1 or higher can be considered good and a ratio of 2-3
excellent.

7.10.6
Returns
Returns: This data represents the mean of the benefit (Efficiency scores displayed in ’EF’ units) of each
design factor across the different scenarios giving an overall view of the return of each factor, taking into
account the probability of the scenarios. It is an imaginary or idealised representation of the utility of the
energy put into each factor and a representation of the distribution of effort that should be put into each
factor for the design of a flexible building which will continue to meet demands and give the benefits of
service accounting for the vicissitudes of time.
