7.10.8
Findings
From the design analysis tool, we can see that the aggregated flexibility score is 68%, with a small standard
deviation of 0.0003.
The results indicate that the most dominant design factor as evaluated by the designer input, is that a
lifecycle plan is the most important design factor for each future scenario. Requiring 53% for a negative
future change, 45% for no future change and 19% for a positive future change, of the design resources to
ensure a flexible building design
From the MSV (mean, Standard deviation, versatility) we can see that the most reward (mean design score)
is attributed to the current design (mu = 28.8), and will maintain if the situation does not change in the
future, with the design being least useful in a positive future change (reduced occupancy)(mu = 6.9). The
standard deviation shows the risk integrated in these results, with the positive scenario having the least
risk,(STD/SIGMA = 9.3) indicating this design reward is the most likely to be accurate. The higher risks
associated with the other design scenarios highlighted the uncertainty of both the future scenarios and the
utility of the design given these scenarios.
The pre-percentile benefits table 7.4 shows a pre-percentile flexibility score, with life cycle being the
predominant design characteristic with a score of 129.8 for the current, negative and unchanged future
scenarios and 12.98 for a positive future scenario. The technical design characteristic is shown to be the
least important in regard to the future utility when considering all future scenarios.
These results are summarised in the Returns tab, showing clearly the mean of the different design factors
across the different design scenarios. with the lifecycle plan receiving a score of 100.6 and technical
receiving a score of 1.72.
The final preliminary flexibility score of 22.24 is then compared with the generated global minimum vari-
ance score of 15.21 to give a final aggregated flexibility score of 0.68 or 68%. Indicating that the building’s
design is above average in relation to its proximity to an optimal flexible design.
7.11
comparison with other models
To assess the accuracy and relevance of the DAT further flexibility analysis has been carried out using the
alternative flexibility tool ”The Balligner rating method” for the whole structure design (Ballinger 2019b).
The Ballinger assessment method is based on a five-scale grading rubric. This rubric is used to assess 5 key
aspects of the adaptability of hospitals, Resiliency, Strategic Zones of flexibility, Fixed vertical elements,
column spacing and Floor-to-floor heights. 7.25
Flexible Design Analysis
98
==PAGE:112==
Figure 7.24: Ballinger flexibility recommendations
(a) Ballinger example 1
(b) Ballinger example 2
Figure 7.25: Ballinger Scoring system
Ballinger has design recommendations for the scoring of these matrices 7.24. In the assessment run as
part of this thesis using the Ballinger assessment model the hospital was found to have an ”above average”
score 7.26.
The hospital was scored as excellent for resiliency, excellent for strategic zones of flexibility, excellent
fixed vertical elements, average for column spacing and below-average floor to floor heights. The full
analysis is supplied in the appendix A.4
Figure 7.26: Ballinger NHN results
Flexible Design Analysis
99
==PAGE:113==
7.11.1
Comparison of the results with literature findings
When comparing the flexibility score of 68% with the Balinger score of ”above average”, we can see
that these scores are complementary. Though the Ballinger score was a whole structure assessment and
not specific to indoor climate, it can be reasonably extended that an adaptable building leads to adaptable
spaces and trickles down to the adaptability of specific factors for specific areas. though a more general,
non-numeric score it is enough to support the concept of flexibility at this stage of the general understanding
and acceptance of those systems which exist to rate it.
7.11.2
Main findings
The NHN super hospital was developed with clear concepts of adaptability in mind. Under analysis, these
features have been realized with a good degree of actualization. The primary concepts of the flexibility of
the structure are theoretically sound,
7.11.3
Limitations
This case study analyzes the flexibility of the project as projected at the time of writing (2023). The
tool used for analysis is intended for early design phase adaptation, which is a limitation of this report as
construction phase two has already begun for the structure.
The pedigree matrix and sensitivity score both indicate the level of information and reliability of the data
is in the lower middle quartile, so there is not a high level of accuracy for these results.
7.12
NHN case study summation
In conclusion, the NHN hospital has a good level of adaptability. It is clear the high level of quality in the
design and long design lead time have led to a robust and well-developed structure. From the analysis and
flexibility score results it is clear that the indoor environment in an adapted office or bedward would be
suitable.
7.12.1
Recommendations
Further research into the attainability of the other proposed adaptation designs for the NHN supper hospital
could be subsequently evaluated and a single, best solution, further developed into a concept design for
use by future facility management teams.
It is recommended for the hospital design and practice, based on this study, not to utilize the offices as bed
wards if possible in over-capacity (negative) future scenarios in the long term, but that this flexibility is a
viable option for short-term surges in inpatients.

Results
8.1
Prelude
This chapter presents the results obtained from the development and implementation of the tool, devel-
oped in this thesis, aimed at evaluating the flexibility of buildings through the utilization of a flexibility
index. The previous chapters have provided an overview of the research objectives, methodology, and
data collection processes involved in this study. Here the outcomes and findings of the previous chapters
are presented.
8.2
Literary review results
The results of the literary review (Chapter3) primarily a general development of the understanding of the
field of Flexible design in buildings. It also served as a review of flexible building characteristics and
examines the core components that render a building flexible. From this the design characteristics used
in this report and tool development were generated, as outlined in chapter 4. These developed design
categories are:
• Cost
• Ownership
• Lifecycleplan
• Maintenance
• Society
• Technical
• Structural
• Aesthetic
• Space
• Digitization
These characteristics are synergistic with the different elements that affect flexibility can be assessed
through the concepts of:
• levels (openbuilding.co 2020)
• layers (Brand 1994)
• typology (Robert Schmidt 2016)
• Strategies (ibid.)
• Characteristics (ibid.)
• tactics (ibid.)
• Solutions (ibid.)
• variables (Ballinger 2019a).
Flexible Design Analysis
101
==PAGE:115==
From this literature, the need to develop an industry-accepted standard definition of flexibility and a frame-
work for a method to assess a building’s level of flexibility became apparent. A design tool is a useful
extension of this process and can outline important aspects that need to be considered in the evaluation of
a building’s flexibility, but ultimately a unified macro process (such as in the LCA process) is far more
important at this stage of the development of the field of flexibility. The DATEx tool developed here can
be seen as a test to develop this macro framework through more base level and mico evaluation of struc-
tures and their subsystems. More in-depth explanations of the concepts that were developed and ideas that
were formulated as a result of the literature review are listed in Appendix A.4.9.

can be found on the podcast ”Transforming flexible adaptability” (Colclough 2023). The interviewees in

• Ole Kongsbak - Head of Construction NHN

8.5
Case study results
From the case study analysis, four predominant areas of flexibility designed into the structure were discov-
ered. The addition of additional lightweight structures on the second and third stories allowed for excess
capacity in structural design. The addition of lightweight satellite structures around the main buildings.
Extension at the end of each arm of the clover share. The final solution for flexibility was in the inter-
changeability of use of offices and patient rooms and it was this option that was chosen to assess with the
flexible design analysis tool as a gauge of the building’s adaptability.
From analysis, it seems that the indoor climate and space would allow for this adaptation however it would
have an adverse effect on the system layout and user flow within the structure, namely the reduced service
areas and ”field offices” for the nurses.
The main outputs of the design analysis tool were that the adaptability of the offices to bed wards received
a flexibility index score of 68%, with a small standard deviation of 0.0003 showing a strong probability
of the accuracy of this evaluation. This is strengthened by the close correlation of the Monti Carlo results
with the flexibility index generated. The area of ”lifecycle plan” was seen as the design factor that requires
the most attention in regards to the flexible design of the structure, with an energy input requirement of
53% (negative scenario), 45% (no change scenario) and 19% (positive scenario). The full results can be
Flexible Design Analysis
103
==PAGE:117==
seen in chapter 7. It was also highlighted that the flexibility index score ( 68%) is in line with the Ballinger
rating for the whole structure (above average).
8.6
Tool results
The tool’s development process and final outcomes can be summarized in a series of steps: user input for
gathering data, including Scenario probability analyses of three scenarios, a negative scenario, a steady-
state scenario and a positive scenario. Assessing data accuracy with a pedigree matrix score, assigning
weights to design and AHP processes, and evaluating flexibility probability through Monte Carlo simu-
lations. The results section highlights the benefits of each design factor for future structural flexibility,
accounting for various user-defined scenarios. The ”Results Current State” provides a reference point by
including the current design state. The ”Scenario MSV” measures flexibility index scores and related met-
rics for different scenarios. The ”Benefit” section quantifies design factors’ efficiency scores, while ”RS”
combines risk and reward analysis. ”Returns” offers an overview of factor returns across scenarios. The
”Flexibility Index” calculates a final score reflecting the current design’s adaptability to future scenarios,
producing actionable insights.
8.7
Flexibility Framework
From four iterations of different Flexibility analysis frameworks, the final proposed system for the analyses
of the flexibility of a structure was compiled as seen in figure 1.6. This is discussed in detail in chapter 9.
Flexible Design Analysis
104
==PAGE:118==
9
Discussion
9.1
Prelude
Through meticulous analysis and interpretation of the obtained results, this chapter aims to shed light on
the effectiveness and reliability of the developed flexibility analysis tool. This will provide a comprehen-
sive understanding of the tool’s performance and its potential implications for future building design and
development.
9.1.1
Context
A key element considered in the development of the DAT is the adaptability of the tool itself. A tool
for assessing flexibility which cannot be easily adapted to many situations and needs could be considered
redundant. The use of open source software and the ability of the end user to have a clear understanding of
the backend calculations and full licence to alter the weightings and the categories of flexibility themselves
is paramount. It is apparent from the literature and numerous other attempts to rate flexibility that exact
definitions, methods and categorisation change regularly between analysts and schools of flexibility. It is,
therefore, a key tenet of this tool that the categories listed can easily be altered and the numeric values
associated with each change, without this alteration adversely affecting the results or concept of the tool
itself. Within the Excel file, it is simple to insert new concepts by replacing the original descriptions in the
sheet. Using the detailed description in chapter 6 it should also be possible for an analyst to re-assign new
weighting to these principles. It is this simplicity and ease of use that allows this tool to be adapted to any
analyst’s need and concepts of flexibility.
The most prevalent factor in adaptability seems to be perception. A building is flexible if we perceive it
to be and this perception comes from our needs and our abilities. For instance, a homeowner will find
more adaptability in his home if he does not have the capital or knowledge to define an alternative vision
of the environment. A developer is far more likely to have exact goals which must be met. There are
commercial incentives to enable high-cost (and destructive) features or alterations to an old or new property
(such as unnecessary demolition or the use of excessive mass concrete), thus limiting its flexibility. It is
therefore not primarily the structure which makes itself adaptable but the actor with the most influence
over it. For instance, the Christiana community (Dale, Supervisor, and Langlais 2011) is versatile not as
a function of its diverse structures but as the inhabitants have ultimate control over the spaces. From the
case study, it is apparent that the NHN super hospital’s flexibility is limited due to significant economic
considerations during construction. Its high cost meant that every cubic meter was utilized from the outset
(not an unfinished structure). The inability to incorporate taller ceiling heights has caused inflexibility in
some of the areas ((Colclough 2023)(Episode 3). Its strict and well-planned vision and dedicated use case,
means that it cannot be, and should not be, designed for multiple purposes. The clearer the vision in our
minds of what is needed the less happy, or accepting we will be with the emerging building. It is theorised
that planned adaptability for a second life of a structure is therefore not as important, as, at the right price,
and with the right situation, it will be adapted for another purpose, whereas its flexibility of first use can
be catered for and see actionable benefits for its inhabitants and initial investors.
It is further proposed that The ”black box” of adaptability should be the basic metric for all buildings’
adaptability, making a clear and easily implemented ideology, as outlined by the Ballinger method. Further
design considerations and tactics could be utilised by the avid designer who wishes to develop a more
lasting solution. However, the added value of secondary characteristics and ”novel” solutions cannot be
categorically rated for their importance for future use, or controlled wholly by the designer. For instance the
effect of the novelty of the surrounding buildings on the aesthetic and cultural importance of the structure.
To elaborate, a square of significant buildings will have a far higher perceived value than a single entity as it
Flexible Design Analysis
105
==PAGE:119==
will serve more purposes, with each structure supporting the longevity of the others. This homogenisation
of a structure and its surroundings, can often not be influenced by a single designer.
9.2
Literary review
The wealth of knowledge and vast quantities of material available in the field of flexibility makes choosing
relevant works a difficult task. This report attempts to analyse some of the seminal works in the field and
outline their core concepts. However, there are theories and schools of adaptability not considered in this
report as well as many methods for analysing flexibility which have not been discussed. The sources in the
Appendix A.8 have been selected for their relevance and as further reading for those interested in continued
study of the subject and represent a fuller, though not complete, representation of the field of flexibility
as a whole. The lack of detailed analysis of all current concepts of flexibility adds to the importance of
an open and fluid discussion of how to categorise and rate flexibility, as well as the ability to adapt the
weighting and categorisation of the DATEx tool.
The black box of adaptable design and the simpler, more implementable, concepts from the current litera-
ture have been observed to have a far more effective engagement with stakeholders and actors. That is not
to say that an in-depth understanding of the field is not needed, but that a surface-level explanation and
guide are more accessible to the general public. The literature, all in all, paints an interesting insight into
flexibility and the detailed components that lead to a building’s longevity and continued use. The many
facets of the theories within the literature have gone towards developing the categorisation of the DATEx
tool and the method for analysing the flexibility of buildings presented in this thesis.
9.3
Survey
The most interesting result from the survey responded for the purpose of this thesis was question 5 as it
supplied the basis for the weighting system used, with the assessed factors’ importance in descending order
of Maintenance 4.33, Lifecycle plan 4.17, Space 3.83, Structure 3.67, Technology and Ownership 3.50,
Society 3.33, Cost 3.00, Aesthetics 2.83, and Digitalization 1.83. Similarly, Question 6 was of secondary
importance for its utilisation in the weighting scheme. Question 5’s responses are in line with the literature.
The score for digitization is of note as a field posed by this thesis and without directly equitable sister
categories in other academic reports. It makes sense that it received the lowest score. However, it is
hypothesized that as the use and knowledge of VDC and BIM’s role in a building’s life cycle becomes
predominant and more obvious, as their temporal implementation increases, this factor will change in
importance.
The responses themselves also require consideration. The number of respondents was lower than hoped
for, standing at 6, and the average response time ( 20 minutes) was higher than expected, with the Survey
Monkey software estimating a 6-minute response time. This could represent a deeper level of consideration
or a fault in the SurveyMonkey estimation/time assessment algorithms. The diverse backgrounds and
nationalities of the respondents help to justify the concept’s weightings as common, though the grouped
age range does make the results generationally biased. Finally, the gender equality in respondents was of
merit representing balanced responses.
The importance of various building characteristics was assessed as a check against the analysed flexibil-
ity indicators to see if respondents rated building design and building adaptability with the same logic.
Digitalization, as it pertains to all categories of building design but a specific subgroup can claim it as
a whole, as presented in the survey, was the only flexibility category not compared to the respondent’s
logical assessment of structures. The comparison of the two results can be seen in Tabel 9.1 below.
This table can then be used to convert the building design aspect weighting to relative weighting in regards
to the flexibility categorisation as seen in table 9.2.
Notable differences were the importance of aesthetics, ownership, and to a lesser degree structure. As the
results are mainly comparable, this response indicates solid consideration of the principles presented by the

respondents. Though a good building design and the flexibility of the structure are not intrinsically linked
in regards to the importance of their characteristics, they are entangled on many levels. It also outlines the
importance of variation in the categorisation presented.
An open analysis of the results of each question response is presented below to highlight the logic that was
developed from the survey process.
The concept of first life as being more important than second use was expected, with the majority of re-
sponses believing structures are used as intended, though this is possibly too vague of a question to harbour
useful results. Additional questions such as what ”intended” meant to the respondents, to what level they
saw this intended use and under which building classes they were considering, may have strengthened
the usefulness of the results of this question. However, we can say that when accepting this as a general
opinion in its current form, the adaptability of use is less of a concern for the majority of building stock
than the flexibility of specific use cases, and second life. Thus strengthening the ideology that second life
is less important than first use.
The idea of renovation over demolition was unanimous. This does not necessarily represent the cost, both
environmentally and financially of renovation, and the value of recycling building components. Again a
vague question which could do with examples, or more case-specific definitions to harbour useful results.
However, it can be seen that this strengthens the case for a flexible design to be implemented into the
concept of a structure.
Flexible Design Analysis
107
==PAGE:121==
The majority of respondents seeing flexibility in structures as a responsibility of society rather than a per-
sonal responsibility leads to the ratification of the open building philosophy and highlights the importance
of regulation in regards to a successful implementation of flexible buildings, without social control and
external motivation the industry will be slow to change.
The high level of engagement of respondents with maintenance shows an understanding of the importance
of continual input into the running of a building. It is posed here that the need for this input to change
and evolve with the inhabitants and the need of society at large is a logical extension of the need for
maintenance.
The idea that light structures are more flexible than heavy structures emphasises the perspective that the
impact of the initial structure, rather than its potential lifecycle impact is preferable to flexibility, leading
to contradicting conclusions from the analysis of previous results. Though renovation is preferred over
demolition, robust structures are seen as less flexible than their counterparts. This does however strengthen
the idea of the the importance of first use, a structure designed to have a lifetime to match its use-case. It
could also be taken that the respondents say lightweight structures as more easily transformed to adapting
needs than heavy structures.
Heavy structures were seen as being ”generally better” highlighting the dissociation between the current
view of flexibility and the concept of an optimal building design. The inclusion of a maintenance caveat
in the optional answers from the respondents shows that the conceived life of the structure could have
played a part in the scoring. The effect on the lifespan of a structure due to its flexibility seems to have not
been a part of the decision-making by the respondents when considering the logic of there responses. The
theories for the benefits of a longer life, heavy structure and a more malleable lightweight construction are
not within the scope of this study, the results of this question are to ascertain the view of the respondents
in regards to the flexibility of a structure and its whole design solution, as such there responses are taken
as generally accepted principles for the development of the DATEx tool.
Traditional and modern buildings were seen as being equally flexible by respondents highlighting the
importance of flexibility in both design and renovation.
The claim of all respondents of knowing how to operate the structure they inhabit is seen as being opti-
mistic, as there is a chance that many areas of a building’s maintenance and operational requirements have
been overlooked (especially specialised components). Further, more detailed questions would be required
to fully ascertain the level of engagement of the users. In any case, this concept allows for the ideal that
an adaptable building could be engaged with by a user and its systems implemented during its life.
The respondents (on average) rated their daily spaces/environments as 48% flexible indicating the level of
unintentional flexibility in most structures, and the potential for flexibility by design. This is strengthened
by the further responses of 66.67% of respondents feeling they could change their daily environment and
66.67% of respondents had adapted space or structure. This indicates a general rating or expectation for
the flexibility of a building.
The initial engagement with a building by inhabitants of altering the space shows that, to some degree, all
spaces are adapted by users from their first interaction
The main factors relating to a user’s continual engagement with a structure came down to light, aesthetics,
maintenance, ownership, cost, layout, heating, community, space and, interestingly, privacy and memories.
The idea that given one’s own space and freedom, a building’s appeal changes, and the concepts of how
we can design buildings to generate positive memories, are outside of this thesis, but of note.
These survey results were used to formulate and justify the theories presented in this report.
Flexible Design Analysis
108
==PAGE:122==
9.4
Interviews
The use of podcasting as a way to preserve and present the interview brought new challenges and learning
to the report. Finding a host to provide an RSS feed, finding and editing software to improve quality and
add to the recordings, and setting up a podcast channel, all required work and research. To further the
life and utility of the podcast a dedicated WordPress plugin and permanent RSS feed would have to be
established to allow for the continuation, and growth, of this base of knowledge, but this was outside the
scope of this project.
The interview gave rise to different design characteristics that are important for the flexibility of a structure
and different strategies developed for the expansion of the NHN super hospital project. The main areas
that came up were the indoor climate and ventilation possibilities, fire zoning, materials and cost. It was
from these interviews that the different design assets were chosen and developed from the tool and the area
of the hospital bed wards, and specifically their indoor climate, were chosen as an area of analysis.
It is highlighted in the interviews that flexibility is still not seen as a cornerstone of design, or simply
inherent in other solutions, with the adaptability of users relayed on more than the optimisation of a flexible
structure. A note-worthy concept was also generated from the conversation with Jan Eric Thompson of the
NHN client team (Episode 3) Colclough 2023 was the possibility of an advertising campaign for building
use and flexibility awareness, much like for energy awareness 50 years ago. These interviews were intrinsic
to the development of both the concepts of this thesis, the DATEx tool and the case study and are discussed
in more detail in appendix A.5.4.
9.5
Framework
The LCA framework has been a large influence on the principles of this report as it seems far more ac-
tionable to develop a framework for the analysis of flexibility than to develop an encompassing tool. This
is due to the inevitable differing of opinions and concepts when it comes to forecasting future events and
deciding on a ”right” way to model and develop a design. Any tool would be liable to competition and
change. An example of this can be seen between REVIT, Blender BIM and Rhinosius modelling software
packages. Though all these design software have different tools and modes of operation they are all still
linked to the same basic logic developed from the lineage of hand drawing that came before. The devel-
opment of such logic and systematic base for future flexibility tools and analyses should therefore be the
first port of call, allowing future developers a fast track to realising their viewpoint and creating an arena
for discussion of differing opinions and ratings.
Figure 9.1: Flexibility Framework initial concept
Flexible Design Analysis
109
==PAGE:123==
The first iteration of the flexibility index took the form of a gateway model (figure 9.1). The first gateway
allows for a design process not considering flexibility through set parameters, but only basic consideration
of the future of the structure, to progress to a final design, the alternative is a set of seven aspects of effects
on the design of a building that can be considered in relation to an improved flexible design. This leads
to an analysis of these factors through an unspecified tool, which could be a basic spreadsheet or a more
complex mechanism like the solution developed in this report. From the results of such a tool, a gateway
is produced to either accept the final design or to re-assess the reliability and sensitivity of the data and go
back into the aspects of the effects appraisal. This initial outline of a framework of assessment leads to
more detailed and specific sequences of actions but still holds merit on its own.
Figure 9.2: Flexibility Framework DTU course
The framework for the development and analysis of the flexibility of a structure as tough as a special
course at DTU (figure 9.2) Kristoffer Negendahl 2023 was then analysed to assess the requirements for an
academic project in this field.
A large part of this theory is based on defining the scope of the analysis with the discipline, technology,
performance factor and scenarios accounting for a large part of the system. This helps to highlight the
importance of narrowing down the field of focus so as not to be lost in the vagary that shrouds the field of
flexibility. It also highlights the need for a single result to be computed, allowing for ease of comparison
and evaluation. The use of parametric models and the named implementation of AHP are also noteworthy
from this model.
The next framework (figure 9.3) developed as part of this thesis was an application of the current the-
ory with a more robust LCA analysis. it highlights the overarching categories of decision modelling,
probability modelling, data gathering and reality capture and current theory and a fluid system for cross-
implementation while highlighting concepts that are currently used in the field.
Flexible Design Analysis
110
==PAGE:124==
Figure 9.3: Flexibility Framework LCA and current theory
The framework version in chapter 1, figure 1.6, of the flexibility matrix was developed as an amalgamation
of the DTU best practices Framework and the LCA framework. It was the base, and method for this thesis
and is seen as a comprehensive system for the analysis of flexible structure with clear goal definitions,
weighting factor analyses, scenario-building strategy, probability comparison and reliability checks. The
inclusion of a case study for testing and comparison is also seen as imperative for any rating, as learned
from this report, a flexibility score in singularity without connection to other comparable examples gives
little credibility to the results or the utility of a more flexible solution. It is this framework that is considered
as best practice in the theory of this thesis.
9.6
DAT - discussing the outcome of the tool
After developing, testing and refining the tool it is possible to draw meaning from its outputs based on a
logical justification of the perceived meaning. However, it must be noted that the requirement for supplying
evidence of the considerations made for different design characteristics and proof of meeting the claimed
levels of flexibility input in the tools solution ranking is as important in the tools effective utilisation as
the final aggregated flexibility score. Another important aspect of the tool is its adaptability exemplified
by the cross-platform and open-source platforms implementation in the tools construction.
The tool produces seven results for analysis, including the final aggregated flexibility index score as well as
a Pedigree matrix and sensitivity/reliability assessment (in the form of a Monte Carlo simulation). How-
ever, the tool lacks a more refined sensitivity analysis of the data at this stage in its development. The
interpretation of the results is based on logical progression and their worth to a designer is down to his or
her own requirements and concepts. The interpretation of these outputs is as follows.
• Results This output gauges the contribution of the design characteristics to the success of the design
in different scenarios and can be used to develop more robust designs.
• Scenario MSV This output can be used as flexibility index scores for different scenarios if it is
desirable to analyse the effect of a design in just one of the scenarios assessed and how much risk is
associated with these scores.
Flexible Design Analysis
111
==PAGE:125==
• Benefit This output outlines how robust and future-proof the design is in a proposed flexibility
unit (FU) or effective flexibility (EF). It allows cross-project comparison of design characteristics’
importance to different projects and their contribution to the building as a whole.
• Returns Outlines an imaginary or idealised representation of the utility of the energy put into each
factor. It has potential as a useful factor to apply to other assessments of cost, such as financial or
environmental impact. On its own, it serves as a guide to the areas of the design that need the most
attention in the early design phase.
• Aggregated flexibility score The final aggregated flexibility score is the metric the building is rated
with. It could be used for comparison across structures given proof of synergistic logic. It is this
rating that outlines the flexibility of the buildings design solution.It is clear that this result is subject
to the designer/analysers’ knowledge and skill, as well as being based on assumptions of weighted
importance for different building classes and actor disciplines. The results would be very differ-
ent depending on the location, time and specific project, however, this is perhaps a strength as the
adaptability of a structure is case-specific.
From this interpretation, it is considered that specialised flexibility analysers should be the end users of the
tool, much as sustainability analysis is now a stand-alone field. These specialists could analyse a structure,
in regards to the interests of different actors and for different building classes, removing some of the effect
of bias from the results.
9.6.1
Mathematical modelling and relationships
The mathematical modelling based on the work of (Langballe 2021) builds relationships between design
characteristics and their effect on a building and its components. These relationships and their verification
are paramount to a useful and implementable tool, however, they are a secondary consideration in this
thesis due to the substantial prior research in this area. As such the development of a common framework
and implementation of MPT was the main focus of this thesis.
9.6.2
Efficiency Frontier/Pareto Front
The MPT data is useful only if we assume that diversification reduces the risk of any distribution of de-
sign characteristics. This usefulness can be argued for however given that any process can be seen as
an economic process, not least in the AEC industry where funding is often the tipping point for the end
result of the majority of projects. This is further strengthened by MPTs proven usefulness in multi-billion
dollar industries such as the New York Stock Exchange. The acceptance of it in financial engineering
and economics tells of a common trust in its functionality and usefulness. The mathematics and basis of
the code, supplied in the appendix A.2 is the subject of much explanation and development and will not
be further investigated here. It is also reasonable to implement MPT here based on the president of the
considerations of a Pareto front in building analysis, outlined fully in (Kristoffer Negendahl and Nielsen
2015). This approach is also developed in many other works on building analysis and decision theory.
This thesis proposes that the Pareto front and the efficiency frontier share a synonymous foundation.
9.6.3
Probability/scenarios
The scenario choice is an element of the tool that can also affect the outcome and the results. If the wrong
scenarios are chosen or improperly assessed as to their probability and the degree of severity that the events
they represent have on the structure then the score loses all relevance. To choose a scenario a probability-
to-importance matrix is used to assess the scenarios, before developing the alternative state scenarios.
This scenario and its alternative states are then assigned a probability ratio. This is a useful element of the
tool, however, a more developed scenario matrix for addressing the most critical outcomes is worth more
in-depth probability scoring and scenario development would be a welcome addition to the tool.
9.6.4
Python
The Python IFC analysis requires each area of analysis to be specifically coded and evaluated. It is the lack
of depth that initially reduces the possibility of implementation of the tool It is proposed that as the tools
Flexible Design Analysis
112
==PAGE:126==
IFC library is developed more of the process could be automated and as such deeper and more meaningful
analysis developed. A more developed UI and removing the Excell component of the tool could also be
easily implemented. This would ease the assessment as cross-platform inputs and analysis would not be
needed, though this could remove some of the adaptability of the tool as a whole.
9.7
Variable inputs
It can be seen by the normalising of the data that the proportion of each future scenario characteristic
remains the same as the initial results, this is counted by the future scenario factor, however, this means
that this factor and the logic behind it is the ultimate factor in the accuracy and usefulness of the results.
The process of normalisation also leads to drastic variations in results wich must be remedied. The effect
of variable weighting and normalisation can be seen through the numerous different solutions generated
during the development and refinement of the tool which were:
• A preliminary flexibility score of 37.84 with a standard deviation of 43.1 and a GMV flexibility
rating of 18.7 with a GMV standard deviation of 0.01 giving an aggregated flexibility score of 49%.
This was effected by a change in building class from commercial to residential.
• A preliminary flexibility score of 16.84 with a standard deviation of 21.79 and a GMV flexibility
score of 18.7 with a GMV standard deviation of 0.01 giving an aggregated flexibility score of 110%
(taken as 90% flexibility in relation to the score proximity to 1). This was due to an additional
standards weighting as part of an increased user input version of the tool.
• Preliminary flexibility index of 14.82 GMV score of 15.21 and aggregated flexibility index of 92%,
With the predominant design characteristic being lifecycle, having a weighting of 36% (negative
scenario), 28% (no change scenario) and 10% (positive scenario). This was due to an increase in the
importance factor placed on the scenario factor weighting.
The examples used in Chapter 6 outline some of the variations in weightings which account for these
different results. It is important to note that they have not been chronologically catalogued or directly
associated with the effect of a change in weightings with a change in the final flexibility score. This is
because there were too many interactions and interlinked adjustments to draw any real conclusions from
these variations. These are listed here and presented in chapter 6 to outline the importance of a logical and
consistent weighting system.
9.7.1
Effects of Altered logic - Random case
This section outlines the effect of altering the adjustment of the normalisation process from an adjusted
average to a simplified feature scaling method. This tool version was implemented with an adjustment of
the in-build back-end weighting to produce an alternative solution for a random case. This was done to
assess the effect of variations in the data on the results (This new layout can be explored in the technical
appendix, Appendix B, accompanying this report).
The inputs outlined in figure 9.4 the MPT output (outlined in figure 9.10 and figure 9.9 ) produced a
flexibility score of 0.003 (as outlined in figure 9.5 and figure 9.8. When assessing the effect of the de-
sign characteristics on this score, as seen in figure 9.7 and figure 9.6, society is seen as the predominant
influencing factor.

9.7.2
Data relevance
From the results of the altered tool inputs and backend factors, it is clear that a relevant weighting system
and rating metric is essential for any useful or logical implementation of the tool. It highlights the impor-
tance of simple early design stage implementation only and calls for further development of the category’s
weightings along with less adjustable settings. From this, it appears the tool is too flexible. The initial
concept of the weightings system as a secondary consideration of this thesis has proven to be harder to
actualise in practice as the weighting used for the tool is paramount in justifying and defending its results.
Flexible Design Analysis
115
==PAGE:129==
9.8
Case study
The case study involved an in-depth look at the NHN hospital project and incorporated the steps as outlined
in the flexibility frameworks1.6. The scale of the project led to the utilisation of only a small and specific
area of the hospital, from which a general assumption on the flexibility of the structure was made, given
that it was one of three designed flexibility scenarios. The structuring of the case study highlighted the
need for in-depth knowledge of a project, the input of several stakeholders and key actors of the project
and an overview of the workings and design of the structure to successfully analyse its flexibility. It is
this insight that lends itself to further the need for a ”flexibility” manager or analyst who can draw from
the expertise of many fields and check the validity of the claims from various disciplines in regard to the
flexibility of their designs.
The tool’s adaptability and ease of use are a result of the assumption that the designer and analyst can
accurately gauge the importance of each aspect of the build. It is also assumed that their opinions and
the project category weighting are synonymous with the groupings of building class and discipline. If
the weighting of the importance of the discipline changes or the analyst can not accurately gauge the
appropriate information and data to analyse, then the tool will not function as intended. It is therefore
emphasised that the tool is a guide and reference check for a designer in the early design phase, and is as
much subject to the biases of the designer as the designs themselves.
The use of an additional rating system (The Ballinger assessment) for the same case study has been used
here to assess the homogony of the theoretical background of the field and to highlight the necessity for a
standardised process, based on the scientific method, to analyse the adaptability of buildings so that, at the
very least, two separate tools can be compared and appraised as schools in the same discipline. Though
the tools were run at different scales of the building (full structure and micro-system) the homogony of the
results at least partly lends itself to the accuracy of the assessments.
9.8.1
Case study results analysis
The analysis shows that the adaptation from office to bedward is possible in regards to indoor climate, with
a flexibility score of 68%. However from discussions with the project management team (see (Colclough
2023)) and reviewing relevant literature (chapter 3) it is clear that the concept of utilising the offices as
bed wards would not be the most ideal solution as it would hamper the staff’s ability to treat patients and
make their job and work environment less than ideal. From the Results sheet of DATEx it can be seen that
a holistic lifecycle plan has the highest importance to the flexibility of the structure. The lifecycle plans
of extension in the inner courtyard, at the edges of the clever, extension via lightweight satellite structures
and end of life in the form of a hotel, indicate that this feature has been contemplated with solid designs
and concept sketches. The low technical score of 1.72 is also indicative of the design, considering the
premise that natural ventilation for adaptation of the structure will be easier by far to implement, due to the
concrete structure, cost and life of MEP services. The ”state of the future” requirements of the hospital also
indicate that planning for a specific technology will more than likely lead to fast redundancy. It is clear
that it is far more beneficial to plan for change and allow an organic transition of technologies than to try
and cater for or predict a specific, robust and long-lasting technical solution in the case of the NHN super
hospital. Therefore design considerations for passive solutions should be implemented but additional costs
for a theory of adaptability in the design cannot be justified, as such a cost analyst and a rating of solutions
to scenario obstacles could be an additional assessment area for the tool.
The NHN project was an interesting example as the need for a hospital to change with requirements is
fundamental to the design brief of such structures and yet the budget, build time and dedicated areas
required, lead to a structure that is inflexible and a system that is so convoluted that a change of use would
cause major issues to the flow of the users. The question here seems to be how can we develop an inflexible
structure with the capacity to change with requirements. Adaptation of use rather than flexibility of the
physical elements seems to be key as well as the easy addition of separate space for the possibility of
needed space.
Flexible Design Analysis
116
==PAGE:130==
9.8.2
Case study strengths and limitations
The tool has indicated the areas for the highest focus and areas of lesser importance as well as indicating
a general score for the flexibility of the spaces of bedward and office in regards to indoor climate. These
numbers, indicative of action and allowing cross-comparison with other structures, do not give a clear path
to achieving a more flexible design. The tool analyses the mentality and view of the designer as much as the
planned structure and as such is unlikely to affect the outcome in a large way. The results were generated
using the BIM inputs from the bed ward and office area openings ratio as a gauge for the flexibility of the
spaces but not every design factor is equally, or appropriately developed and full implementation of the
flexible analysis was not achieved. Furthermore, the large standard deviation of the results shows that the
results generated are speculative at best. When taken in combination with the pedigree matrix, a picture
emerges of a far more murky result than the process scores generated would suggest.
9.8.3
Implications
The implications of these result for the NHN super hospital is limited as construction is already in the
second phase (of four) at the time of writing (2023). However, it does highlight the benefit of the lifecycle
solutions developed. When the future environment and use of the building change, large amounts of time,
money and effort do not need to be used to regroup experts with invaluable knowledge of the project,
some of whom may not be accessible at the time of need. The score indicates that the design concept
of utilising the offices as hospital rooms would work from an indoor climate perspective, but, from fur-
ther investigation, would clearly impact the flow of the structure. This would further impact the areas of
space, and society (in the interaction between staff and staff and patients etc, flowing out into the social
consciousness), reducing the effectiveness of the lifecycle planning within this concept of flexibility.
9.9
Ethical considerations
Data protection and confidentiality were considered and adhered to when assessing and reproducing infor-
mation gained from the CEJ NHN database. All interviews were carried out with informed consent and
all surveys were carried out anonymously. The link between the author of this thesis and the management
team at NHN has been noted.
9.10
Amendments
During the compilation of this thesis, many amendments were made, not least of which was the exclusion
of an additional building standards weighting category, altering of the backend discipline class and building
class weighting, trialling various normalising methods and alternative class weighting relationships.
9.11
Limitations
The limitations of this thesis are an important element of its utilisation. Consideration of the elements
that require further development is seen as part of the sphere of influence this thesis can be considered in.
These include the following:
• Literature Selection: The literature assessed was chosen based on the concept of flexibility as pre-
sented at DTU in the special courses, Målbar Fleksibilitet i Byggeriet (2023), and does not consider
every school of thought in regards to the flexibility of a structure, Nor does it evaluate the concepts
of ”best” practice, such as lightweight or robust design and high-tech or low-tech solutions, where
possible.
• Data Collection: The research for this thesis was limited with only six participants in the survey.
The interviews were predominantly conducted with the NHN client team and are not representative
of a wider data source or general or niche theories on flexibility and best practices.
• Scope of the Tool: The tool is currently built to only evaluate the indoor climate of a structure and
not the not full building’s flexibility. However, the metric of passive indoor climate controls is used
Flexible Design Analysis
117
==PAGE:131==
as a proxy for the overall flexibility of a building as a whole due to its relative importance for a
building’s use, associated costs and continued relevance, thus increasing its lifespan.
• Case Study: The tool is only tested on one case study and may not be indicative of the flexibility
of various structures. The case study also lacks sufficient depth.
• IFC Libraries: The IFC libraries generated for use in the tool are basic, consisting of a simple
assessment of opening size to slab/space size as a metric for the potential of passive ventilation.
• MPT It is assumed that modern portfolio theory is applicable to building design and that a building
can be represented by a portfolio of design assets, and further, that a successful design follows a
logical and rational framework and can be modelled as such.
• Weighting: The weighting for the disciplines and building classes for the tool are assumed from
best knowledge and could be refined by conducting further research and development.
• Probability Models The probability models used for future scenarios are simple and leave the actual
probability analysis up to the analyst instead of having more comprehensive scenarios modelled and
assessed within the tool.
• Sensitivity Analysis: The sensitivity of the analyses is briefly considered and a full perturbation
analysis of the inputs for the final result is not incorporated.
• BIM compatibility: BIM plugins and parametric design features are not developed for the tool.
• Comparative Analysis: It is not possible to compare two different design solutions at the same time,
but rather run one and then the other and compare their separate flexibility results. A comparison of
multiple structures would require strict adherence to maintaining logical sequences.
9.11.1
Assessment of limitations
Areas for improvement include the development of a more streamlined script and intuitive user interface, as
this would greatly increase the value of the tool. To test the accuracy of the analysis tool more case studies
should be performed using different focus points to refine the PPI’s and further develop the core theory.
Additionally, the IFC integration aspect of the tool is not fully implemented as it does not automatically
assign a weighting to the relevant solution weighting section, rather it prints a takeoff of elements of the
model for analysis. To fully implement this aspect of the tool the Excel sheet, or at least the results section
thereof, should be fully replaced with a Python script, to remove the need for cross-checking the two
separate programs.
Expanding on the limitations listed above it is clear that the weighting of the characteristics is of particular
importance. A hierarchy of evidence approach was used to filter the categories and weightings of the tool,
this was based on the limited data that it was possible to collect during this thesis’s time frame. The impor-
tance factor assigned to different building classes and disciplines would benefit from further development.
The probability calculations and weighting formulas are generic and there is room for interpretation and
improvement in the numerical values and assessments therein. This is linked to the effect of most survey
responses and interviews coming from the team responsible for the development of the super hospital. A
more widely received survey with 50+ respondents would add to the meaningfulness of the research. The
answer rate of 6 respondents is used here no alternative was presented, however, its results are not held to
be representative.
Further considerations of the limitation of the use of the tool include that the tool should not be taken out
of the geographical and temporal context of its design and implementation, the flexibility of a building and
the concepts that dictate its lifespan can vary greatly by location, time of construction n and society. The
temporal limitations of assessing a structure that is still being constructed bear importance as the wealth
of data gathered cannot be assured to be fully accurate on completion.
Flexible Design Analysis
118
==PAGE:132==
The results generated are limited in that the flexibility score and the tool outputs are meant as a guide
in the early design phase and to compare like structures. Their results should not be taken out of this
context or applied to situations outside of their design scope without further adaption. The final score
is based largely on user bias and as such all results are part of an opinion, an analytical and numerically
assessed and comparable opinion, but an opinion nonetheless. This subjectivity has to be allowed for in
the assessment of flexibility.
”A flexible design will often be a mixture of specific and generic forms of accommodation, he adds: “Get
the mix right and it will last longer — maybe much longer, but not forever. There are always trade-offs in
design. That’s why it’s fascinating.”- Robert Schmidt” (WSP - Northampton 2021)
9.12
Recommendations
Further areas for consideration to enhance the analysis tool encompass investigating the impacts of:
• Cause-effect relations.
• resilience of the affected systems.
• existence of thresholds.
• What are the critical impact levels?
• Geographical/location effects.
• The effect of population density.
• The severity of the effect.
• Possibility to compensate for effects.
• Temporal aspects of consequences (when will they be felt?).
• Restoration cost and effect.
• Whether the mechanism is reversible.
As outlined in Anders Damgaard 2023
9.12.1
Planed developments
The addition of more alternative tools to compare the results of different theories of flexibility was planned,
but not implemented, as were deeper indoor climate considerations utilising IDA ice, data analysis with
Power BI and automated implementation of the IFC analysis results. These actions are considered as the
logical next steps of the work outlined here, along with more detailed surveys and the development of the
back-end weighting system. Other actions to counter the limitations and add value to this report include:
• IFC libraries In any further development of the tool, more IFC assessment libraries could be com-
piled. This would allow for deeper and more elaborate evaluation of the actual design of the structure
to be taken, and a less perspective-based scoring of the flexibility of the structure achieved. These
larger IFC libraries for the assessment of flexible buildings should be developed to allow for a wider
spectrum of analysis and a deeper, full-design, solution assessment
• perturbation analysis A full perturbation analysis of the inputs for the final result should be incor-
porated.
• parametric design Development of the use of parametric design tools within the flexibility analysis
tool should be considered.
Flexible Design Analysis
119
==PAGE:133==
• simultaneous analysis The addition of multiple, simultaneous, design solutions analyses would add
value to any future tool or adaption of the tool outlined here to allow different design variations to
be assessed and compared for the same design brief.
• Social integration Integration with the wider public to raise awareness and highlight the importance
of flexibility in design should be undertaken.
• Alternative design solutions Assessing alternative design solutions for the case study as a test for
the accuracy of the tool, such as movable designs (vehicles as a solution to overcapacity) adjustable
patient rooms (utilizing a grid and rolling rooms) and modular fabric treatment pods, were conceived
but never developed. This possibility is suggested for future consideration.
• cost saving The development of a component indicating the cost saving of a flexible design could be
implemented, or a formula to integrate cost and the flexibility score generated, using the flexibility
index as a factorial for further calculations.
• Representative Box plot With more data or a more developed data set, representing the flexibility
index with a box plot to assess its flexibility rating would be a useful implementation of existing
theories. This is presented in appendix B but not considered implemented within the tool.
9.12.2
Concepts to consider
Some further considerations that have not been covered in this thesis but are of note, such as multi-tenancy
spaces/functionality mixes, flexibility by layer over discipline, an operational flexibility range, considera-
tion bias and cyclically phased grading are outlined here.
Multi tenancy spaces/Functionality mixes
Multi-tenancy spaces/Functionality mixes have not been discussed in this thesis and were only peripherally
investigated as part of the specific research. Multi-tenancy spaces, or functionality mixes, refer to the ver-
satile use of a single space for various purposes. This concept can increase the optimisation of building use
and the flexibility of the building design. By carefully considering the spatial arrangements and structural
elements, spaces can be created that accommodate diverse functions e.g. from offices to residential use.
Though not implemented in the concepts of the tool development outlined here it is an area worth specific
mention and could be analysed under several of the design characteristics, such as space or life-cycle plan.
Flexibility by layer over discipline
The concept of flexibility is something which must be well defined, not just as a discipline definition but
as an action, is a building that is left empty for a generation and then restored adapted or re-purposed?
Where on the line of reuse to recycle does maintaining the facade of a building but replacing the other
layers stand? The flexibility score of a structure by layers, and the composition of a single score based on
these scores, with additional weighting based on a chosen cost, would seem like a sensible continuation
and refinement of any assessment method. The Dutch hospital concepts (Hinkema 2023) utilized for the
construction and possible extension of the NHN project highlight a further separation of layers. A building
could be seen from the view of both physical layers and layers of utility such as layers of use of a building,
comprising, core and peripheral zones. A kitchen or bathroom, requiring greater technical elements, and
possibly subject to the use of more inhabitants of a multi-tenant dwelling, tends to change far less often
than the layout of a bedroom, or private space, these robust cores are at the centre of the peripheral zones
of residential units, these peripheral zones could include spaces such as sheds or garages.
Operational flexibility range
When talking of flexibility, as with other engineering disciplines, the concept of a range and a distinction
between a maximum and operating quantification seems to be important. A wind turbine can be rated at
a maximum of 7kw, but when combined with average (sub-optimal) wind speeds, generator efficiencies
and circuit resistance you find that 150 - 200 watts will be your average actual output. So with flexibility,
it is important to note that a maximum flexibility, as well as an operational flexibility rating, should be
reached.
Flexible Design Analysis
120
==PAGE:134==
Consideration bias
The concept that design for change instigates change is also something to be considered, if a building is
marketed, designed and sold on the idea that it can be converted to a hotel at the end of its life, thus adding
to the value of that structure, it would follow that the design and concept of flexibility increase the likely
hood of that event and the prolonged life of that structure. The act of developing scenarios and planning for
the flexibility of a building could inherently affect the implementation of that flexibility and the likelihood
of future scenarios or building use that is planned for.
Cyclically phased grading
A phase and cyclical representation of the results and the design characteristics’ effect on flexibility, instead
of a linear interpretation, was planned but not implemented. This would accommodate varying design
philosophies. For instance, it would allow for both theories that a lightweight temporary structure, and a
heavy, long lifespan structure can both be flexible or the notion that both a low-cost and a high-end building
tend to exhibit greater flexibility compared to a structure with a mid-range design in terms of cost. This
circular view of flex ability is an important factor to be researched.
Flexible Design Analysis
121
==PAGE:135==
10
Conclusion
From this thesis, it is clear that one single flexible index score is possible to achieve, but a full flexibility
analysis benefits from the inclusion of more outputs and deeper levels of analysis. Furthermore, an output
that can be used as a factor in assessing the ”cost” of a structure under different criteria (environmental
compact, financial cost, energy requirements) is also possible.
This evaluation should encompass various future scenarios and their probabilities, while considering a
building as a synthesis of design attributes, each exerting a distinct influence on its flexibility. These
attributes hold differing importance in relation to the scenarios examined. Automation of the process is
desirable and building a compendium of ready-to-use IFC analysis libraries would be necessary for a mar-
ketable design analysis tool. The input of the analyst is also an important factor as it is clear that flexibility
is a value judgement (Robert Schmidt 2016). The adaptability of the tool itself by using open source
software such as Python, or widely available software such as Excel, with clear backend interdependence
allowing for a change of weighting and evaluation characteristics, is also a beneficial aspect of the tool.
Furthermore, the need for the assessment of a building and supporting documentation from many disci-
plines within the AECM industry and the use of an analysis tool as part of a larger report on flexibility is
clear. This led to the concept of the development of a flexibility framework, similar to LCA, as a systematic
evaluation based on a developed and accepted framework is key to the future and professional acceptance
of flexibility analyses.
A flexibility index score proves valuable in early design phases to determine where efforts should concen-
trate for extending the lifespan of the building. However, it allows cross-evaluation of different structures’
flexibility only under the same controls and logical progression and this process has not been validated.
While limitations exist, such as data scarcity, insufficient survey respondents and an underdeveloped case
study, the proposed tool demonstrates potential. Further research to refine flexibility assessment categories
and expand the IFC libraries, thus automating the process, would enhance its usefulness. Integrating cost
assessment and broader stakeholder input, including BIM plugins, parametric design, and more compre-
hensive probability models, could amplify the tool’s utility. Ultimately the tool’s context-bound nature,
subjective inputs, and linear approach should be considered when interpreting results.
10.1
The future
It is theorised that a guide to an assessment method, similar to LCA, rather than a definitive, mathematical
tool may be a better development at the current level of interest and understanding of the field. Further-
more, it would be beneficial to develop different guides for adaptability for different specialities, such as
structural, architectural, developer, owner occupier, government, contractor etc.
Flexible design analysis in buildings should embrace adaptability, mirroring the ever-shifting landscape of
architectural theory and evolving in step with changing insights into the nature of buildings and ourselves.

Temporal constraints 
(analysis period)
Geographic constraints (location)
Factor 
Description
High Rating
Low rating
Report required
Cost
Is the structure high or low cost. How has this been 
considered in regards to its flexibility?
The budget is extremely important and a 
defining feature of the design. There is little 
room for change as the budget is set.
The budget is secondary to 
other factors, such as time, 
quality or the other desing 
charicteristics
Cost analysis
Ownership
What are the prevalent ownership models for the 
structure and what future ownership models have 
been considered?
An ownership model is well defined and will 
remain in place for the life of the building.
The ownership model is badly 
defined with distribution 
between different actors, such 
as owner, occupier and user. 
The ownership model is highly 
variable across the life of the 
building.
Document of intent and  
outline of governing 
ownership principles 
Lifecycle plan
How has a road map for future development and 
the capacity for unplanned change been 
considered?
The building has a well developed lifecycle 
plan with detailed drawings and plans 
covering many contingencies.
The building has no lifecycle 
plan and is delivered as is with 
no consideration of future 
scenarios implemented in the 
design.
Lifecyle pan and LCA 
report (with a clear guid 
to implementing the 
findings)
Maintenance
Is there an integrated maintenance plan as part of 
the design,. How will the design degrade without 
this plan?
The facility management team/end user has 
been consulted during the design process 
and there is a well developed handover 
package regarding the construction of the 
building which will/can be continually 
contributed to by the facility management 
team .
The facility management team 
has not been consulted and 
there is no handover package 
and no/poor documentation of 
the construction process.
Facility management 
handbook/data handover 
and facility managers 
input report/notes.
Society
What design concepts  have been considered, has 
an introspective examination on what  the 
geopolitical and socio-cultural expectations for the 
structure are with considerations of the 
growth/future paradigm of the macro and micro-
social setting of the structure? 
Local and regional actors (such as social 
bodies, groups and individuals) have been 
informed of the building and design process 
and there feedback has been gathered and 
incorporated. Continued integration with 
community is planned after constitution and 
there are aspects of the design which are for 
/accessible to/benefit the public.
No external actors have been 
engaged with, no parts of the 
design are intended for public 
integration, no consideration of 
the buildings community has 
been made. 
Public relations report, 
including regional 
planning considerations 
and local 
surveys/feedback 
responses. 
Technical
considerations of the prevailing design method, 
prior consideration, layout and implementation of 
MEP services.
A robust MEP design, utilizing low energy 
systems which are easily accessible in a 
layout allowing for expansion and reduction 
of the services without additional structural 
considerations or augmentation of other 
aspects of the building. 
low level integration with 
building standards regards to 
ventilation. Heating, lighting 
etc  utilizing untested solutions 
in a strict single use solution 
with a set capacity installed in 
a way which reduces access to 
the elements of the system and 
secured in a way which hinders 
there removal/replacement. 
Initial MEP assessment 
report facilitating 
integration of MEP 
considerations in the 
early design stage.
Structural
What logical use and future use strategy is designed 
for and which concepts of flexibility have been 
included?
A concept of flexibility has been included in 
the layout and spacing of structural 
supports. It is easy to change the internal 
spaces and add spaces for additional needs. 
A change in load case has been considered 
and planed for.
Large, numerous, close 
proximity supports are utilized 
with no facility of addition of 
further support considered. No 
possibility of adjusting or 
adding spaces due to structural 
elements.
Structural report 
detailing the adherence 
(or lack there of) of the 
structure to the 
principles of flexibility 
and lifecycle plan 
developed.
Aesthetic
Is the aesthetic character of the building a priority
The design is novel and seen as exemplary 
by its peers, generally or  in a certain design 
style. It is/will be intrinsic to the landscape 
and a focal point of the location it is built in. 
It builds an emotional connection with its 
occupants.
The design is not developed, un 
unique, does not fit well with 
its surroundings and hinders its 
occupants while reducing there 
emotional  connection with the 
building.
Desing analysis and 
report  outlining the 
structures stylistic 
concept and integration 
(or the lack there of) with 
its location.
Space
Do the physical elements allow for multiple user 
activities and is the layout of those spaces 
augmentable? 
Good zoning of the building is incorporated 
into its design with consideration of 
alternative activities in the relevant zones. 
Fire planning is well developed and can e 
changed with ease for new zone layouts. 
The structure allow easy access to different 
zones, user navigation is intuitive and it is 
easy for the user too understand the 
purpose of different zones by their design 
and layout. 
No zone planning is 
incorporated, there is a strict 
fire plan with no scope for 
change. Structural elements of 
the building hinder the change 
of use of a space and it is 
intrinsically difficult to navigate 
the building.
Zoning and Fire plan
Digitization
Rate the modelling, information, processing, and 
continued facility management input of a the 
structure and its planned use. 
The project is integrated with BIM at a high 
level . 3D point scanning is used to 
catalogue as  built  model of the building. A 
digital twin is developed along side the 
physical structure and all relevant data in 
regards to design, construction, 
management and maintenance is recorded 
on a accessible platform. All actors have 
integrated input into a single digital source 
where total  building data is stored.
There is no digital integration 
or elements. All planning, 
construction, maintenance and 
use data is lost or recoded 
individually on single physical 
sources and no data 
integration is adopted. 
Intent statement for the 
level of digital 
engagement

This shows the benefit of each design factor to the structures' transformation capacity/flexibility given the possible future scenarios as outlined by the user. It outlines the effect of the design in relation to the 
probability of the future situation the structure will face and can be used to gauge the variance of the factors.
This output includes the current design state and scenario as a further reference set.  No change and current state give identical importance to each factor and so the current state is emitted in further analysis.
==PAGE:156==
Versatility of design
Desing reward Design risk
Versatility Ratio
Negative Future change

33.96190592

Positive Future change
39.36180775
38.30844179
0.973238882
The MSV (Mean, Standard deviation and versatility (sharps ratio)) shows the flexibility index score of the design for possible scenarios. The unit is the "flexibility score" (EF). 
The mean or "design reward" can be seen as a rating of the future reward of the design choices, and can be used as a factor to rate cost savings in terms of, energy, environmental impact, time, or another 
relevant metric.  
The standard deviation from this mean or "design risk" shows the possible variation from this reward, and can be used to measure the uncertainty of the design rewards. 
The versatility ratio is the relationship between the risk and reward for the given scenarios. A ratio of 0.5 indicates that the scenario's reward is generating 0.5 units of excess reward for each unit of risk taken. 
This could be considered an average ratio. a ratio of 1 or higher can be considered good and a ratio of 2-3 excellent.

The benefit of the design solution is displayed as the Flexibility scores. It has units of Effective flexibility "EF", before conversion to percentiles. This can also be seen as how efficient a design solution is in a 
given scenario. The more efficient the design solution in the new scenario, the more flexible its original design. A higher efficiency score indicates a more robust and future-proof design in regard to the specific 
factor of flexibility.

This data represents the mean of the benefit (Efficiency scores displayed in 'EF' units) of each design factor across the different 
scenarios giving an overall view of the return of each factor, taking into account the probability of the scenarios. It is an imaginary or 
idealised representation of the utility of the energy put into each factor and a representation of the distribution of effort that should 
be put into each factor for the design of a flexible building which will continue to meet demands and give the benefits of service 
accounting for the vicissitudes of time.

Aggregated standard deviation
The final aggregated flexibility score is calculated from the relationship between the 
most efficient mean (highest reward to lowest risk) portfolio comprised of a 
combination of design factors across different scenarios. 
This score shows proportionally how far from the "best" design in regards to the 
flexibility of the current design, and design energy breakdown, the current design is 
and reflects the adaptability of the finished structure given the set possible future 
scenarios.

#Transfomation capacity in buildings ,design adaptability tool and flexability rating
system.
3
The following program is run in relation to the complimentary "DATEx" excell file
and is dessinged to assess the adaptability of the desing of a structure. It
produces a global minimum variance (GMV) desing solution (the solution with the
most reward to least risk). Indicating firstly the ennergy (monitery cost,
enviormental impact, time) that should be invested into each design factor of
flexability , as listed in the DATEx" Excel file. It also highlights wich
projected future scenarios will be most detrimental and most benificial for the
longevity of the desing , based on the information gathered from DATEx. The unit
for this comparison is the "flexability score" (EF) of the desin, compiled from
factionised wheighted
factors developed in DATEx and expanded upon in the
reprot "Flexable structures". The mean flexability score of the GMV portfolio
can be compared with the mean of the current designs weighted factors to give a
single flexability ranking of the current soution in relation to the "best case"
solution.
4
