Efficincy frontier and Data anaylsys
169
The following script generates and efficiency frontier , this is a concept used in
portfolio optimization to determine the optimal allocation of assets that
maximizes the expected return for a given level of risk, or minimizes the risk
for a given level of return. A point on the curve of the efficiency frontier
represents a portfolio that offers the highest possible return for a given level
of risk, or the lowest possible risk for a given level of return.
170
171
A global minimum variance portfolio , one with the minimum risk for the maximum
returns , is computed and used as the ideal mix of assets (design factors) in a
specific portfolio (design solution)
172
#Correlation Matrix
173
In portfolio optimization , a correlation matrix is used to assess the
diversification benefits of combining assets with different correlations. By
including assets that have low or negative correlations , a portfolio can
potentially reduce overall risk without sacrificing returns. The correlation
matrix helps in constructing efficient portfolios by considering the covariance
structure of asset returns and selecting assets that have desirable correlations
with each other. This allows an optimal balance between risk and returns to be
achieved and the construct of portfolios that are efficient in terms of
achieving the desired objectives.
174
#In some instances in this script "Returns = Results"
175
Results = df #assigning a new df definition
176
Results_corr = Results.corr().abs() #generating design factor correlation from data
in DATEx
177
corr_rank = Results_corr.unstack().sort_values(kind="quicksort",ascending=False)
178
print (f'Maximum corralation: {corr_rank[len(Results_corr):len(Results_corr)+1]}') #
finding desing factors with maximum correlation for given future scenarios
179
Results_corr
180
#Covariance Matrix
181
182
The covariance matrix is a tool for analyzing the relationships between multiple
variables. It provides information about the co-movements and dependencies among
different variables by quantifying their joint variability. The covariance
matrix allows us to understand how changes in one variable correspond to changes
in another variable , indicating whether they move together or in opposite
Flexible Design Analysis
Page XXIX of CXXXVIII
XXIX
==PAGE:165==
directions.
183
184
This information is crucial for portfolio optimization , risk management , and asset
allocation decisions. By examining the elements of the covariance matrix ,
designers can assess the diversification benefits of combining different assets ,
identify potential sources of risk and return , and construct portfolios that
balance risk and reward effectively.
185
186
The covariance matrix serves as a key input in various statistical techniques such
as factor analysis , regression analysis , and portfolio optimization models ,
enabling us to make informed decisions based on the interrelationships among
variables.
187
188
The covariance matrix provides information about the direction and magnitude of
linear relationships between variables , while the correlation matrix
additionally standardizes the values to provide a measure of the strength and
direction of relationships that can be directly compared across different
datasets.
189
190
Covariance is used in collaboration with the correlation matrix as a correlation
matrix is needed in the context of an efficiency frontier for developing the "
best" design mix because it provides information about the relationships between
different design factors given different possible future scenarios. The
correlation coefficient measures the strength and direction of the linear
relationship between the variables , in this case, the returns of different
design factors in different scenarios. By analyzing the correlation matrix ,
designers can understand how different design factors move in relation to each
other and how their returns are likely to be correlated for possible future
scenarios.
191
Results_cov_matrix = df.cov() # fingind covariance matrix for desuing factor
contributiuons
192
print("=== Covariance Matrix ===") # printing covariance matrix
193
Results_cov_matrix

#Defining portfolios
195
Once we have developed a relationship between the data then portfolios with
different distributions of design characteristics can be developed.
196
A risk-free rate must be used as part of MPT.
197
198
The risk-free rate is assumed to be zero to simplify calculations and isolate the
risk premium associated with other designs. By assuming a risk-free rate of zero
, the focus shifts to evaluating the excess returns generated by riskier assets
above and beyond the risk-free return. This simplification allows for a clearer
analysis of risk and return relationships.
199
200
However , in practice , the risk-free rate is not always zero. It can vary. In
situations where the risk-free rate is not zero, it reflects the compensation a
designer receives for taking on no risk, it implies that investors can earn a
return without incurring any significant risk.
201
202
Therefore , the decision to change the assumed risk-free rate depends on the specific
context and purpose of the analysis. In real-world applications , it is
essential to use an appropriate risk-free rate that aligns with the prevailing
conditions and reflects the opportunity cost of investing in a risk-free asset.
This may involve considering short-term solutions , government legislation , or
other suitable proxies for the risk-free rate based on the first used horizon
and the specific structure being analyzed.
203
204
An example of a risk-free design factor would be the inclusion of solar panels that
were entirely covered by a government grant and which did not impose on the
aesthetic , structural or space considerations , and were easily removable.
205
RFR = 0 #risk free rate - can be changed if project specifications give favorable
design choice regarding certan desing factors

#Here a small positive constant is added to the diagonal of the covariance matrix.
This is called regularization and can stabilize the matrix to make it inevitable
.

#regularization
220
Sigma_b = Results_cov_matrix.values
221
Sigma_b += np.eye(Sigma_b.shape[0]) * 0.001
222
Sigma_inv_b = np.linalg.inv(Sigma_b)
223
224
#The above code adds a small positive constant of 0.001 to the diagonal of your
covariance matrix. You can adjust this value depending on your needs.
225
# To do: put in information about equations from course
226
227
a = mu_b.T @ Sigma_inv_b @ mu_b
228
b = mu_b.T @ Sigma_inv_b @ np.ones_like(mu_b)
229
c = np.ones_like(mu_b).T @ Sigma_inv_b @ np.ones_like(mu_b)
230
mu_gmv = b/c
231
var_gmv = 1/c
232
233
mus_b = np.linspace(np.min(mu_b) - np.ptp(mu_b), np.max(mu_b) + np.ptp(mu_b))
234
sigmas_b = np.sqrt((c*mus_b**2 - 2*b*mus_b + a) / (a*c - b**2)).squeeze()
235
236
sigma_p = np.linspace(0, 1)
237
mu_e_b = mu_b - RFR
238
mu_p_b = (sigma_p * np.sqrt(mu_e_b.T @ Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b) + RFR).squeeze()
239
240
mu_e_tan_b = (mu_e_b.T @ Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b) / (np.ones_like(mu_e_b).T @
Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b)
241
sigma_tan_b = np.sqrt(mu_e_b.T @ Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b) / (np.ones_like(mu_e_b).T @
Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b)
242
slope_b = mu_e_tan_b / sigma_tan_b

@ Sigma_inv_b @ mu_e_b)).flatten()

Global minimum variance portfolio
250
def objective_function(x, Sigma):
251
return x.T @ Sigma @ x
252
253
def efficient_frontier_optimizer(mu, Sigma, constraints = None, bounds = None,
min_return = None, max_return = None):
254
if min_return is None:
255
min_return = np.min(mu) - np.ptp(mu)
256
if max_return is None:
Flexible Design Analysis
Page XXXI of CXXXVIII
XXXI
==PAGE:167==
257
max_return = np.max(mu) + np.ptp(mu)
258
259
target_returns = np.linspace(min_return , max_return)
260
result_success = np.empty_like(target_returns)
261
result_returns = np.empty_like(target_returns)
262
result_vars = np.empty_like(target_returns)
263
result_w = []
264
265
for (i, target) in enumerate(target_returns):
266
if constraints is None:
267
_constraints = []
268
else:
269
_constraints = constraints.copy()
270
C_DESIRED_RETURN = LinearConstraint(A = mu.T - RFR, lb = target , ub = target
) # mu_e' w = mu_e_p
271
_constraints.append(C_DESIRED_RETURN)
272
273
t = minimize(
274
fun = objective_function ,
275
x0 = np.zeros_like(mu),
276
args = (Sigma, ),
277
#method = "SLSQP",
278
constraints=_constraints ,
279
bounds=bounds
280
)
281
282
result_success[i] = t.success
283
result_returns[i] = target #returns excess return (mu_e)
284
result_vars[i] = t.fun
285
result_w.append(t.x)
286
return result_success.astype(bool), result_returns , result_vars , result_w
287

mu_b, Sigma_b ,

constraints = [

all energy invested in desing

],

)

305
mu_b, Sigma_b ,

308
],
309

energy avalabel that is not already invested)
310
min_return = returns_a[lowest_success],
311
max_return = returns_a[highest_success])

A.3.1
AHP
The mathematical equation outlining the Analytical Hierarchy Process is presented here.
Priority(A) = WA
n
n
X
i=1
 CAi
WAi

(A.1)
In this equation:
- Priority(A) represents the priority of criterion or alternative A. - WA represents the weight of criterion
A. - n is the total number of criteria or alternatives. - CAi represents the numerical value or rating of the
criterion or alternative Ai. - WAi represents the weight of criterion or alternative Ai.
This equation is used in AHP to calculate the priority of a criterion or alternative based on its weight and
the relative importance of all other criteria or alternatives. It can be customize it to suit a specific AHP
problem by substituting A, WA, n, CAi, and WAi with specific values.

A.3.3
MPT
The mathematic principles of the efficiency frontier are outlined in the slides from the 42104 Introduction
to Financial Engineering Fall 22, lecture notes 7 and 8 and are presented in the appendix.
Matrix multiplication is used to formulate the expected returns and risks of different portfolios.
Taking [ = [12∙∙∙n]′ is a one-column matrix of expected returns on different design characteristics and
[w = [w1w2∙∙∙wn]′is a one-column matrix representing the fractions of the allocated energy in each design
characteristic.
The covariance matrix between returns is denoted by Σ
he expected return on such a portfolio is [P = wT ]
he variance of returns for such a portfolio is 2
P = wT w , where Σ is the covariance matrix of the returns
The Lagrange formula is then used
L(w, 1, 2) = 1/2wT w−1(wT −P)−2(wT 1−1)
The results vector is then reshaped and a variable ascribed to it and Sigma_B is defined as the covariance
matrix.
a small positive constant is added to the diagonal of the covariance matrix. This is called regularization
and can stabilize the matrix to make it inevitable.
The variables for the matrix expressions for expected return and variance, a, b and c are defined and the
global minimum variance portfolio risk (var_gmv = 1/c) and return (mu_gmv = b/c) is calculated. and the
global minimum variance portfolio risk (var_gmv = 1/c) and return (mu_gmv = b/c) is calculated.
This outlined the basic logic of the python script. A fuller description of the MPT process is:
1. Expected Portfolio Return: The expected return of a portfolio is a weighted sum of the expected returns
of its individual assets. If the assets with expected returns R1, R2, . . . , Rn and weights w1, w2, . . . , wn are
in the portfolio, the expected return µp of the portfolio is given by:
µp = w1R1 + w2R2 + . . . + wnRn
2. Portfolio Variance: The variance of a portfolio measures the risk associated with it. If the individual
assets have variances σ2
1, σ2
2, . . . , σ2
n and the covariances between asset pairs are Cov(Ri, Rj), then the
portfolio variance σ2
p is calculated as:
Flexible Design Analysis
Page XXXV of CXXXVIII
XXXV
==PAGE:171==
σ2
p = w2
1σ2
1 + w2
2σ2
2 + . . . + w2
nσ2
n + 2w1w2Cov(R1, R2) + 2w1w3Cov(R1, R3) + . . .
3. Portfolio Standard Deviation: The standard deviation of the portfolio is the square root of its variance:
σp =
q
σ2p
These principles are at the core of MPT, which aims to optimize the allocation of assets in a portfolio to
achieve a desired trade-off between risk and return.

Literary review results and concepts
A.4.1
John Habraken (1960’s) open buildings,
The following key takeaways were developed for the sources based on John Habraken’s theory on open
buildings. (openbuilding.co 2020) (Raghad Almarhoon 2021) (schwarzpictures.com 2013)
”Open Building supports the transition to a society based on co-creation, participation, involvement and
inclusion” (openbuilding.co 2020)
The following is a direct reciting of John Habraken’s concepts (ibid.).
Property rights Deed of division, maximize the amount of apartment rights Freedom of function assignment
Centralised connection to utilities
Infrastructure: robust, oversized Meets residential and non-residential requirements Free layout: open
floors bay width vertical connections Partition walls disconnected of supports
Demountable, separated from the support Flexible adjustable Maximised free layout of floors Raised floor
systems Centralised cabinets (preferable on ground floor) Shared facilities
Demountable Divisibility/adaptability: silhouette/double skin façade: great freedom of layout behind a
uniform appearance of the façade collage: freedom of appearance per dwelling or a cluster of units grid:
freedom of appearance in every façade infill
Demountable, flexible adjustable Property of inhabitant/user Phased investment Collective purchase Cir-
cular materials and systems Customized manufacturing
The concept of a clear distinction between the support and the infill, both technical and in the ability of
personal influence. Supports are part of the public domain and are permanent, while the infill belongs to
the individual and is changeable. The Participation and freedom of choice of the user is the key objective.
From the film ”DE DRAGER / A film about Architect John Habraken” (schwarzpictures.com 2013) the
following concepts were taken:
• Scale is a universal parameter.
• Reorganisation of space is fundamental to good design and structural life.
• Images are more important for explanation, not examples.
• Self-determination of the end-user is paramount.
• Industrialisation has driven change. From this we can see social progress as driving and enabling
design,
A.4.2
Steward Brand (1994), How Buildings Learn
The concepts developed from this literature were the inclusion of the maintenance aspect of adaptability
in any flexibility/adaptability tool, such as an open-source building management/tracking software.
A further result was an education on the design categorization of:
• Site
• Structure
• Skin
• Services
• Space plan
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LI of CXXXVIII
LI
==PAGE:187==
• Stuff
From the TV series ”How buildings Learn” (Brand 1997) the following concepts where taken:
How Buildings Learn by Stewart Brand 1 of 6
In episode 2 “The Low Road” the influence of low cost was highlighted (low-design buildings are more
flexible as they give more freedom to inhabitants). The idea that community engagement is important
for the survival of a building as the building must adapt to the needs of the changing society was also
presented, as was the concept of reusable space within a building.
In episode 3 “Built for Change” outlines that there are no finished spaces, access to light (consideration
of windows and a corridor) is crucial, geometrically speaking rectangles are easy to build onto and that an
owners influence over the design is a crucial factor.
In episode “Unreal Estate” Stewart Brand discusses Edge cities, unreasonable bureaucracy (influencing
the metric for location and government), the influence of the ownership and intent of structure (owned by
the client, investment, public use) and the importance of additions with respect for what was before.
In episode 5 ”The Romance of Maintenance” The benefit of material that looks bad before they acts bad,
not materials that act bad before they looks bad is discussed. As is planning for change, considering the
decomposition of a building and setting aside commodities for the future of the building.
This sparked the concept of the maintenance aspect of flexibility – end-user interaction with building
software, and the importance of educating people on how to live in a building. The importance of the
visibility of issues and ease of access for repair. How to factor in continual maintenance into a design,
possibly by developing a low-fee building check by an assessor, like the required motor testing in most
countries but for your building.
Episode 6 ”Shearing Layers” discusses the Façade as a fake, unchanging front, the importance of managing
alterations with older materials. The effect of different rates of change for different sectors (e.g commercial
and residential) and the importance of being able to undo change (keeping what is existing for restoration
later).
This episode keys the term ”Adaptive state”, the ability of buildings to adapt and regenerate themselves.
It discusses the layers of change and how they change at different rates related to each other.
Finally, he highlights the factors of a ”Mature building”: Experienced, Complex, subtle, Wise, Savey,
Idiosyncratic, Partly hidden, resilient, Set in their ways.
A.4.3
Steven Groak (1992), The idea of building: thought and action in the design and
production of buildings
The information for Goarks theries where primarily gathered from the book review by (Barry Russell
1992), this was also contributed to through other sources and articles.
The complex relationship between architecture, technology, and society as well as the idea that the focus
on industrialization in modernism has posed significant challenges for architects and designers, including
the historical context of modernist architecture, with an emphasis on new materials and techniques are the
main concepts developed in this work.
Groak outlines the importance of understanding the holistic properties of buildings. He considers them as
dynamic systems within their social contexts under various aspects, including energy flows, complexity,
and uncertainty, challenging the notion that buildings are static objects. Groak also emphasizes the need
for research and development in the building industry, highlighting its adaptability and innovation despite
limited research efforts. The book further explores the role of skills and competence in the building in-
dustry, acknowledging the culture of change and the need for continuous adaptation. It also addresses the
decline of technical precedent and the challenges posed by evolving technologies. Groak discusses various
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LII of CXXXVIII
LII
==PAGE:188==
architectural concepts, including structure, form, and representation. He examines the impact of technol-
ogy on architectural design and the need for a new approach to technology in building. Overall, Groak’s
book offers a holistic perspective on the complexities of architecture, technology, and society. It encour-
ages readers to consider the multifaceted nature of building design and the evolving role of technology in
shaping the built environment.
A.4.4
Frank Duffy (1992), The Changing Workplace
Duffys work was primarily assessed through the book review by (Gorb 1992) wich was strengthened with
secondary online searches.
Duffy’s unique background as an architect and social anthropologist developed his view of office buildings
and their relationship with users. The key takeaways from his work include:
Activity-Based Working (ABW): Duffy discusses the shift from traditional, fixed workspaces to more
flexible, activity-based approaches where employees choose environments based on the nature of their
work.
Dilution of Hierarchy: He explores how changes in workplace design can challenge traditional hierarchies
and promote more collaborative and flat organizational structures.
Technology Integration: Duffy delves into the integration of technology within the workplace, including
the need for adaptable IT infrastructure.
Human-Centered Design: He emphasizes the importance of designing workplaces that prioritize the well-
being and productivity of employees.
Workspace Typologies: He introduces the concept of different workspace typologies to cater to various
work activities and employee preferences.
Organizational Change: Duffy acknowledges that changes in workplace design often require correspond-
ing changes in organizational culture and practices.
A.4.5
Schmidt and Austin (2016), Adaptable Architecture: Theory and practice
Many concepts were developed from this work. Focus on the question what is a building? vans, mobile
homes and boats are movable structures, are they buildings? How is the new wave of van life and movable
structures incorporated into the concept of flexible adaptation in buildings? Can the need for flexible
adaptability can be met with an open-sourced app for building management, building controls (heat, light,
ventilation, structure) by the user through a smartphone, planned adaptability (daily/yearly) review by FM
or clients/users (similar to a yearly building assessment strategy). other concepts include how mixed-use
buildings can maximise the reward for the investment in a structure and its design, Value judgment as a
key part of adaptable flexibility is paramount to the tool developed in this thesis, substantiated by (Robert
Schmidt 2016)
Further results from this literature include a development of the segregation between first use and Second
life and the categorization of the design attributes of:
• Adjustable – change of task/user
• Versatile – change of space
• Refitable – change of performance
• Convertible – change of use
• Scalable – change of size
• Movable – change of location

a further 60 building characteristics and 135 design tactics which will not be further outlined here.
It develops the concept of a building as a living thing, not a static and completable entity.
”A Manifesto for Adaptability Proposition 1 – We need a broadened scope for adaptability Proposition 2
– Adaptability is context specific Proposition 3 – Adaptability is supported by simplicity and familiarity

The concept that space (building) and event (use) have become synonymous and that we should see build-
ings not as finished, stationary works but as imperfect objects whose forms are in flux, evolving to fit
functional, technological and aesthetic transformations in society was developed along with the awareness

The concept of the inclusion of a flexibility analysis as part of an integrated Project Delivery, the theory
that a building’s quality should increases with age, the need for the development of further knowledge and
research in the area of movable buildings, that flexablity should be considered alongside other design out-
comes, rather than in isolation and that flexibility is a value judgment and can be measured in a building’s

A.4.6
Ballinger - ”the big 5”
The results of a study of Ballinger’s ”The Big Five” (Ballinger 2019a) (Ballinger 2019b) highlighted the
importance of:
• Floor to floor hights
• Column spacing
• Fixed vertical elements
• Strategic zones of flexibility
• Resiliency
The concept developed in its work of note include: State-of-the-future technology and the importance of
”integrating digitalization while keeping human factor”. That ”The only constant in healthcare is change”
and the ”Need to adapt to changing technology” is utmost in Hospital design. That a flexible design has
a ”Tipping point and not meeting all requirements can cause issues”. That ”Flow influences the effects of
big five”. That ”What we know today is not what will define buildings in the future” and finally that ”The
future care models of hospitals is the most important factor”.
A.4.7
AdaptSTAR model: A climate-friendly strategy to promote built environment
sustainability
From this paper (Conejos, Craig Langston, and J. Smith 2013) the concept development of including the
different weighting of indicators of different categories, (e.g. the social metric being more important for
government projects, structural metrics having a heavier weight for commercial buildings etc.) was gained.
As was the concept of including the option of evaluating by only a specific selected criterion (eg Structure,
energy etc.) as part of the tool. The additional model criteria of Fire, disability, and Acoustics were gained.
Finally, the reduction of case study analysts to ”Loose Fit (Functional)” aspects only is considered after
this review.
It also outlines that the concept of an efficiency frontier or Pareto front in use within the field of flexibil-
ity, though not assessed in this review, the paper discusses the theory in (Langston 2008) of a ”useful to
physical” obsolescence efficiency frontier is of note.
It develops the concept of weighting and assesses it according to
• physical
• economic
• functional
• technological
• social
• legal
• political categories
The thesis encompassed several critical concepts, such as physical obsolescence within the framework
of efficiency frontiers modelling. Of significant importance was the consideration of spatial adaptability,
enabling spaces to evolve in response to changing requirements. This adaptability includes the incorpora-
tion of plug-and-play elements, disassembly options for future reuse and recycling (such as demountable
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LV of CXXXVIII
LV
==PAGE:191==
systems and modular design), spatial flow and mobility, as well as the promotion of open, continuous, and
flexible floor plans with convertibility, divisibility, elasticity, and multi-functionality.
Additionally, the thesis touched on various architectural features and design principles, such as atria and
open areas, interior gardens, structural grids defining ideal span limits and facilitating fully interchangeable
spaces and elements, and the integration of service ducts, corridors, vertical circulation, and raised floors
to enhance service accessibility. These considerations collectively contribute to the creation of flexablity,
sustainable, and efficient built environments.
A.4.8
Stakeholder perspectives on developing more adaptable buildings
This Thesis yielded several significant concepts and insights. Firstly, the incorporation of cost-benefit
analysis into the design tool emerged as a central element of consideration. Additionally, the concept of
the discount factor took center stage, emphasizing the need to distinguish between elements useful in the
present and those primarily valuable in the future. The capacity of buildings to adapt to changing demands,
sometimes in ways not initially intended, was a recurring theme. It was noted that stakeholders like soci-
ety, end-users, future owners, and facility managers often have limited influence in the building design and
lifecycle. Balancing a building’s second life with its initial purpose and cost implications was discussed,
along with strategies to mitigate compromises. The conversation underscored the importance of includ-
ing diverse stakeholder perspectives, as research on flexibility in building design tends to overrepresent
architects’ views. Decision-making challenges were addressed, with an acknowledgement that a lack of
data often leads to decisions based on guesswork or past experience. The role of the discount factor was
further emphasized in assessing the costs and benefits of adaptability. The unintended impediments that
adaptable design can pose to development were highlighted. Moreover, the discussion touched on the role
of uncertainty in flexible design and the fragmentation of stakeholders’ goals and influence. The challenge
of addressing short-term thinking (myopia) was also considered. Ultimately, the need to break the current
vicious cycle in flexible design processes and foster a virtuous cycle promoting better decision-making
and more adaptive buildings was a key takeaway. (Pinder, Schmidt, and Saker 2013)
A.4.9
Transformation as a design capacity (Langballe 2021)
In this thesis, a primary takeaway is the defining of flexibility criteria as outlined in the theoretical concepts
chapter4.2. The thesis considers various building life cycle stages, encompassing production, construction,
use, and end-of-life phases. It emphasizes the critical role of the design phase in laying the foundation for
future successful renovations.
The assessment of flexibility in buildings presented draws inspiration from the principles of DGNB (Nach-
haltiges Bauen 2007), which revolves around six fundamental topics: Environmental quality, Economical
quality, Sociocultural and Functional quality, Site quality, Technical quality, and Process quality. Specif-
ically, the Economical quality category incorporates criteria related to Economic Development, with a
focus on Flexibility and adaptability. These criteria are meticulously assessed through indicators, with
weighted scores varying based on building type and significance. The building typologies encompass Of-
fice, Education, Residential, Hotel, Consumer market, Shopping center, and Department stores, and the
scoring considers pilot projects. The maximum score for Flexibility and adaptability is 110 points, with
an additional 10-point bonus.
The thesis also highlights the mathematical foundation for weighting, scoring, and grading criteria, much of
which has been adapted from existing work. Additionally, it stresses the importance of a flexibility score’s
comparability, suggesting that it should be capable of being measured against other quantifications, such as
cost and the environmental impact of transformable versus non-transformable building designs. Moreover,
it underscores the value of including an LCA report, cost estimation, and LCC (Life Cycle Cost) report as
integral components of a flexibility assessment. Finally, the report explores the utility of employing AHP
in flexibility assessments, contributing to a comprehensive and informed evaluation.

A.5.4
Interview notes and resulting insight
Anders Kaan - BIM specialist
In the interview with Anders Kaan, several key topics were explored. One notable aspect highlighted was
the principle of mirroring Bedwards on levels two and three in various iterations. The discussion also
touched on the importance of investigating the hospital’s fire design and the implications of fire cells on
interior wall mobility.
Another theory discussed revolved around the shared functions of different services by various disciplines
within the hospital, emphasizing that these functions were not contingent on room positions. The interview
further delved into the significance of ventilation and indoor climate as fundamental aspects of space
adaptability. It was underscored that while technical installations were relatively inflexible due to their
specific design for the current layout, the structural support of prefabricated concrete columns as well as
the lightweight, timber, top levels of structure, contributed to the overall flexibility of the interior layout.
With minimal internal structural walls and a focus on the cores and stairs for support, the hospital achieved
spacious, unobstructed layouts.
The conversation expanded to consider regulations and standards for indoor environments, particularly
in the context of changing space use and occupancy. The development of an adaptability tool, with a
continued emphasis on indoor climate factors like ventilation, temperature, and airflow, was also addressed.
Questions arose regarding the requirements and costs associated with space redesign, with a notable point
that there was no logical extension possibility for increasing capacity.
The interview moved on to discuss the digital design process and the potential benefits of utilizing 3D laser
scans (Imerso) and BIM technology to gain insights ”inside walls” post-construction. This technology was
seen as a valuable asset for the future of facility management, as was digital twins and their applications
in operations and maintenance.
The primary function of construction, catering to current users while acknowledging the need for users
to adapt to the building, was highlighted. The interview also underlined the importance of evaluating the
building’s performance after its initial use.
Lastly, the conversation touched upon the necessity for flexible pandemic spaces and the integration of
robotic services and AI into hospital services, recognizing the evolving landscape of healthcare infrastruc-
ture and technology.
Mette stockholm - Lead designer
In the interview with Mette Stockholm, several important concepts and considerations were discussed.
Firstly, the idea of making something that’s worth keeping was discussed initially, leading on to the concept
that the principal areas of the building with shorter lifespans could just be replaced .
The impact of the structure on the community and surroundings was highlighted as was the need for robust
and effective initial designs, a far better solution to be finished the first time than to add satellites as
operations are more expensive with satellites.
Mette outlines that the efficiency of staff and the degree of help they can provide are impacted by the
layout. and the structure and services are linked. Staff should be focused and present, not spend their time
walking long distances or on inefficient workflow, logistics, documentation etc, they need time for their
tasks. This generated the design feature of decentralized offices as key to the success of the hospital, which
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LVIII of CXXXVIII
LVIII
==PAGE:194==
is a standard concept for single-room layouts across all hospitals and is a universal principle in hospital
design. She outlined another global parameter of Unit sizes, with Efficiency peaking at 20-30 beds per
unit, or a more likely implemented range of 24-28 beds.
The concept that an expansion strategy needs to be part of the structure was highlighted and allowed for
technical areas needing the most development the fastest, such as labs and surgical units. She outlined the
design requirements for critical areas to be expanded by 50% and all other areas to be expanded by 20%
and that functions that expand on behalf of each other are critical, such as expanding bed-wards by using
office space.
The concept of expansion by ”Cutting the clover” for bed wards, but not for critical functions, as they
would enlarge out into the site, would not affect the expression of the site or infrastructure as the building
is prepared to enlarge out under the cantilever.
She explains that developed installation plans have been made and that extra loads were calculated for
load-bearing walls in the design.
She highlights that a greenfield hospital is a massive opportunity for development which leads to a long
design phase, meaning things change during the design. Though the changes were not as expected and
the demand for beds where going down more than predicted. Outpatient numbers have flatted out, as the
current hospitals were not receiving as many out patience as prognoses. This is contributed to the effect
of society, healthy lifestyle etc. and virtual treatment.
Though the new hospital design was not developed by looking backwards at what was there. The needed
capacity is instead calculated on activity, number of citizens and number of visits.
How doctors are employed is different across the world and the current solution is designed for Danish sys-
tems and operations. The hospital is one unit, one company, though each unit can be like a small company
itself with up to 400 employees per hospital. The developed systems mean that no fixed borders are present
within the hospital and rational sharing of resources is implemented, though some technology/speciality
are fixed.
Mette highlights that the only space change in current hospitals, from past analyses, is the office space
and that costly flexible design that causes other problems, such as movable walls that affect acoustics and
thermal properties, should be avoided.
She discusses current design solutions such as ambulance pods that could go to acute patients and decrease
the intake in the emergency department.
She highlights the robust infrastructure, such as standard sizing and standard units such as rooms, where
the dimensions for several rooms are the same. This standardization and robust infrastructure is a key
factor of the adaptability of the structure.
Though she points out that standardization can be misused, diminishing other areas of the structure’s de-
sign, which is why the NHN hospital project is designed with a ”twist”, it is not just a lot of containers on
top of each other.
She sees the primary characteristics of a flexible building as sustainability, beauty, efficiency and long-
lasting design.
A guiding philosophy for the project is that people get better sooner with beautiful buildings. People sleep
better if they are in a nice environment, they listen better and follow instructions better if they are in a
single room.
On the topic of flexibility, the main idea to emerge was that designers with talent are needed. A tool that
looks at trouble spots could be useful for engineering but it would be hard to implement in design/architec-
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LIX of CXXXVIII
LIX
==PAGE:195==
ture as it is already implemented in the process of design and architecture, if it is a principle of the design
brief.
The design process most likely to succeed in the future is to have a robust layout and then later put flexible
solutions into it, then consider the layout again. The layout would solve the issues found from the tool.
One should go back to the layout and solve issues from the tool after analysis. The concepts quickly be-
come conceptual and standard typologies for different layouts could be applied based on these understood
concepts. Mette highlighted that one cannot put a flexibility tool on an initial layout as it would not be
developed enough to support analysis. She saw limitations in the tool outcome as it would depend on who
you ask. It is hard to make a tool for everyone. She passed that cost should be seen as a main parameter
and focus as flexibility is seen as an add-on that costs money. Flexibility is seen as costing money and was
overruled in the NHN super hospital but extra capacity would have received more attention if there was a
bigger budget.
Mette and the design team used a spaghetti diagram to check to see if they meant what they said for the
design, if no links the design assets were seen as not important or needing more attention. It was a pressure
test (similar to the flexibility analysis tool developed in this report).
The NHN super hospital was not a linear project. Total connection was maintained with the hospital staff
and operators as the hospital needs the information from the construction for operation.
Finally, Mette went on to say the concept of flexibility had further difficulties as it is hard to measure
something based on intuition.
In summary, the interview covered a wide range of topics related to hospital design and flexibility, from
layout and efficiency to expansion strategies and the role of digital technology. The primary goal of creating
beautiful and efficient buildings that support patient well-being and staff productivity was a recurring theme
throughout the discussion.
Jan Eric Thompson - Architect
The discussion with Jan began with his first exposure to flexibility 30 years ago with the concept of resi-
dential flexibility as a form of streamlined production, though he then wondered ”Is it worth it?”. These
initial concepts revolved around features like hidden heating elements and smaller zoning for adaptable
heating, though he points out that it is hard to design heating for changing space. Toilets and kitchens, in
his view, are the hardest adaptable spaces as ”water runs downwards” and shafts changing spaces require
a high level of effort. One must design the core for services and it is hard to change things later.
People’s interactions with structures was the next topic of conversation with his view that after 50 years
people don’t change things, they would rather tear them down. The person using the structure will always
have another preference you didn’t design for (a 53rd requirement of 52 solutions) and the structure will
need to be torn down anyway. Often people do not live in structures long enough to implement flexibility.
Jan also highlighted that revised fire strategies for a new layout would be essential and possibly difficult.
”People always want something a little bit different than the flexibility you thought about” - Jan Eric
Thompson (Colclough 2023)(Episode 3)
For Jan, room height is the primary element for flexibility in a structure. He possesses that room height
is key for indoor climate as well. It can allow for denser population spacing and remove the need for
mechanical ventilation, with excess temperature and humidity that can be removed into the dead space.
He is a believer that passive solutions are the answer and that people can wear sweaters if they are cold. He
believes that people should change their habits based on their environment. This would help in optimizing
energy costs and energy usage. Optimizing with passive solutions is easier according to Jan. People will
accept things more if they perceive themselves as having less control, the illusion of fine control gives rise
to less contentment.
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LX of CXXXVIII
LX
==PAGE:196==
He believes it is unlikely to adapt offices to Bedwards as there would be no space for the nurses to work
from, removing the few relaxing zones available to them. Bringing something from the lower floor to
the higher floor or the other way around would be a better solution for him. Maybe moving services to
different levels.
He sees no flexibility in the spaces of the hospital ”You could move departments but where they are moved
to the MEP won’t fit as departments have different demands”.
From evidence from the old hospitals, it seems that rooms become more and more dedicated, meaning
equipment can be shared but spaces cannot be adapted so the systems, not the space, must be flexible. He
poses that flexable spaces wouldn’t be good for any need as an ”average” does not exist in reality.
Jan mentions the importance of material and that modern materials tend to be light and easier to produce
and construct and as such less robust, less flexible and reduced strength.
He also talks about the duality of healing architecture and that it reduces the time people stay in the hospital.
He asks ”Does it make sense?”. His view is that it is better to Optimise the project. If people spend 1-2
nights in a hospital then two kinds of Bedwards are needed, split use by design.
He highlights that the social areas are removed from the design of the new hospital and that the staff’s
functions are affected by their ability to rest. The work environment affects the building’s use outcome.
For Jan the ultimate flexible design feature is an Unfinished design ”Don’t plan for flexibility just give
the space for it”. In the hospital design, the change of an area from one class to another is not possible as
there is no space for bigger equipment. Fire sections are the next most important. Fire sections in the wall
are easy to work around and windows and doors need to be fireproof, altering these incurs high costs and
re-design.
To Jan the best solution for today is easiest to construct though knowing we will need to change it 5 times
in the life of the building could save money in the long run if we plan for the changes. ”you can’t eat what
will be on the table tomorrow”
Karen Vase Langballe - specialist in Transformation capacity
Karen is a Civil Engineer in the area of sustainability and energy working for the Artila group. She com-
pleted her Thesis on developing a transformation capacity tool for rating flexibility in buildings with the
3XN architectural firm and built on the principle of connecting civil Engineering and Architecture.
In the interview she reflects on flexibility and transformation potential and about the amount of building
materials it could save, linking it to LCA and DGNB. She discusses the effect of DGNB on the life of
a building. Most of the theory for her report comes from Delf University as to progressive for flexible
buildings and transformation potential. She highlights that there is a missing LCA input into current trans-
formation potential (Flexibility analyses) tools and that a combination of LCA and flexibility analyses
would add worth to any tool. For her, the idea of a tool talks of capacity and certain parameters and an
LCA of material saved with flexible buildings could show the cost savings of flexible solutions. From her
research of the ”Built Institute”, she saw that a lot of case studies are needed to increase reliability, but in
general, there is not a lot of research –need to build it one step at a time.
She sees her thesis as a beginning, and that the process of numerically analysing flexibility potential should
be taken one step at a time so it can be built on and adjusted. She talked of how the tool she developed
needs to be tested and adjusted for commercial use.
In her view, People can’t see the risk involved in design decisions and so they don’t plan for a flexible
building or adapt old buildings. For her economic factors are a big influence and 10 years tends to be the
limit of consideration in construction.
She viewed the NHN hospital as a suitable case for a flexibility analysis tool as bigger buildings are ex-
pected to last for a longer time. In discussing her competition entry for adaptable school structure she men-
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LXI of CXXXVIII
LXI
==PAGE:197==
tioned that their concept was the development of a medium-stage treatment area for overflow/increased
capacity for covid conditions. She and her team designed a mixed-use building, seeing duel function
buildings, such as a school and a post sorting office as an ideal flexible solution.
We discussed the CIRCUIT initiative that the project was developed for and how it promotes circular
economy, flexibility and sustainability, though it was agreed that Work in both the field of flexibility and
circuit are not connected and this is an area in need of development for both the concept and the initiative.
On the topic of design characteristics, or indicators, Karen mentions that it is difficult to decide on how to
measure indicators and her solution was to use DGNB principles, but a new way to measure indicators is
needed as DGNB is not always optimal. A flexibility analysis tool, she believes, can be much smarter with
the right measurements. For her the popularity of indicators is the logical way to rank them and that more
case studies could improve the weighting of these indicators/design characteristics. These weights could
be adjusted over time, but to keep in mind that weighting is just a starting point in any flexible analysis
tool. Her final takeaway was that it is important that the typologies of different buildings are taken into
account.
Sabeena Ahmed - Team lead of envelope package NHN
Sabeena has worked on the project since her position as an intern for her bachelor’s 3 years ago. She
has studied Architectural technologies, Construction management and Informatics management and is the
team lead of the envelope package for the NHN super hospital project.
She sees flexibility and digitization going hand in hand and points out that with digital solutions you can
see these effects before the building is finished. Sustainability and digitisation, to her, are one and the
ability for data to show what we can do better and what we don’t want to do is invaluable.
She explains how facility management is involved in the construction and design process and has been
included since the early stages of the NHN super hospital project. She describes how part handovers
to facility management are undertaken utilising Dalux and building cards for the building’s operations.
She describes how the facility management will utilize Dalux and how the software is used for handover
packages, which is as the facility managers want It. Maintenance of the structure and services is considered
with facility management.
She highlights that the Building phase is a short period of time in regard to operations and maintenance of
the structure and the important thing to buildings and users in the long run is ease of maintenance. This
integrated design process is for the NHN super hospital project.
For Sabeena a building is made flexible by Good materials (though an initial expense) and designed for
changing purposes, as recycling material can only go so far and as we don’t know what’s behind the walls
of finished structures.
She sees little flexibility for use at the NHN super hospital as it will always be a hospital, though possibly
the bed wards could have flexibility. She poses the question ”How relevant is it for the next 50 years?”
and suggests that in the future it may be easier to change or adapt the structure ”You never know where
we will be when we have to renovate”.
She talks of how the layout of the NHN project is built from lessons learned from the downside of the old
hospitals ”The old hospitals were the right way at the time, now we need a new solution to make current
use easier”.
Sabeena outlines how the new hospital represents a large change of system and will change people’s ev-
eryday life and that the workers need to prepare for the new systems.
She outlines how the hospital will engage with the community and society and serves a second purpose,
with the Art and aesthetics of the building being a tourist attraction, as large parts of the hospital will be
publicly accessible, building community and society involvement. She outlined that the larger community
Flexible Design Analysis
Page LXII of CXXXVIII
LXII
==PAGE:198==
is also involved as the head of construction meets with the comune and that the other developments in the
area are linked to a common development vision.
Her last point was for the development of the AEC industry ”Always be open to new trends and commu-
nicate”
Ole Kongsbak - Head of Construction NHN
The discussion with Ole started before recording was possible and had to be cut short. The discussion starts
by talking about the concept of split-level flexibility, with the flexibility of the modular timber structures
on levels 2 and 3 being entirely different form those of the base levels of the basement, level 0 and level
1. For him the top floors have high levels of flexibility and the base levels have low levels of flexibility.
He outlined the Potential for mixes of functionalities, be it education, for conferences or as residential units
and poses that the generous layout and aesthetics suit many uses, with the scale of a structure affecting its
use.
He outlines that the goal is to design a specific hospital and broader adaptability was a secondary reflection
and not high on the priority list for the management team. He goes on to mention that the design brief covers
adaptability for use as a hospital but not the structure’s secondary life.
He highlights that the Old hospital opened in 1942 and is now 85 years old, showing the long life achievable
by publicly owned speciality facilities.
He emphasises that the materials used are not cheap and that modern materials have better properties for
many things and that many of the components of the structure are modular components built off-site.
He explains how the design brief requires that the structure should be expanded by 20% or so and that
the courtyard is available for expansion for bed-wards while lower-level expansion is possible under the
cantilever. Ole then provides the Expansion potential dossier in the design manual on Dalux.
The current project stems form the project competition in 2013 and highlights the buildings long develop-
ment phase.
We end by reaffirming that the management team is concentrated on the current project and not what there
will be in the future as the main purpose is to build a hospital but that expansion plans do exist to cut down
and extend out at the corners and extents of the clover.

Flexible adaptability in building design
NHN ﬂexibility in design survey 
Dette spørgeskema er en del af forskningen til et kandidatafhandlingsprojekt om
ﬂeksibilitet i bygningsdesign.
For denne undersøgelse er tilpasningsevne evnen til at foretage ændringer eller
justeringer for at passe til et nyt miljø, der kan forudsiges, ﬂeksibilitet er evnen
til at tilpasse sig for at imødekomme ukendte behov i fremtiden.
-----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------
This questionnaire is part of the research for a masters thesis project on ﬂexibility
in building design. 
This study deﬁnes ﬂexibility is a building's ability to support change, adaptability
is a building's ability to be changed. 
For this study adaptability is the ability to make modiﬁcations or adjustments to
suit a new environment that can be forecast, ﬂexibility is the ability to adjust to
accommodate unknown needs in the future.  
Often the two are used interchangeably. 
To add complexity ﬂexibility can be deﬁned as the use cases in the long term (use
speciﬁc) which includes adaptability, the use cases in the short term (speciﬁed),
with respect to the design of buildings for future use.

A.6.5
Survey Results
The results are presented for each question.
Question 1. The survey was completed by Stakeholders from various backgrounds, including project
management, and engineering.
Question 2. The predominant age range was 25-34 (83%)
Question 3. The respondents’ gender identity was evenly distributed with 50% male and 50% Female, 0%
other.
Question 4. The respondents were a mix of Danish, Hungarian and Irish.
Question 5. The weighted average given to the importance of the following design characteristics in rela-
tion to building design were:
Question 6. The weighted average is given to the developed flexibility characteristics where
Question 7. 66.67% of respondents saw adaptability of first use as more important for a building than
flexibility for its second life.
Question 8. 66.67% of respondents thought that buildings are used as intended.
Question 9. 100% of respondents saw renovation as a better long-term solution than demolition.
Question 10. 66.67% of respondents thought that the flexibility in structures was a responsibility of society
Flexible Design Analysis
Page CXVII of CXXXVIII
CXVII
==PAGE:253==
rather than a personal responsibility.
Question 11. The respondents saw themselves (as an average) as having 60% engagement with the main-
tenance of the structures they interact with.
Question 12. Short-term light structures (as opposed to long-term heavy structures) were seen by the
majority (33.33%) as being more flexible, with additional comments that aesthetics and durability of the
used materials affected the adaptability and that Long-term light structures would be best.
Question 13. When asked which structural type was better - short-term light structures or Long term heavy
structures, The majority of respondents (50.00%) saw Long-term, heavy structures as preferable, with the
additional comment that ”If there is enough resources to keep light structure properties in good condition
they should be fine too”
Question 14. Traditional and modern buildings were seen as being equally flexible by respondents.
Question 15. 100% of the respondents claimed to know how to operate the buildings/spaces they inhabit.
Question 16. The respondents (on average) rated their daily spaces/environments as 48% flexible.
Question 17. 66.67% of respondents felt they could change their daily environment.
Question 18. 66.67% of respondents had adapted space or structure.
Question 19.
When asked what the first thing the respondents did when they moved into a new space they said:
Find out what is the best layout for me.
Figure out layout to maximise space, check ventilation and insulation
make it personal, colourful
Refurnish and redecorate
Deep clean the space
Light and privacy
Question 20.
When asked ”What makes you leave/get discontented with/from a space/structure,” The respondents said:
Not enough natural light / too much maintenance cost or effort / the ugliness of the building
Not owning it and things being fixed outside of my control (having to wait to fix things)
when its ugly, too expensive, non-sustainable
Lack of light
Better layout
Open office
Question 21.
When asked ”What makes you connect with/use a space/structure?” the respondents said:
Aesthetics! Nice materials are used, especially natural materials which can age.
Light, practicality of the layout and community in the area
memories
Flexible Design Analysis
Page CXVIII of CXXXVIII
CXVIII
==PAGE:254==
Light, warmth, spaciousness.
The layout of the space
Tidy, clean, light, privacy, organized.
These are the results of the survey that were utilised in this report.
